THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: THIRD PERIOD)
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 123 of 1981)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Apple and Pear Export Charge Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 147 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Export Ch~rge Act 1976.
Apple and Pear Levy Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 1460f 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Levy Act 1976.
Appropriation Act (No.1) 1981-82 (Act No. 1640f 1981)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1982.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1981-82 (Act No. 165 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year endingon 30June 1981.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 145 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 124 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 126 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 125 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 130 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (lnsurance Bus;ness) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Life Insurance Business) Act 1981 (Act No. 128 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of certain life insurance business having a connection with the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981 (Act No. 129 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of the registration of certain vehicles in the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian CapitalTerritory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 127 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Australian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 150 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 50 of the Austrillian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Act 1977.
Australian National University Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 1660f 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Natiollal University Act 1946.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1981 (Act No. 168 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964, and for related purposes.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 177 of 1981)An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905, and for related purposes.
Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 151 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Act 1905 in relation to the exportation of food.
Commonwealth Grants Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 173 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Grants Commission Act 1973.
Companies and Securities Legislation (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 153 of 1981)- An Act to amend laws relating to companies and securities and for related purposes.
Continental Shelf (Living Natural Resources) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 182 of 1981)An Act to amend the Continental Shelf(Living Natural Resources) Act 1968 to facilitate the collection of levy imposed by the Foreign Fishing Boats Levy Act 1981 and of amounts payable under certain agreements, and for certain other purposes.
Crimes (Currency) Aat 1981 (Act No. 1220f 1981)An Act to make provision with respect to offences connected with the counterfeiting of money or certain kinds of securities and other activities injurious to Australian currency, and for related purposes.
Customs (Unlawful Exportation of Food) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 152 of 1981)- An Act ~to amend the Cllstoms Act 1901 in relation to the exportation of food.

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2173

Tuesday, 20 October 1981
Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 1.45 p.m., and read prayers.
PRIVILEGE
Dr THEOPHANOUS (Burke)-Mr Speaker, seek your indulgence to raise a matter of privilege.
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable gentleman may proceed.
Dr THEOPHANOUS-I refer you to May's Parliamentary Practice, which, at page 151, states:
Conduct not amounting to a direct attempt to infl uence a Member in the discharge of his duties, but having a tendency to impair his independence in the future performance of his duty, will also be treated as a breach of privilege.
The matter I want to raise is, I believe, very serious and relates to the possible obstruction of officials of this Parliament in the performance of their duties for members of parliament. You are aware that many members of parliament often request specific items of research from the Legislative Research Service which are confidential and which are marked 'Not for Attribution'.
Mr SPEAKER-I interrupt the honourable gentleman. Did I understand him to base the privilege claim on the obstruction of officials in the conduct of their business?
Dr THEOPHANOUS-No, Mr Speaker. I refer to page 151 of May's Parliamentary Practice which refers to:
. . . having a tendency to impair his independence in the future performance of his duty . . .
Mr SPEAKER-That is, of the member? Dr THEOPHANOUS-Right. May I proceed?
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable gentleman should proceed.
Dr THEOPHANOUS-I was saying that there are items from the Parliamentary Library which are marked: 'Not for Attribution'. They are prepared by the Legislative Research Service on specific request from a member of Parliament. Papers produced in this fashion are said to be entirely confidential. The only knowledge of the existence of these papers is supposed to be held by the researcher himself or herself and the head of the section. No one else is supposed to have knowledge of what was written, for which member of Parliament it was written and by whom it was written.
Earlier this year, I produced a confidential paper for the Parliamentary Labor Caucus on the

ownership of resources enterprises. Part of this paper was based on a specific project which I worked on jointly with a member of the economic section of the Legislative Research Service. As a result, he produced a paper entitled 'Compare the Revenue Effects of a Resources Rent Tax with 50 per cent Government Equity'. As far as I am aware, no one, except my own staff, knew that the section concerning resources rent taxes was written in co-operation with the Parliamentary Library. Indeed, the issue is so specific and controversial that the natural assumption would be that it was written by my research staff. Yet last week a member of the staff of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) rang Mr Richard Webb, head of the economic section of the Research Service, and requested 'a copy of the paper written for Dr Theophanous on the merits of resources rent taxes'.
It is the practice of the Parliamentary Library not to issue such confidential papers unless the member for whom they were originally written grants his or her permission. Mr Webb thus asked the researcher to seek my permission for the paper to be released to the Prime Minister's office. When this occurred, the response of the researcher and myself was the same: How did the Prime Minister's office know that the Parliamentary Library had written a paper for me on this specific topic? I asked the researcher to ask Mr
Webb about the matter. Mr Webb later rang the Prime Minister's office to confirm that this was indeed what it was requesting. This incident raised grave doubts in the minds -of the research staff in that section as to the confidentiality of their work. I remind the House that each researcher fills in a specific form called a Request Co-ordination Unit form for every job done for a particular member. The form states, amongst other things, the topic of the research. In this case the topic was listed as 'Compare Revenue Effects of Resources Rent Taxes with Alternatives'. That is very similar to the topic requested by the Prime Minister's office. The form, by the way, also lists the member of parliament for whom the paper was written.
This breach of confidentiality was considered so serious that the director of the economics sec-
tion, Mr Webb, raised the matter at a meeting yesterday morning of all sectional directors of the Library. I am informed that no one was able to explain satisfactorily how the breach occurred. Given these events, I and other members of parliament are entitled to feel uneasy aboutthe possibility of interference in the work of the research service and a possible breach of confidentiality of members' work. It raises the suspicion of the

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2175

Sales Tax on Publications
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives of the Commonwealth of Australia in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That the Government's decision to impose a tax on books, magazines and newspapers is abhorrent to the notion of the right to information, and
That no such tax is levied on any other country with which Australia is usually compared.
Your petitions therefore humbly pray.
That the Government will withdraw its announced intention to impose a sales tax on books, magazines and newspapers.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling.
Petition received.
Postal Services
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives of the Commonwealth of Australia in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That the plan by the Australian Postal Commission to downgrade postal and mail services throughout Australia will place in jeopardy the existing services and the security of the mails by:
Downgrading a large number of official post offices Reducing the number of post offices open for business during the lunch period Reducing the number of street posting boxes Delaying telegrams by reducing the number of delivery offices Downgrading post office services by employing untrained and unqualified staff Using private contractors for mail cartage.
Your petitioners therefore pray that the Australian Government will take immediate action to prevent the Australian Postal Commission implementing any plan which will downgrade the postal and mail services of the citizens of Australia.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Duffy.

Trans Australia Airlines
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives of the Australian Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
Many Australians depend almost exclusively upon Trans-Australia Airlines for the provision of essential air services,
The Federal Liberal Party policy objective of selling Trans-Australia Airlines within 4 years is contrary to the national interest.
Sale of Trans-Australia Airlines would inevitably result in:
(i) the creation of a private monopoly of major airline services in Australia;
(ii) higher domestic air fares.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject outright any proposal to sell Trans-Australia Airlines.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Kent and Mr Morris.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray,
by Mr Anthony.

Health Care Services

To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled the petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:

That the decrease in Federal Government funding for health, transfer of Commonwealth responsibility to the States, application of the 'user pay' principle to health services and encouragement of private business and profits in the health sphere will inevitably lead to a downgrading of health services and increasing health risks among people who cannot afford health insurance or private payment of doctors and hospitals,

That the cuts in Federal Government spending resulting from the recommendations of the Cabinet Sub-Committee of Review of Government Functions, (razor gang), will lead to an 'increase in unemployment and a general deterioration in community services and living standards,

Therefore your petitioners urge that:

1. The Government continue Commonwealth Funding of health services at an improved, and certainly not a lesser, level than that operating prior to 1 September 1981.

2. That recommendations of the Cabinet Sub-Committee

of Review of Government Functions, (razor gang), be

not proceeded with.

'

And your petitioners in duty bound will ever pray.

by Mr Cadman.

Community Health Centres
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That the continued cutback of our local community health centre will lead to a decline in the health status of our community, We appreciate their varied services, which include: baby health services, counselling, chiropody, physiotherapy, community nurses, preventative services etc.
We believe that the community health program will cut rising health costs by supporting people in their own homes, Therefore, we support the basic health care and prevention carried out by our health centre.
We request that adequate funding is provided for the continuation of and expansion of these valuable services to our community,
Your Petitioners therefore, humbly pray that your Honourable House consider this petition.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Cadman.

Sales Tax Legislation
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australian respectfully showeth:
I. That the Government's Sales Tax legislation imposes an unreasonable burden on the community gener,ally, especially pensioners, families, those in receipt of smaller incomes and small businesses.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Government will withdraw or repeal its Sales Tax Legislation.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Donald Cameron.
Petition received.
Sales Tax on Publications
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
I. That the 2t per cent sales tax on books, magazines and newspapers is a tax on knowledge, a tax on information and a tax on culture.
2. That freedom can be protected in a democratic society only if its citizens have access to information and ideas through books, magazines and newspapers.
3. That it is in early life that a taste for books and the habit of reading in the home are most easily acquired. The ability of families to provide the stimulas which books offer will be limited by the higher cost imposed by the tax.
4. That the development of the Australian publishing industry is based on a vigorous consumer demand. Such a tax can inhibit the industry and create unemployment among its workers.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that you will reconsider the proposed tax.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Ian Cameron.
Petition received.
Sales Tax Legislation
The Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
I. That the Budget imposition of additional sales tax to both existing sales taxed items and to previously exempt goods:
(a) will add to the regressivity of the tax structure by imposing a similar burden on all sections of the community regardless of the capacity to pay tax;
(b) will'be highly inflationary and a disincentive to econ-
omic performance in key sectors of manufacturing in-
dustry, e.g. car manufacturing; textiles and footwear; boat-building;
(c) is yet another breach of Government promise, given the Treasurer's undertaking in the Parliament on March 12 of this year that there would be no increases in indirect taxation (without at the same time giving personal tax relief).
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House will request the Government to repeal the budget amendments to sales tax legislation and to withdraw its sales tax amendment bills which add sales tax to presently exempt goods as from January I, 1982.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Darling.

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2179

Funding of Women's Centres
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. Your petitioners most humbly pray that the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled should take action as it deems appropriate to ensure the continuing effectiveness of the women's refuge program. [n particular that:
1. Realistic funding levels be introduced to maintain existing services offered by women's refuges.
2. That the needs of child care in women's refuges be acknowledged by a specific and adequate allocation of funds for this purpose.
3. That funding be allocated for the purpose of assisting women and children to establish themselves in the community on leaving refuges.
4. That funding be allocated for refuges to address themselves to areas of special needs of migrant women in refuges.
5. That funding be made available for the establishment of new women's refuges.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrFry.
Petition received.
Aspect House for Handicapped Children
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
1. All children in Tasmania are not treated equally.
2. Persons with intellectual, physical and multiple handicaps in particular will be denied access to Aspect House, 6 Warragul Avenue, New Town in Tasmania if the house is closed.
3. Aspect House is faced with imminent closure because of lack of Government policy and on-going funding.
4. Aspect House is the only community-based facility in an ordinary house in an ordinary suburban setting which provides relief to parents and families engaged in the onerous and demanding task of rearing their handicapped children in the community.
5. That the rights of all children to live with their families should be respected.
6. That we seek Government to take immediate action to prevent the closure of this unique facility.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Hodgman.
Petition received.
Health Care Services
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That many Australians will be financially disadvantaged by health changes proposed by the Federal Government, especially with the withdrawal of Commonwealth medical benefits for the uninsured, and the introduction of a means test for free public hospital care;
That the health insurance changes promote inequity, inefficiency and lack of universal coverage;
That the public hospital system and community health programmes will be financially disadvantaged by the abdication of Commonwealth responsibility and funding.

Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will take all steps necessary to reintroduce a system of health care based on ability to pay, and access to health care based on medical need.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrHowe.
Petition received.
Tertiary Education
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament assembled. This Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
(i) the financial situation of students enrolled at Australian post-secondary institutions is already extremely difficult (as indicated in the Commonwealth Department of Education's 1979 survey of student incomes and expenditure) and any substantial increase in the financial commitments of students would gravely worsen their financial difficulties.
(ii) the imposition of post-secondary tuition fees of comparable levels to those imposed prior to 1971 would force many currently enrolled students to discontinue their studies and deter many persons willing and able at present to' undertake post-secondary study from doing so.
(iii) those students and potential students from social groups currently under-represented and disadvantaged in the post-secondary education system would be most severely affected by the re-introduction of fees for post-secondary education.
Your petitioners therefore pray that:
(1) post-secondary tuition fees not be re-introduced for any courses or for any category of students
(2) the visa charges upon private overseas students be removed immediately
(3) the Minister of Education immediately clarify the Government's attitude to the re-introduction of postsecondary tuition fees.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Kent.
Petition received.

Your petitioners therefore pray that the Industries Assistance Commission report be not given effect.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Kent.

Subsidy Payments for Nursing Home Patients
To the Right Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
I. The majority of Pensioners in Nursing Homes are currently not receiving any significant rebate after all fees and laundry expenses are paid. This situation does not allow for a basically acceptable quality of life and undermines the dignity of people who are unable to fend for themselves. We call upon the Federal Government to investigate the plight of these people and guarantee them at least the opportunity to financially support their own basic needs.
2. When residents of Nursing Homes go to stay with friends or relatives for short periods (e.g. weekends) they are compelled to pay the Commonwealth subsidy for the period of their absence. This expense is obviously not able to be met by the majority of Nursing Home residents or their friends and relatives. The result is that people who would benefit from short periods away from the Nursing Home are restricted to its confines because they are not in the position to pay for their release. This situation. causes emotional trauma on festive occasions such as Christmas and birthdays.
We feel this is a hardship that should not be borne by Nursing Home residents and call on the Federal Government to relax its policy regarding subsidy payments for Nursing Home residents on short leave.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that Government policy on subsidy payments for Nursing Home residents on short leave, be relaxed.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Les McMahon.
Petition received.
Medibank
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That many Australians will be financially disadvantaged by the health changes proposed by the Federal Government, especially the withdrawal of Commonwealth medical benefits for the uninsured, and the introduction of a means test for free public hospital care;
. That the health insurance changes promote inequity, inefficiency and lack of universal coverage;
That the public hospital system and community health programs will be financially disadvantaged by the abdication of Commonwealth responsibility and funding.

Petition received.
Rights for Disabled
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition 9f undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
I. The failure of the Federal Government to seek information from the census re disabled people-i.e. partial or total disability-to enable for adequate planning of, and for, types of services needed by disabled people to be undertaken.
2. The failure of the Federal Government to lead the way in employment of disabled people by employing a gr~ater number of such people within the Commonwealth Public Service-note the small number currently employed within the Service.
3. The wastage of the Federal Government funds perpetuating large, bureaucratic institutions and organisations where there are often few disabled people on their boards and little emphasis is placed on consumer participation in decision making.
4. The failure of the Federal Government to encourage independence of disabled people, or to develop 'normalised' support systems-attendant care.
5. The failure of the Federal Government to provide 'noninstitutionalised, specialised accommodation for those people who would not be able to benefit from, or cope with attendant care.
6. Failure by the Federal Government to alter, or create a 'disability' allowance means test free for all disabled peoplethis would assist with necessary equiprnent and mobility costs.
7. Failure to provide a national compensation scheme thus saving time and enabling people to be automatically covered in the event of a disability.
8. Failure by the Federal Government to abolish tariffs on all equipment available to the disabled-e.g. Optacon.
9. Mismanagement of Federal I.Y.D.P. Funds-e.g. nonreceipt by some States of funds through unresolved negotiations.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that immediate action be taken to remedy the above stated and that the Federal Government recognise that disabled people are citizens who should have equal rights.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Mountford.
Petition received.
Interest Rates
To the Right Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Australia assembled. This humble petition of citizens of this country are concerned with increased house interest rates and that this Government has not taken action to peg increases in such rates. We ask that the Government establish a maximum fixed interest rate on a First Mortgage that would cover the entire period of time for which the loan is taken out. The rate of interest to be set at the time of signing the contract.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrScott.

Endangered Species
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled, the humble petition of we the undersigned citizens respectfully showeth that the trade in products made from animals or plants nearing extinction is not necessary to the existence of Australians or for the betterment of their society.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that prohibition of the sale of these products be effected via the implementation of a new and enforceable Endangered Species Act.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Scott.
Petition received.
Education
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned members of parents and citizens associations respectfully showeth:
That retention of the Schools Commission and the Curriculum Development Centre in their present forms is essential for the balanced and progressive allocation of educational resources and facilities in the Australian community.
That serious concern has been generated by the decision within the School Commission guidelines, to cut funds to the general recurrent and capital programs by reallocation to transition, the decision not to implement the school improvement program; and the divisiveness created between government and non-government schools due to increases of $43.25 million to non-government schools while government schools funds have not increased.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray:
That the Government will reverse these decisions-will retain the Schools Commission and Curriculum Development Centre in their original forms; retain the Schools Commission proposals including recurrent and capital funding proposals; basket of services and the schools improvement program.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Donald Cameron and Mr Fife.
Petitions received.
Interest Rates
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Federal Parliament, assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned residents of Hindmarsh Division State of South Australia:
Petition that: Competition between banks and finance companies by giving ever increasing interest rates to obtain the investors' capital, is against the nation's best interest. It penetrates every form of activity by constant demands for higher wages to meet these explosive conditions.
It is an intolerable burden to the younger generation of family life, that by necessity are the recipients of these excessive interest rates that are catastrophic to house purchase and natural living. We therefore respectfully petition for an urgent annual realistic controlled reduction right 'across the board' on all existing and future interest rate agreements. With a maximum interest perceniage ceiling on investors' capital, and a scale of interest charges for loans as appropriate.

Your petitioners therefore, humbly pray that your honourable House will give effect to our submissions.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrScott,

Staging of American B52 Bombers
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned in South Australia respectfully showeth:

That the proposed use of Darwin Airport as a refuelling and Rest and Recreation base for American B52 bombers:

Jeopardises the welfare of the citizens of Darwin and of

all Australians, in that it establishes a stronger potential of

Darwin being a nuclear target in any conflict between the

USA and another power.

.

Impedes the sovereignty of the Australian nation in that Australian citizens would have neither control nor knowledge of whether B52s:

(a) Carried nuclear weapons

(b) Were engaged in bombing missions against another State.

Violates the Australian people's independence and links this nation irreconcilably with United States foreign poli-
cies thereby destroying the potential for independent Australian defence policy.

Violates democratic procedures in committing the Aus-
tralian nation to participation in future wars without due
consultation and informed assessment by Australian citizens and further increases the militarisation of the Indian Ocean thereby increasing the likelihood of super power conflict in this region.

Jeopardises the welfare of the citizens of Darwin, in that
noise pollution and the threat of accidental radioactive fallout will increase substantially to the residents.

Negates the potential of the Australian nation to
establish itself as a nation of peace in the Pacific region and violates Australia's commitment to the Nuclear NonProliferation Treaty.

Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Members of
the House of Representatives will ensure that the Prime Minister withdraw the offer of Darwin, or any other location in Australia, as a base or depot for American B52 bombers. And
that furthermore will ensure that any request of such usage by the US Government be rejected.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

by Mr Young.

Petition received.

QUESTIONS WITHOUT NOTICE
SINAI PEACEKEEPING FORCE
Mr LIONEL BOWEN-I refer the Prime Minister to the fact that the Government is still continuing to delay an announcement as to whether the Government proposes to commit Australian Defence Force personnel to the Middle East. As this is a matter of vital importance to the nation and it should have the fullest consideration of the Parliament, will the Prime Minister give an assurance that any decision relating to the commitment of Australian Defence Force personnel to the Middle East will not be announced while the Parliament is not in session?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-There are one or two things I can say about this matter. The assassination of President Sadat inevitably has had an effect on the peace process in the Middle East. The progress of the peace process, however, does

Questions Without Notice

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2183

not depend on one man, no matter how great his contribution, no matter how much he has done. The process of implementing the Camp David Accords and the peace treaty between Egypt and Israel is under way and, largely because of President Sadat's contribution, now has its own momentum. Although President Sadat's death is a great loss the peace process will, I believe, not be reversed. I welcome the fact that the new President has declared his intention to maintain the direction of President Sadat's policies with respect to the Middle East. Nevertheless, the assassination of President Sadat raises a number of new issues which the Government has had to consider in its examination of the question of Australian participation in the Sinai force and the Government is in close touch with leaders of other governments.
I took the opportunity of meeting with other leaders in Melbourne to discuss the Sinai question. I expect to be in a position to make some announcement about this decision in the relatively near future, but that is not necessarily waiting on decisions of this Government but decisions of other governments. There is no procrastination on this issue by this Government. I think it is sensible to weigh all the factors and the latest developments. The other countries involved in discussions with us on this issue understand and respect our wish to consider the matter very closely and to consult widely on a decision of such significance. It is very difficult to give in categoric terms the kind of assurance that the honourable gentleman wants because the decision of other governments is obviously of vital importance to Australia. I hope that the honourable gentleman understands that. Having said that, I note very keenly what the honourable gentleman has said. Quite plainly, it is a matter which in the normal course, one way or the other, ought to be announced in this Parliament.
NEW INTERNATIONAL MOTOR RACING CIRCUIT
Mr MacKENZIE-My question, which is directed to the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, relates to the new international standard motor racing circuit to be constructed near Melbourne. Why has the Government decided to support the construction of a new circuit in Melbourne instead of in Canberra, as was originally announced by the Prime Minister last year? Why were circuits such as Mount Panorama at Bathurst not considered for upgrading to international standard? Is it a fact that the original Commonwealth grant of $2m for the new track in the Australian Capital Territory has been

increased to $3m for the track in Melbourne? As the Australian Capital Territory will still be eligible for the $2m for international sporting facilities, is it now correct to say that the Government will be spending $5m on international facilities in Melbourne and Canberra? Finally, when can the member for Calare anticipate an announcement on Commonwealth funding for other racetracks, including $3m to upgrade the Mount Panorama circuit at Bathurst?
Mr WILSON-I have been aware of the honourable member's keen interest and the interest of people of Bathurst in the development of the raceway in that city. When the Government announced in the policy statement that $25m would be made available for an international sports facilities program, that announcement was made on the basis that funds would be made available to the States and the Territories on a dollar for dollar basis. The Victorian Government, in the light of reports by the Confederation of Australian Motor Sport, has decided in respect of the funds that it would receive as its share of the $25m program to include within the spending of that share the development of a motor racing circuit in Victoria. In the light of that recommendation, the Victorians have requested that their share of the international sports facilities program be attributed, in part-to the extent of $3m-to the building of that track. If CAMS had recommended the development of a circuit for New South Wales or if the New South Wales Government independently had wished to develop a motor racing circuit in that State, that proposal could have been included within the framework of that State's share in the international sports facilities program. But, as I understand it, the New South Wales Government wishes to spend its share of the program on the developments it is proposing for Homebush Bay.
The commitment under the program for the Australian Capital Territory is for $Im. My colleague, the Minister for the Capital Territory, in the exercise of Territory or State-like functions, will provide another $Im on a matching basis for international sports facilities in the Australian Capital Territory. It is a matter for the Minister for the Capital Territory to take advice and to have consultations in the Territory, as he sees fit, to determine the sporting facilities on which that money should be spent. In answer to the. honourable member's question relating to Bathurst, it really is a matter for the New South Wales Government to determine whether it wants to put a motor raceway high enough on its priorities to share within that State's allocation under the international sports facilities program.

2184 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Questions Without Notice

TELECOM: DATA SERVICE AND TELEX EQUIPMENT
Mr LES McMAHON-I direct my question to the Minister for Communications. Is it true that the media groups, John Fairfax and Sons Ltd through the Australian Financial Review, the Sydney Morning Herald and Weekly Times, News Ltd and David Syme through the Age and the Advertiser group have indicated their intention to work with Telecom Australia in developing a national videotex system?
Mr SINCLAIR-A good many people have been interested in developing a viewdata system. There ate distinctions between videotex and viewdata. The videotex system at the moment is being operated on a trial basis by Channel 7 studios in Sydney. Discussions on this matter do not involve Telecom because Telecom is involved in the cable system. The cable system is viewdata. That is why I am not too sure whether the honourable gentleman's question relates to the development of an inscription process on television so that the hearing impaired can view it. The honourable gentleman knows that within the Budget we provided a sum of money so that it would be possible to develop a viewdata system which could be utilised throughout Australia. The hearing impaired would then be able to have on the bottom of their television screens the transcription of the spoken message heard by those with ordinary hearing. In contrast, the viewdata system is down the Prestel stream. If the honourable gentleman's question was in that direction, I am not aware of discussions between Telecom and those groups, although there were discussions between Telecom and a lot of people regarding the development of the way by which a viewdata system could be developed. I would be happy to hear from the honourable gentleman further. When he identifies just which stream he wishes to follow perhaps I could direct my answer to that.
VISIT BY SOVIET JOURNALIST
Mr DRUMMOND-I direct my question to the Minister for Foreign Affairs. I refer to an editorial in today's Age concerning the refusal by the Government to issue a visa to a Soviet journalist to visit Australia. If the Government is as concerned as it claims to be about the refusal of Indonesia to admit resident Australian correspondents to that country, why has the Government refused a visa to the deputy editor of the Soviet Government newspaper Izvestia to visit Australia?
Mr STREET-The current Government guidelines on entry from the Soviet Union do not exclude visits by representatives of the Soviet

media. In fact, there have been several occasions on which visas have been granted to Soviet journalists since the current restrictions were imposed following the Soviet invasion of Afghanistan. Applications for visas from Soviet representatives are examined on a case by case basis. The decision to refuse Mr Kondrashov's visa was made only after very careful consideration of all the factors involved. I know honourable members will understand that there are sometimes factors which cannot be made pUblic. I note that there are resident Soviet correspondents in Australia, representing Tass and Pravda.
So far as Australian journalists in Indonesia are concerned, ;lS I have said before I regret very much the decisions of the Indonesian Government not to renew the visas of Australian resident correspondents there. I have asked the Indonesian Government to reconsider these decisions because I believe that a free flow of information conveyed by the media between two countries is to the benefit of both. I emphasise that the decision on Mr Kondrashov's visa did not relate to his activities as a journalist.
TELECOM: DATA SERVICES AND TELEX EQUIPMENT
Mr SCOTT-My question is addressed to the Minister for Communications. Is it a fact that a large number of private companies, either as suppliers of terminal equipment or providers of information, have acknowledged that the initiative for the development of a videotex system should be taken by the national communications carrierthat is, Telecom Australia? Do these companies include Philips, AWA, Sony and other suppliers as well as national media groups?
Mr SINCLAIR-I think the honourable member again will find he is getting himself mixed up between videotex and viewdata. I understand videotex is the radiated form of this information.
Mr Armitage-You are trying to confuse us.
Mr SINCLAIR-I know the honourable member finds himself in that state most of the time. The viewdata system to which I believe he directed his question was being developed on the basis that most of the terminals and most of the equipment provided would come from the private sector. The fundamental planning involved a negative cash flow for at least the first two years. The involvement of others still left Telecom using resources, which could otherwise have been used for the development of its network, its cable system and the installation of telephones, that were to be used to develop the viewdata operation. The

Questions Without Notice

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2185

Government believed that in spite of the involvement of the private sector, which would have been very extensive under the Telecom-Prestel proposal, it was preferable for the development to be entirely within the private sector. It was for that reason that the Government announced that it sees advantage in the private sector developing viewdata, be it down the Prestel track, the Telidon track, the Antiope track or indeed through the use of any of the other techniques which I understand are not far from being available.
TERTIARY EDUCATION: WESTERN NEW SOUTH WALES
Mr HICKS-My question is directed to the Minister for Education and relates to the threatened closure of the W.S. and L.B. Robinson College at Broken Hill by the University of New South Wales and the Minister's visit to Broken Hill on Friday to discuss the matter with the mayor and other local representatives. Did the Minister undertake to approach the New South Wales Minister for Education to suggest a meeting between the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission and the New South Wales Higher Education Board to consider the whole question of the structure of post-secondary education in the far western region of the State? Has he made this approach and, if so, what response did he receive?
Mr FIFE-It is true that last Friday, in the company of the honourable member for Riverina, I visited Broken Hill to meet the mayor and other local dignitaries to discuss the proposed closure of the College of the University of New South Wales at Broken Hill. I emphasise to the House that, as far as I know, this is not yet a decision but' it is a recommendation that is likely to go to the Council of the University of New South Wales. During the visit to Broken Hill with the honourable member for Riverina, who is very concerned that people in his electorate and in the western districts of New South Wales will be without appropriate tertiary education faCilities, I did indicate to people I met that my prime concern as Minister for Education was to see that appropriate courses and appropriate facilities were available for students. That obviously has to be the prime consideration of an education minister and indeed of a government. How that facility and how those programs are delivered, of course, is another matter.
I suggested, as a result of my observation and the assertion that I made, that the -best-way to progress this matter was to arrange for discussions to be held between the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission and the New South Wales

Higher Education Board. The New South Wales

Minister for Education has been in touch with me

regarding this matter and his concern about the

future of tertiary education facilities out in the

western part of the State. As I indicated to the

honourable member on Friday, I have been in

touch with Mr Mulock and have suggested that

this meeting between our two advisory bodies

might take place. As at an hour ago I had not

received a response from Mr Mulock but I feel

certain that I will hear from him in the course of
this week. When I do t will advise the honourable

member for Riverina.

DIAMOND PRODUCTION
Mr KEATING-I refer the Prime Minister to his answer last week on diamond marketing policy. I ask: Does he stand by that answer? If so, does he deny statements in the Press and on radio by Sir Charles Court that he had conveyed to Sir Charles that he regarded diamond marketing as being the exclusive responsibility of the Western Australian Government? If Sir Charles's claims are true, how does the Prime Minister square this with his answer last week and his earlier pronouncements on South African policy as well as the Commonwealth's legitimate constitutional control over exports as it is practised with other significant mineral commodities?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-A sentence which I did use last week was accurate but a number of the conclusions drawn from'it-including I think, by the honourable gentleman-are drawing a very long bow indeed. The sentence which I used last week indicated that there would be no point for Australia in an arrangement that merely served to strengthen an overseas monopoly or corporation at the expense of Australia or an Australian corporation. Without using the exact words, that is the substance of what I said. I am sure that the honourable gentleman would confirm that. That is so no matter what the arrangement is and no matter what the commodity is because we are concerned with the Australian interest, the Western Australian interest and the interests of corporations which operate in Australia. Therefore whatever is done obviously needs to have these considerations in mind.
Quite plainly, the Western AustralianGovernment and the corporation involved are working to achieve resolution of a number of matters. Precisely how the diamonds might be sold has not yet, as I understand it, come on their agenda in any firm way. But when arrangements are made they need to be ones that will be in the interests of Australia, in the interests of Western Australia and in the interests of the corporation. That is not

2186 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Questions Without Notice

in conflict in any way with what I said last week. If there is any honourable gentleman who believes that it would ever be in the interests of Australia or a corporation which operates in Australia to make arrangements that would serve only to strengthen some overseas monopoly, he has a very strange view of it indeed. But it is worth noting, I think, that these matters need to be worked out.
Four years ago the export of gemstones was released from export controls and there is no proposal to re-establish export controls. But at the same time there are obviously foreign investment procedures, normal procedures and policies of this Government, that operations within Australia need to comply with. That is understood in Western Australia; it is understood by the corporation. There is a great attempt by somebody-I am not sure whether it is the hon()urable gentleman; maybe I am attributing too much to his initiative-to build a difference where I do not believe there is any.
SALES TAX
Mr BAUME-I direct the Treasurer's attention to advertisements appearing in various newspapers in my electorate alleging that taxes on magazines, newspapers, and books are practically unheard of in the free world. Is this correct? Has the Treasurer made public statements outlining the large number of nations imposing such consumer taxes on the written word? Have any metropolitan newspapers in Australia, which are campaigning against the sales tax, reported these statements? Has the Treasurer been obliged to resubmit his public statements on this matter as 'letters to the editor' in order to get them printed in metropolitan newspapers?
Mr HOWARD-My attention has been drawn to one copy of this particular advertisement. It does continue a pattern of incorrect statements, misrepresentation and downright refusal on the part of some people to acknowledge plain unassailable facts. It is not correct to say that the imposition of a sales tax on newspapers and publications is practically unheard of in the free world. In fact, every country in western Europe, with the exception of the United Kingdom and Norway, charges indirect taxes on newspapers, magazines and books at rates in some instances of up to 22 per cent, compared with the 2t per cent level imposed by the Budget.
I have s.aid on many occasions in the past, and I repeat, that of course the imposition of new taxes-the application of taxes for the first time to new areas-is never popular. I am more than happy to engage in a proper debate and exchange

on the merits of doing so, but I would have thought in an area as elementary as this that there should at least be an acknowledgment of basic facts. I reject as completely untrue and distorted much of the campaign that is being waged against the Government in relation to the imposition of tax in this particular area. In no sense, shape or form do our Budget measures or any part of them represent anything in the nature of an attack on free speech.
AUSTRALIAN DIAMOND INDUSTRY
Mr KEATING-I desire to put another question to the Prime Minister to indicate to the Parliament that the Commonwealth, particularly the Prime Minister, has not buckled to parochial coalition interests in respect of diamonds. I ask the Prime Minister whether he agrees that the foreign investment aspects to which he has just referred have already been cleared by the Government, with CRA being a naturalising company and Ashton Mining having been approved as a national mining company? Could the Prime Minister then tell the House, if he says that there is no proposal to establish export controls, what instruments of the Commonwealth he envisages being used to guarantee that the marketing of Australian diamonds proceeds in the national interest?
Mr ANTHONY-Concerning the naturalisation of the firms involved with the Argyle diamond project, I point out that authority has been given for them to become Australianised companies and therefore the majority interest, we hope, within a period of years will be Australian and will be Australian controlled. As regards the style of marketing of the diamonds that might take place, that-is still unknown as the company is carrying out an investigation as to what are the best means it might use to market its diamonds. That might take some considerable time.
If the question is whether the Commonwealth has authority to exercise controls over exports, of course it has; and there is no dispute about that. That is the authority of the Commonwealth government. Whether this Government or any other government would want to get into the area of exercising export controls on precious stones is another question completely. Four years ago we lifted controls on precious stones, covering such things as sapphires and opals, because it was shown that administratively it was an impossible operation. I certainly would not like to think that officials of my Department or other government officials were to be given the job of assessing the value of individual diamonds, a most difficult and complex question. What action might be taken cannot even be considered until the question of

Questions Without Notice

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2187

how the diamonds are to be marketed is put before the Government.

TAX AVOIDANCE
Mr SAINSBURYI refer the Treasurer to the extensive legislation of the past three years dealing with tax avoidance. I ask whether he might have any information to give to the House regarding the effectiveness so far of the new general antiavoidance measures of the income tax Act?
Mr HOWARDThe new general antiavoidance section, namely, Part IVA of the Income Tax Assessment Act, was passed in May of this year with the support, I recall and acknowledge, of the Opposition. Whilst evidence at this stage regarding its effectiveness is necessarily anecdotal, lam pleased to say to the House that a number of indications have come to me, to the Government's advisory committee on taxation,

and to the Government private members committee on taxation, to the effect that the new general anti-avoidance section is, to use the vernacular, starting to bite. There is evidence that a number of firms and organisations previously marketing paper schemes on a very large scale have either eliminated or reduced their operations. The early indications are that the legislation, which took so long in preparation and over which the Government and its advisers, both public and private, worked so hard, in fact will match our expectations. I am sure that that development, if borne out by further evidence, will have the support of both sides of the House.

BRANDY: DUTY
Mr FISHER-My question is directed to the Minister for Primary Industry. What is the

Questions Without Notice

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2189

Government doing to implement the 1979 Industries Assistance Commission recommendation to increase the duty on imported brandy from the current 8 per cent to 25 per cent? What is it doing to prevent the European Economic Community from banning four-litre wine casks after 1984? Will the Minister request the EEC to drop its present discriminatory and costly requilement of analysis on imported Australian wine?
Mr NIXON-The European Economic Community policies on wine, as do many of its other policies, very much disadvantage Australian agricultural industries. The Ministers of the Government and the Prime Minister have been at pains to point out over a long period just what those disadvantages mean. Indeed, I had the opportunity only a couple of weeks ago of meeting the new EEC Commissioner for Agriculture, Mr Dalsager, and talking with him about these policies. I did not raise the wine question at the time. But the fact is that the subsidised brandy imports from France have been on the increase in recent months. Unfortunately, the lAC's recommendations for increased protection in respect of this matter could not be implemented in 1979 because of technical difficulties associated with the renegotiation of our international commitments under the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade on brandy. But I am pleased to report that circumstances now allow action to be taken and the Government has taken the first steps towards renegotiating those GATT commitments.
In respect to the second part of the honourable member's question, the proposed European Community's directive on wine package sizes which is to operate from 1 January 1984 makes no provision for a four-litre pack. As the House will know, the Australian wine industry has developed a four-litre pack which has had a considerable imc pact on wine markets. So the directive cuts right across our traditional marketing practices. The Government has taken steps seeking inclusion of this size of pack in the EEe's regulations. I understand that the matter is currently before the Commission and that we can expect an answer in late November or early December.
In respect of the last part of the honourable member's question, Australian exporters, particularly the smaller ones, find the EEC analysis and certification requirements both costly and burdensome. This is another matter that has been taken up with the EEe. I understand also that the United States of America has a similar quarrel with the EEC on the matter. Negotiations are presently under way between the EEC and the United States on this aspect of the honourable member's question. Australia would expect to be

treated in the same way as the United States is treated, as a result of its negotiations. That is the point that we will be chasing.
ICI AUSTRALIA LTD: PETROCHEMICAL COMPLEX
Mr HAYDEN-I ask a question of the Minister for Industry and Commerce. Is Sir Phillip aware that ICI Australia Ltd--
Mr Speaker. I ask the honourable gentleman, in accordance with the practice of the House, to use the title 'Minister for Industry and Commerce'.
Mr HAYDEN-I am sorry. I ask: Is the Minister aware that ICI Australia Ltd today announced that it is deferring indefinitely its proposal to build a $500m petrochemical complex near Geelong in Victoria? Is it a fact that this project would have provided 1,000 full time jobs when completed? Is he aware that ICI has publicly blamed uncertaintyover his Government's support for manufacturing industry as one of the major reasons for the company's decision? Finally, will this latest private sector vote of no confidence in the Government's economic management encourage the Government to reconsider the broad thrust of its economic policy and the damaging strategy of its B u d g e t ? -
'Sir PHILLIP LYNCH-The Government is naturally disappointed at the announcement that ICI Australia Ltd will defer development of the proposed petrochemical complex at Point Wilson in Victoria, although I riote from the statement which has bee.n issued by the company that the economics of the project will certainly remain under review. The company's decision was based on a number of factors which certainly highlighted increasing costs and, most importantly, the effect of wage movements on the construction costs at the complex. I think it is fair to say in response to the honourable gentleman that the world chemical industry, as he is aware, is currently in a position of excess supply which will be exacerbated in the short term by the construction of large new plants in Canada and the Middle East based on low priced feedstock.
I remain confident that in the final analysis Australia, with secure supplies of raw materials, will offer the opportunities for major developments in the chemical industry such as the production of caustic soda from our large and expanding alumina industry. However, as I have said many times and as other Ministers have emphasised many times, resource development projects ar.e neither preordained nor gl\aranteed. They will always be subject to an .ongoing firm

2190 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Questions Without Notice

assessment of market conditions, costs and other factors. Ministers have constantly stressed the need for projects of this type to remain internationally competitive. ICI's announcement demonstrates the crucial importance of keeping wages and other costs under control so as to maintain the competitive nature of Australian industry.
Finally, although individual projects may be deferred from time to time, there is no doubt as to the overall strength of Australia's investment in industrial development. In the course of the past financial year real business investment rose by 21 per cent and all major surveys point to further significant increases during this year. I say to the honourable gentleman that resource development, the investment which is clearly taking place and the continuing strength of manufacturing industry are a reflection of the strength of this Government's economic and industrial policies and will therefore not be subject to any change of the type which has been suggested by the honourable gentleman.

UNAUTHORISED ARRIVALS
Ministerial Statement
Mr MACPHEE (Balaclava-Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs)-by leave-Over recent months my Department received information which suggested that a syndicate operating in a number of countries was planning to send a boat load of illegal immigrants to Australia under the guise of refugees fleeing from Vietnam. The first information suggesting that these plans

may have been put into effect was received on 18 September when we learned that a boat, identified as VT 838, carrying approximately 140 ethnic Chinese, had transited at Pulau Bidong in
Malaysia the previous day with the stated intention of coming on to Australia. There were grounds for doubts about this boat and its passengers based on the facts that the group were determined not to land in Malaysia and be processed there; that the boat was a Thai fishing vessel; and reports from eye witnesses who observed the group stating that they were well dressed and not showing signs of any privations that might be expee<ted of a group claiming to have been at sea eight days prior to their arrival at Bidong.
Against this background, preparations were made for an intensive investigation of the group to commence as soon as the vessel arrived in Australia. A team of specialist officers from my Department and the Australian Federal Police went to Darwin to undertake this task. Assistance was also sought from Hong Kong authorities because of their great experience with the problem of illegal immigration. We were fortunate that the Royal Hong Kong Police were able to send two expert officers to Darwin before the VT 838 arrived. Their contribution with initial planning and during the investigations proved invaluable. The Northern Territory Government was
informed and the Commissioner of the Northern Territory Police Force assigned officers to assist the team with custody arrangements for the group. I would like to extend my appreciation and commendation to all involved in the exercise.
Against the possibility that the people on board the VT 838 were not genuine refugees but part of an organised attempt to circumvent Australia's migration controls, it was decided to advise the Governor-General to proclaim the Immigration (Unauthorized Arrivals) Act 1980. This Act and an amendment Act 1980 were proClaimed on 30 September 1981 to commence on and from that date and gave the Government power to deal with the organisers, master and crew of the VT 838 should they be identified.
The VT 838 arrived at Darwin on 5 October. On board were 146 persons, comprising 53 adult maies, 23 adult fem,ales, and 70 minors. They all claimed to be refugees who had travelled direct from Vietnam to Darwin. On the basis of
suspicions held that the group were seeking to enter Australia illegally, they were taken into custody under the Migration Act and detained at the former quarantine station near Darwin. Intensive interviews were conducted to establish the bona fides of their claim for refugee status. Those interviews have all been completed. Further inquiries

Unaut horised Arrivals

20. October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2193

are being made overseas; but I can inform honourable members in broad terms something about the organisation behind the VT 838 and the people who arrived in her. This information was obtained through statements from most of the adults in the group including two organisers and four crew members.
The organisation of this illegal venture was centred in Taiwan with links in Hong Kong and Thailand. The two principal organisers, a Taiwanese resident and his wife, travelled on the voyage. They were assisted by four crew members who were also living in Taiwan. The organisers advertised their plan by word of mouth amongst the ethnic Chinese Vietnamese community in Taiwan. Most of the passengers were recruited there, though six came from Hong Kong. It is believed that most of them had resided in Vietnam before taking up residence legally in third countries. Their date of departure from Vietnam varied from as long ago as 1954 to as recently as February this year. Some of the minors were born in Taiwan. Almost all the group have relatives there or in Hong Kong.
The cost of the journey was $US2,aaa per adult and from $USl,aaa to $USl,5aa for minors. The families of crew members were charged special discounted rates. By early September a crew and a full compliment of passengers had been recruited. One of the organisers then travelled to Thailand, where arrangements were made with Thai contacts to acquire and provision a suitable boat. Arrangements were made also to receive the passengers and convey them to the place of departure. In the meantime the other organiser made arrangements to fly the passengers from Taiwan and Hong Kong to Bangkok.
On 11 September the Taiwan passengers travelled on three regular international air services to Bangkok. One of these flights transited in Hong Kong, where the six Hong Kong passengers boarded. On arrival at Bangkok members of the entire group were conveyed to hotels, where they remained for two days. During this time the organisers concluded arrangements for the purchase of a fishing vessel. The group members then travelled by train to Hat Yai in Southern Thailand, where they were accommodated in hotels for one night. On 15 September two chartered buses took them to an isolated fishing village near Songkhla, where they boarded a 25-metre Thai fishing vessel. This boat departed the same day under the control of several Thai fishermen and was escorted by another vessel which later took the fishermen back to shore.

When the boat had cleared Thai waters the identification numbers VT 838 were painted on the bow to resemble registration markings on Vietnamese fishing vessels. Members of the group . were then ordered by the organisers to jettison all identification documents and to remove labels from clothing. They then rehearsed a cover story that they were refugees from Vietnam who had left Vung Tau on 9 September. On 17 September the vessel was intercepted by a Malaysian patrol craft and escorted to Pulau Bidong, where there is a United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees refugee camp. The group declined UNHCR encouragement to disembark and resumed the journey to Darwin. Two passengers jumped ship as the VT 838 left Bidong.
None of the group has any claim on Australia to protection under 'the United Nations Convention and Protocol relating to the status of refugees. It is established international practice not to grant asylum to a person who has already established close links or residence with another country as all in this group have done. Their situation' is no different from that or' anyone who, while legally resident in another country, enters Australia illegally. They may well be seeking a better future for themselves and their families, but they do not deserve special consideration over thousands of other people with similar backgrounds who apply to settle legally in Australia each year; nor should they be advantaged over the thousands of genuine refugees from Indo-China and other troubled areas who, unlike this group, have not yet been granted the opportunity to settle permanently in 11 country of resettlement.
Illegal immigration is nothing new. The Regularisation of Status Program, which I announced in June 1980., attracted over 11,50.0. applications from persons who were here illegally. Nearly half of these were from the United Kingdom and Europe. However, honourable members will recall that one of the objectives in mind at that time was to eliminate in the future the incentive for ile legal immigration by taking rigorous action against prohibited immigrants in future. Each member of the group is no longer claiming refugee status. They are currently prohibited immigrants who have sought to gain entry to Australia by a carefully contrived, large scale plan to mislead and put at risk Australia's migration controls.
I want to stress that, in considering the future of the group, my ultimate responsibility is to discourage similar attempts at illegal entry. In this context, I believe the prosecution and eventual deportation of the organisers and crew and the early removal of the fare paying passengers to their

2194 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Unauthorised Arrivals

countries of last residence will be critical in deterring other people from joining similar ventures. I have therefore signed orders for their deportation. Arrangements to effect their deportation, including obtaining necessary travel documents to replace those jettisoned by the group, are expected to take several weeks to complete. Meanwhile a team of officials is preparing the case for prosecutions against the organisers and crew.
In short, the people from the VT 838 are not refugees; they are illegal immigrants. The fact that they have sought to enter Australia purporting to have come directly from Vietnam should not reflect upon Indo-Chinese refugees who have already settled so successfully in our country. It has been a.remarkable demonstration of the maturity of Australia that, in such a relatively short time, it has been able to accept in such a positive way the 56,000 Indo-Chinese refugees who are now part of our community. Those persons, like other legal residents of Australia, should welcome the action being taken by the Government. I should say in conclusion that inquiries have not produced any information linking the VT 838 organisation with earlier arrivals. Interviews conducted at the time of those arrivals did not uncover evidence to suggest that they had not all come direct from Vietnam. I present the following pap.er:
ImmigrantsIllegal' entry-Ministerial Statement, 20 October 1981.

That the House take note of the paper.
Mr YOUNG (Port Adelaide) (3.8)-This incident is in many ways very much a tragedy. There is no doubt that some people in Australia would put forward the ,view that Australia could quite easily accomplish the settlement of the 146 people who arrived in this boat, irrespective of the status which may be given to them. In some ways that may be seen as being a humanitarian approach. But I suspect that everybody would reach the conclusion that, whether it be the refugee program or the orderly migration program, Australia must maintain control over the decisions which are arrived at which allow people to settle permanently in this country. We cannot appear to be encouraging decisions being taken overseas which seek in any way at all to circumvent Australian decision making. When I talk of Australian decision making I do not mean just decisions taken by government; I mean decisions taken with the acceptance and the tolerance of the Australian people. The unique movement of the people from Indo-China to Australia over the last five years, whilst resulting from a government decision, really has been done with the approval and within

the confines of the tolerance of the Australian people. That is why it has b.een done so successfully.
I have no doubt at a\1 that the Australian people now would say that we cannot allow people to organise and dupe others into believing that all they have to do is arrive on our doorstep
to be accepted. It may be that they were encouraged in that approach by virtue of the way in which we have operated the refugee program since 1975. But I hope people overseas will get the message that it is the intention of Australia to participate in these programs in an orderly fashion, where that is possible, to eradicate the arrival of people on our shores as boat people-I believe over 2000 have come in that way-and to interview people beforehand to try to get some understanding of their aims so that we can successfully settle them when they come to Australia.
We cannot predict in 1981 what likely future demands will be made on Australia as a country of permanent settlement. No one can predict what lies ahead for our region. At Question Time, for instance, the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) said what a great place Chile was. But there are many people from Chile who are now alive, settled and working in Australia who would have been put to death, without trial, had they stayed in Chile. So it is with so many of the people for whom we have made homes. Hundreds of
thousands of people who have come here since the war had nowhere else to go. It has been a great achievement for Australia, particularly in the last five years, because although we said and wrote for many years that White Australia had gone, it has only been in the last five years that we have demonstrated that. It is to our credit that many of the countries in the region now acknowledge that it is not just words as far as Australia is concerned; it is practice. But again I reiterate that it can be done only as long as we have the confidence of the Australian people to be able to do it.
We should not ignore the fact that there are
problems associated with the settlement of any new grollp. We should not exaggerate the problems but we should not overlook the problems.
Some people in Australia already are alarmed at the numbers of people who are coming into Australia from Indo-China. But I think we can say with a great deal of satisfaction that the settlement of refugees has proved very satisfactory. Everyone in Australia should attempt to understand the enormous problems of those people who have come to live here. People from Indo-China normally do not look' to migrate from their own country. It is only under very special circumstances, which have existed since the' Vietnam

The Economy

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2195

War and which have built up pressures for these people, that they want to leave their own country. It is' to the credit of Australia, the United States of America, France, Germany and other countries that they have been able to offer these people permanent settlement. That is one thing, but the issue we have before Parliament today is quite another matter; that is, the attempt by people to make money from applying a confidence trick to so many innocent people from Taiwan by saying that all that they have to do is to arrive in Darwin to be given the same treatment as was given to all those people who previously had arrived.
I think there is a warning in this for us to double our guard against any person or any group of people who attempt to do this in the future. People who want to come to Australia must join the queue and we must make the decision on how many people we can properly settle, how many people from overseas the Australian populace is prepared to accept and to fit within the confines of what is required in Australia. We have done this very successfully over a very long period. But I have absolutely no hesitation at all, on behalf of the Opposition, in saying that the Government is left with no alternative but to take the action that has been announced this afternoon by the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs (Mr Macphee). I know there will be some complexities in applying what the Minister has announced because of where the people come from, because of the prosecutions that must be launched against the people who have arranged this journey.
