THE ECONOMY
Discussion of MaUer of Public Importance
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Giles)-Mr Speaker has received a letter from the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The Treasurer's failure to acknowledge or prevent the intensifying credit squeeze.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number ofmembers required by the Standing Orders having risen ill their places-
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (3.17)-Just over a month ago I proposed a matter of public importance regarding private capital inflow and theimplications for the money supply growth of the growing failure of the Government's budgetary strategy for almost half of its projected money supply growth to come from external sources. I said then that if nothing was done to change that strategy there was likely to be a severe shortage of credit and pressure for further interest rate rises. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) said then, and has repeated several times since, that suchclaims were grossly exaggerated and that the-fe was nothing to worry about. However, although he goes on saying that, all the developments since our matter of public importance a little over a month ago bear out and reinforce the truth of what we were saying then. The e.vidence in our submission clearly is that a credit squeeze is developing and is occurring for the very reasons we pointed to a month ago.
I remind the House that the basis of the monetary projections in the Budget are, firstly, that there is to be a target rate of growth in the money supply of 10 per cent to II per cent; that is, the money supply as measured by M3 will increase by 10 per cent or 11 per cent this year. The Government says that almost half-$2.8m-of that growth of 10 or 11 per cent which it expects targeting to achieve will come from external sourc~s; that is, 4.75 per cent of a 10+ per cent growth will come from these external sources. That external source in more technical terms is the surplus on private foreign exchange transactions which in turn is the surplus of private capital inflow over the deficit on private balance of payments current account. The expectation of such a high proportion of money supply growth from external sources was based on the expectation of a further rise in private capital inflow above the enormous levels which occurred last year. Last year we had private capital inflow of an unbelievable $6.5

2196 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

The Economy

billion against the previous record high of $1.9 billion. This year the Budget was framed, according to the Treasurer, on the assumption that the private capital inflow would be a further increase over that $6.5 billion to $7.8 billion in this financial year; that is, four times the level of two years ago which level at that time was the highest ever in our history.
On the basis of that assumption the Treasurer was therefore able to estimate that there would be this 45 per cent of total projected money supply growth coming from external sources. On that assumption, the Government brought in a Budget which was more contractionary than last year. Last year's Budget reduced the domestic deficit by $1,OOOm. It'took that amount of money out of circulation. This year's Budget, being framed on a similar amount of inflow to the money supply from external sources, sought to take out an even greater amount of money from the economy by .increasing the domestic deficit by a further $1.1 billion. So, this year the Government framed its Budget on the assumption that there would be a large inflow of money from external sources
which it would offset by increasing the domestic deficit to $1. 5 billion. It did that by increasing taxes, both income and sales, for all Australian taxpayers and bearing down on Government expenditure through the razor gang measures and other cutbacks. We find that total tax revenue is budgeted to increase by 16 per cent and Government expenditure by only 13 per cent. In that way the Government takes $ 1.5 billion out of the economy to offset what was supposed to come from external sources.
That is the assumption on which the Government framed the Budget. It is an assumption which of course was always a dubious kind of assumption' to make. As the Commonwealth
Bank.of Australia mentioned in its recent economic newsletter, the Budget was based on the most capricious component of money supply formation. Of course, that capricious component is this area of money supply growth from external sources. Since the Government brought down the Budget all the developments have been along the lines which have made unbelievable the assumptions on which 'that Budget was based. It has become clear that the Government's assumption about what would happen in respect of the balance of payments simply will not happen. A dramatic turnaround will have to take place in the rest of this year if the Government's forecasts are to be borne out, and there is no sign of any such dramatic turnaround. The balance of payments figures for one quarter of the year, the September quarter, show that we have an all time high deficit

both in total and on the current account. The current account deficit increased from $1.4 billion in the first quarter last year to $2.3 billion this year.
This reflects the virtually static export figures over the same period because of the world recession. Although capital inflow in the first quarter is up 15 per. cent on what it was last year, our international reserves have fallen by $1.1 billion to the end of September. It seems clear from these figures that there is no surplus on private foreign exchange transactions at all. In other words, the source of money supply growth which was to account for almost half of the growth of the money supply simply has not turned up. There is no growth whatever from that source so far as one can judge from the publicly available figures. Of course, that has extraordinary importance for budgetary and monetary policies.
The evidence since the end of the September quarter shows no improvement to mid-October. The international reserve figures up until last Wednesday show virtually the same level of reserves as at the end of September. Therefore, we can say that in the first half of October there has been no improvement in this situation; in other words, no surplus on private fO,reign exchange transactions is likely to have developed in that period. Thus, with over a quarter of the year gone, there has been no addition to the money supply from this source and no sign that such an increase will eventually be forthcoming. The effect on the money supply growth not surprisingly is that it has slowed down very considerably. The available figures show a quite disturbing picture. The figures for the three months June to August show an annualised rate of growth of money supply of 9 per cent. That is our calculation based on the published figures from the Reserve Bank of
Australia.
I note in passing that the Reserve Bank has recently stopped publishing these figures for reasons which remain unexplained. Up until the last few months we had quarterly figures given on an annualised basis so that we could see what was happening to the trend in money supply growth rates on a quarterly basis. We now have to work those figures out for ourselves as they are not published any more. If they were published, they would show this figure of 9 per cent. That quarterly figure hides what has happened so far in this financial year. If we look at July and August, the two months which are available for this financial year, we see that growth in the money supply over that period at an annualised rate is 4.9 per cent. That is far below the target rate of growth of 10 to 11 per cent. If that were to be continued for any length of time-it has already continued for some

The Economy

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2197

time and there is little evidence so far of any change in September, or in the period since the end of August-then we would certainly have an intensifying credit squeeze, with all of the implications that has for shortage of funds, and rising interest rates, and the effect on growth and employment. This is a very serious development indeed. If it continues and the Government does nothing about it, or does insufficient to deal with the problem, the economy will be in severe straits.
I say in passing that, even if the Budget assumptions were being fulfilled, we would still argue strongly that the target rate of growth of the money supply at 10 to 11 per cent is too tight, given the expectations of inflation of nearly 11 per cent and growth of 3t per cent. In other words, there will be an increase of over 14 per cent in the, money value of output this year, on the Budget assumptions. If there is a target rate of growth of 10 to 11 per cent, it is likely that that money supply growth will be too tight to prevent further pressures for higher interest rates and thus there will be a shortage of credit. Of course, we are not talking about a situation in which the Budget assumptions are being fulfilled. They are being very much not fulfilled in this area of the external source of money supply growth. Therefore, we have a situation where total money supply growth is running far below the budgeted level, with much more severe implications for shortage of credit and rising interest rates.
There is other evidence of credit shortage. Statistics for lending by banks and building societies for housing for the three months to July, which are the latest figures available, show that both the number of loans and the total amount of money lent for this purpose was 13 per cent less in that period than for the same period last year. The latest figures for building societies alone show that in August of this year the total number of loans approved for purchase or construction of dwellings has declined by 38 per cent compared with the figure for the same period last year.
If we look at interest rates, despite the Treasurer's assertions that there are favourable signs, in only the last few days we have seen further increases in interest rates by building societies in Victoria. So we still have a situation in which interest rate increases are occurring. Given that interest rates are at their highest level ever in our history-that is what this Treasurer has been able to achieve for us in his period as the
Treasurer of n,;s nation-if there are to be further
increases in interest rates, that is something which has enormous implications for the people of this country.

What needs to be done? Firstly, we say that the
Government has to do something. It IS not good
enough for the Treasurer simply to say: 'Look, all this is untrue. There is no need to be concerned.' The facts are that there is need to be concerned. The facts support those who are making the assertions that credit is tight, that the money supply growth is far below expectations, and it is occurring in that way because the Budget assumptions simply are not being borne out. It is not enough to make reassuring noises, saying that the Government policy is flexible and that various measures are available for the Government to offset these factors. That is not much good unless these measures in fact are used, and there has not been much sign of that happening, All the Government has done so far is to raise the limit for bank advances from 10 per cent to 12 per cent. That was done a little over a month ago. Although that may be of some assistance, the situation is now so serious that it must be clear that that was a totally inadequate response.
We say that the Government should rel'ease funds from statutory reserve deposits, These are funds which the Reserve Bank of Australia requires the trading banks to hold with the Reserve Bank. An amount of $1.9 billion is held in statutory reserves at pr~sent. That is money which is locked up and which, if it were released to some degree, would be available to the banks to lend out and so would offset this current shortage of credit. We believe that it is high time such action was taken by the Government.
We also believe that there is need for a change in the Budget strategy. The Budget is clearly off target, given that it was framed on an assumption which has failed to be borne out; that is, the rationale for increasing income taxes and for increasing sales taxes. The Government said to the Australian people in a fairly convoluted way: 'We have to do this because we have to offset the money supply impact of this private capital inflow coming in and adding to the money'supply. If we do not increase taxes in this way, we will have a higher rate of growth of money supply than we would otherwise achieve.' That situation is not applying and there is no evidence that it will apply in the rest of this financial year. Given that the basic assumption on which the Australian people are being taxed has been taken away, the reason for that taxation policy and that Budget strategy has substantially evaporated. The Government must now look not only at adjusting its monetary policy but also at adjusting its budgetary policy because the very basis on which it was framed is no longer being fulfilled. We believe that those are

2198 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

The Economy

important factors for this Government to take into account.
1 also draw attention to the fact that, if the Government does not take action, there will, in our view, be further pressure on interest rates. This would be an ironic result, given that the Treasurer has proclaimed that the key objective of this Budget has been to remove the pressure on interest rates. 1 do not have time to go into the reasons why 1 think that is a totally apocryphal argument. Nevertheless, it is the argument that is being used by the Treasurer. It remains the case that if he continues with this strategy and if the current situation continues in respect of the failure of money supply growth to come from external sources, we will find a situation where, with a low rate of growth of money supply there will be increasing pressure on interest rates brought about by a Budget which is supposed to be removing the pressure from interest rates. This is an extraordinarily ironic situation but one which will come to fruition unless the Treasurer faces up to reality, and stops burying his head in the sand and saying there is nothing to worry about, and starts taking some action to ensure that we do not' have even higher interest rates than those that have been inflicted on the Australian people so far.
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (3.32)-1 do not need to condemn the arguments used by the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) in this matter of public importance. That condemnation came best out of the mouth of his own leader, the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden), when he was interviewed on the Mike Carlton show as recently as 16 July 1981. What Mr Hayden said was very instructive.
Mr Willis-Three months ago.
Mr HOWARD-Three months ago, but what he said then was still relevant and remains relevant to the arguments that have been advanced in this debate. Mr Hayden said:
. . . the government won't clamp controls on the inflow of capital . . . 11-
That is the inflow of capital-
adds to the supply of money and the only way the Government can accommodate that extra supply coming in from outside is to squeeze down on the supply available to Australians.
So what the Leader of the Opposition was saying on 16 July was that if too much money is coming in from abroad the availability of domestic credit has to be tightened. The opposite of that is that if there is a shortfall of money coming in from overseas, we can ease up on domestic credit expansion. That is the whole nub of the argument. Unwittingly, without knowing that it might be used against him, the Leader of the Opposition put his

finger on the very argument the Government advances to reject the proposition moved by the honourable member for Gellibrand. It is a serious proposition. 1 put it to the House that in order to succeed the Opposition has to establish two things. It firstly has to establish that so far the evidence suggests that Australia has no hope of achieving the private external surplus of around $2,800m which was projected as a likely outcome at the time the Budget was brought down. Having established that, it also has to establish that it is an automatic consequence of that fact that we will have a drastic shortage of credit in Australia. The Opposition has to discharge both of those propositions before it has any right to concern and scare sections of the Australian community with totally misleading and irresponsible talk about a credit squeeze.
Let me deal with the first of those arguments. The fact is that so far during this financial year the
current account deficit has been very much greater than we forecast at the time of the Budget. That is hardly surprising. 1 think even the honourable member for Gellibrand would agree with me that when we are talking about the external account, we are dealing with a very volatile area
of economic forecasting. It is extremely difficult to predict with any degree of real accuracy what will happen on the trade account, what will happen to invisibles, and what will happen with private capital inflow. Forecasting is a notoriously hazardous exercise in this area. But through a combination of a continually depressed world demand for commodities, many falls in significant metal prices, the disruptive effects of a coal strike in Queensland, the disruptive effects of a number of waterfront strikes around Australia, and other factors, the shortfall of exports to imports has been much greater than we expected. As a result during the first three months of this financial year our current account deficit, not the volume of pri-
vate capital inflow, was significantly different from what we expected. During those first three months the volume of private capital inflow was 15 per cent greater than it was during the first three months of 1980-81.
The other important thing to bear in mind is that it is very dangerous indeed to try to draw
final conclusions about the outcome for the year on the basis of performance during one quarter. Last year, the volume of private capital inflow in the June quarter was $2,306m. In other words, more than one-third of the total record amount of $6,500m that came into Australia from abroad last year came in during the June quarter because that was the quarter in which it was needed. That is the quarter in which traditionally credit is

The Economy

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2199

tighter because of the normal seasonal liquidity rundown. That fact, which seems to have been completely ignored in the remarks that have been made by the honourable member for Gellibrand, once again underscores the danger of grabbing three months figures; muliplying them by four and saying: 'We have doom and disaster'. It is a very hazardous exercise indeed. It is far from proved, on the figures that the honourable member for Gellibrand has advanced, that a private capital inflow figure of $7,000m to $8,000m this year will not be achieved. That was the best estimate that could be made by the Government's advisers at the time the Budget was put together. It was an estimate made in good faith. It was an estimate in respect of which there is no evidence so far to suggest that it will be impossible to achieve.
The prospective business investment figures in Australia, the most recently published quarterly survey of the investment intentions of business in Australia, indicate that prospective business in-
vestment is still very strong. That is as good an indicator as anyone can find in order to try to make some kind of forward projection. Let me say, in case somebody from the Opposition raises it during the debate, the fact that one investment project in Victoria is not going ahead is no reason to suggest that there has been a turnaround in investment prospects in Australia. We ought to get away from having a national inquisition every time an individual investment project does not go ahead. Many investment projects will not go ahead in the future but a great many more will go ahead. We have to look at the overall situation. There can be absolutely no doubt that, by any measure, according to any set of statistics, the
prospects for business investment in this country remain extremely strong.
I use the Scottish nomenclature when I say that the case at the very least is not proven-the case being the suggestion by the honourable member for Gellibrand that we will not achieve the private capital inflow target of $7,000m to $8,OOOm. I suggest to him that he should not jump to conclusions. I suggest to him that multiplying the figures for the first three months of this year by four and assuming that the trade account will perform in the same way for the remainder of this year could well prove to be a very dangerous and unproductive exercise.
But perhaps more important than that is the proposition that even if those predictions are borne out, even if we do not get anything like $7,000m to $8,000m private capital inflow this year, and even if the current account continues in substantial deficit for the remainder of this financial year, there is no reason on earth why we

should have a credit squeeze, given the proper and the right amount of economic management. I win take the honourable member for Gellibrand back to 1974, which was the last occasion on which this country had a very severe credit squeeze. We all remember what happened to the prices that had to be paid by trading banks in Australia for certificates of deposit during some of the more difficult days and weeks of that credit squeeze in 1974. Our financial system has come a long way since those days. It is more flexible, it is capable of a natural response to a tightening in the availability of credit and, through the .external account, it' has more weapons available to it now than it had in 1974. For example, in 1974 there was an embargo on short term borrowings of up to six months, which meant that banks' and other financial institutions caught with a very difficult and unexpected liquidity situation did not have the access to overseas funds that Australian banks, merchant banks and other financial institutions have now to prevent the adverse effects of a credit squeeze from occurring because of unexpected developments in the economy.
Mr Porter-They want to do it all again.
Mr HOWARD-Of course they do. As' the honourable member for Gellibrand said, the Reserve Bank of Australia has available to iUhe capacity to make releases from the statutory reserve deposit. The Government has available to it the capacity to adopt a more flexible approach regarding the financing of the wheal harvest: In recent years, for monetary control reasons, the Government has put the advance payment to growers out to commercial borrowings. If there is a monetary supply reason to qualify or to reverse that in some way the Government has the capacity to do that. It is yet another weapon which is available to the Government to ease the domestic credit situation if it is necessary to do so.
We have a far more flexible exchange rate system than we had when the last credit squeeze occurred. State authorities now borrow for infrastructure purposes. There can be a larger volume of overseas borrowings for those purposes by the State authorities to supplement the external account if that proves to be necessary. The list goes on. There is, of course, the capacity to allow the trading banks to lend larger amounts of money. So when one looks at the instruments that are available, even if I were to accept the proposition of the honourable member for Gellibrand-that is, that it is more probable than not that, on the basis of the performance so far this year, private capital figures estimated at the time of the Budget and the current account deficit will not perform as strongly as we had

2200 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

The Economy

expected-it would not follow for a moment that a credit squeeze ought to occur in this country. I repeat what I have said on a number of occasions over the past few days. The Government will not allow it to occur. The instruments that are available to the Government and the way in which we have shown a flexibility in financial management over the past few years, particularly over the past 12 months, will demonstrate to the. Australian community that it will not occur.
I conclude by returning to some observations I have made on earlier occasions in response to what the Leader of the Opposition and the honourable member for Gellibrand have said on this subject. It is not a Budget strategy to have a private capital inflow of $7,000m to $8,000m. The Budget strategy is to control inflation through a combination of economic instruments. One of those is to run an appropriately tight monetary policy. It stands to reason that if the contribution coming from abroad is less than we expected when the Budget was brough down then we ought-we have the capacity-to make a larger volume of money available domestically. I find it faintly ridiculous and quite ironic that only three or four months ago the Leader of the Opposition was tramping around this country saying: 'Do you know what the Government has to do? It has to stop all that wicked money' coming in'. He was saying: 'Stop it. Block it out. Do not allow it to come in. It is destroying Australia's economy'. The record is replete. On 18 August 1981 the following question was asked in ,the House.
. .". will the Treasurer immediately impose controls on short term foreign capital inflows?
My heavens, where would we be if I had taken the advice of the Leader of the Opposition on 18 August and imposed controls on short term capital inflows? We would certainly have a credit squeeze in Australia at' present. We would have choked off and destroyed one of the most valuable safety valves that this economy has if there is a shortfall of funds from overseas-that is, the capacity for the banking system and the financial system in this country to borrow from abroad on a. short term basis. Yet a few months ago the Leader of the Opposition was encouraging me to do precisely that. That would indeed have caused a very significant credit squeeze.
I reject the basis of the matter of public importance. I reject it because, firstly, the proposition about the performance of our external account has not been established and, secondly and basically, even if it were established, the instruments and the flexibility available to the Australian Government to respond to shortages of money coming from abroad are infinitely greater and

infinitely more likely to avoid a credit squeeze than was the case in 1974 when a credit squeeze last occurred.

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Giles)-Order! The honourable member may not use that sort of language. I ask him to use only stereotype language.
Mr ROBERT BROWN-I am surprised that the Treasurer would challenge the statements of the Leader of the Opposition in the way in which he has. Mr PORTER (Barker) (3.56)-It was interesting to hear the honourable member for Hunter (Mf Robert Brown) suggesting that on the basis of three months figures the Government ought to abandon its monetary policy and do something about increasing the money supply. He quoted what David Love of Syntec said on Nationwide last night to support his argument, a rather tenuous argument and a rather tenuous quote. I quote in detail what David Love said about capital inflow. He said:
It's running slightly, I'd say, below projection, but on the basis of the way US interest rates are going and the way Australian interest rates are now, J would expect a record flow of capital, higher than anything we've seen, in the period from about March onwards through the June'quarter in 1982.
The honourable member for Hunter advocated that we abandon the monetary policy, increase the money supply in the short term and forget about what will happen in the whole of this financial year. That is a reckless argument without any economic foundation. When looking at the basis of the argument about a credit squeeze one obviously has to consider money supply and interest rates. In the period before the Budget was presented the Treasurer (Mr Howard) had a number of things on his mind. Wage demands were excessive and there was an atrocious number of industrial disputes. Inflationary pressures and interest rates were increasing. This last aspect, of course, is of enormous concern to governments. The effect of interest rates on small businesses, on farmers, on builders and on those seeking or paying off a home loan is of great consequence to this Government and to the people concerned.
Steps were taken to reduce the upward pressure on interest rates and inflation. Earlier this year the steps taken by the Government were to reduce our borrowing program and also those of the States. Further, in the Budget the Governmentclearly looking to this problem of interest ratesbudgeted for a sizeable domestic surplus. In effect that means we have to borrow less. All these steps were taken to reduce the governments competition for available loan funds in Australia. Obviously, on that basis, more of the available funds will go to those small businessmen, the farmers, the home builders-the people we are concerned about, the average Australian. The evidence

available to the Government indicated that there was just not enough money in Australia for those who wanted to borrow it, for those who wanted to expand their businesses. Therefore the Government, bearing in mind the demand for borrowed funds in Australia, accepted that there would be a capital inflow which would assist in meeting those demands. But it is no good for the Government, in trying to solve interest rate problems, to act in a vacuum; in other words, to act on that problem without taking into account the rest of the economy. It has to keep the economy on the rails.
Remember that in the lead-up to the Budget a major consideration was the inflationary pressure in the economy. Therefore, the Budget quite rightly stated that the money supply would be kept under control. To allow the money supply to run ahead of the inflation rate and the growth rate is to invite another bout of inflation in the current economic circumstances. Therefore the Treasurer, bearing in mind the attractiveness of Australia as a place to invest and the need those with the large resources projects to have access to funds to get under way, acknowledged in the Budget that there was likely to be a large capital inflow. That capital inflow will facilitate further development projects and will create more jobs.
However, I repeat that it is important to keep the money supply growth under control. There-
fore, the Budget has taken account of the growth in the money supply, originating from both domestic and overseas sources. The Opposition is now saying that because after the first three months of this year capital inflow does not equal a quarter of the estimated total capital inflow we are entering a credit squeeze. It says that is happening because the money supply will be significaftly less than predicted in the Budget and significantly less than required by the economy. The reasoning and validity of the Opposition's argument are without foundation.
When one looks at the facts it is clearly seen that the motive for the statement made by the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) over the weekend and by the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) today is to cause uncertainty in the Australian business community and to unsettle confidence in the future of the Australian economy for cheap political gain. I suppose they believe that if they can wreck the economy, causing a recession, unemployment and a lower standard of living, it will increase their political prospects. Such tactics I believe are dishonest. The facts do not support their argument and their goal will not be achieved. The Leader of the Opposition has been notoriously irresponsible in his predictions in relation to the economy. One

TACTICAL FIGHTER FORCE
Ministerial Statement
Mr KILLEN (Moreton-Minister for Defence)-by leave-The Government has selected, as Australia's new tactical fighter, the FAI8, manufactured by the McDonnell-D?uglas Corporation of the United States of Amenca. In April 1979, I said to the Parliament t.hat ~ur strategic and geographic circumstances differ III many respects from those of the countries for which the contending aircraft were primarily designed. If Qur force is called upon to defend this country or its inte.ests, it may have to do so alone, not as part of a larger allied force having the range of capabilities possible with a mixture of aircraft. We therefore sought an aircraft versatile in several roles, against both air and surface targets, in ~II weather conditions. We could not favour an aircraft because of its excellence in anyone particular quality.

. . . the 1980s are looming as a decade of despair.
Later that year he said:
The decade of the 1980s will be a decade of gloom.
When we got to the 1980s he changed his tune. He said:
By nature, [ am inclined to optimism.
Quite frankly, it is clear that the Leader of ~he Opposition is unbelievable. I cannot remember when he has been right with his predictions. The facts are that capital inflow figures for the last three months are below a quarter of the whole year's estimate. Let us look at the capital inflow figures for last year. They vary enormously from month to month and the first quarter figures are usually low. The net apparent capital inflow for August last year was $264m. The figure for September was $384m and the figure for October was $1 ,072m. That is an increase between months during a period of three months of about 300 per cent. The figure increased from $264m in one month to $1,072m two months later. The Opposition is now saying that because of the first three months' figures of this year we ought to change our economic strategy, release some more money into the financial system of Australia and abandon the budgetary strategy. The Opposition's argument is without foundation. T.here is no cred!t squeeze. It is a blinkered, short-sighted, economically erroneous argument. It appears. th~t t~e Labor Opposition has not learned from ItS time III power in 1974, when it imposed a credit squeeze upon this country.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Giles) Order! The discussion is concluded.

In air combat, our fighters are likely to have to operate over and beyond our vast northern regions without the extensive ~nd close cont~ol available in the European environment. Hostile surface forces would usually have to cross large expanses of water. Australia's tactical fighter therefore requires long range and endurance to be able to seek out its targets at a distance. It requires a comprehensive array of air-to-air and air-tosurface weapons necessary to engage them. The nature of our land is such that we must be able to deploy our Tactical Fighter Force at short notice, over long distances, into bare bases, and to operate there supported by extended air resupply links. The aircraft we sought therefore had to be maintainable with the minimum of front line support equipment. It had to have superior qualities of reliability and operational availability. We also sought minimal peace time training losses of aircraft and pilots. Because the aircraft chosen must see us through the next 20 years or more, we sought the potential to accommodate the improved weapons and other systems that will undoubtedly emerge during its long life span.
These were the operational factors that bore on the selection-a selection which, to a large extent, will determine the shape of the Royal Australian Air Force into the next century. We had to choose between two magnificent aircraft-the FA 18 and the General Dynamics F 16. Both in their different ways are splendidly impressive and capabl~ machines. However, it has become clear that III relation to the operational factors I have just -indicated the FA18 will be better suited to our needs.

2204 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Tactical Fighter Force

The FA 18 has been developed from the outset as a !llulti-role aircraft, with all-weather, air-to-air and air-to-surface missiles, and all-weather navigation and weapons targeting systems. These systems give it unparalleled flexibility. Because it was designed to operate from aircraft carriers, reliability and ease of maintenance were essential elements of the specification of the FA 18. The testing program has established confidence that these objectives, and from them a very high level of availability, will be achieved. The FAI8 is well suited to deployment and operation from forward bases because it requires relatively little airfield support and test equipment. It is well suited to our operating, deployment and maintenance requirements. It has good potential for adding improved weapons and other systems as these become available. It has features which strongly support the pilot in the efficient and safe prosecution of his mission. Finally, my advisers are unanimous in their advice that, principally because of its twin engines, the FA 18 offers a clear prospect of fewer losses throughout its operating life.
Since the Government deferred the selection last year, teams of RAAF and civilian officers have re-examined and refined their assessments of the two contending aircraft. In their endeavours they have had throughout the unstinting assistance of the United States departments of Defense, Navy and Air Force and of the two prime manufacturers and their sub-contractors. The Government is deeply appreciative of the support given. My senior advisers, and in particular the Chief Defence Scientist and the Chief of Air Force Technical Services, now see no substantial technical obstacles to the development of either aircraft in the form that we have sought.
The aerodynamic problems evidenced in the FA 18 have been satisfactorily resolved. The United States Department of Defense has now given approval for full production of the aircraft in the air-to-air fighter role. Clearance of additional weapons for the air-to-surface attack role is not due until about this time next year. My advisers are now confident that the outcome will be acceptable, but we will be requiring the United States Navy not to place the main contract for our aircraft until this clearance is available. Sufficient structural fatigue testing has been completed to clear the FA 18 for its first ten years of operation. Tests necessary to give assurances of a further ten years of life' will continue until 1984. Any consequential modifications to achieve this end will be known by then. In the case of the F16, uncertainties that earlier surrounded the progress of its weapons enhancement program have receded, but

nevertheless they persist in some respects. Particular doubts have risen about the F16's new all weather targeting and navigation systemsnecessary to meet Australia's requirements.
There will be a very proper concern too that the cost of such a vast project'should be known with confidence. There will be a temptation to turn back to earlier experiences and see them reflected in the future, and there can never, of course, be absolute guarantees about a production program which reaches until the end of the decade, Concerns about cost should be tempered with an understanding of the processes that have contributed and will continue to contribute to our confidence. In their examination of the two contenders, our evaluation teams have had unconstrained access to all of the material on these aircraft that is available to the ,US Department of Defense. That organisation has behind it the experience of managing the world's most formidable manufacturing programs.
The review processes available to me have been strengthened by the defence re-organisation of the mid-1970s. Recommendations now flow from a formalised process of examination which consolidates the deliberate judgments of the most senior specialists in all of the relevant skills-scientific, engineering, financial, legal-contractual and more. It deserves to be said that the professional and technical qualifications of those involved in the new tactical fighter project are second to none. I hope the country will make proper acknowledgment of that fact. To implement the project, we are making arrangements that will allow this country to place its experts within the US Government's project management team. They will participate in its day to day processes. The Government believes that a program can be implemented that minimises Australia's exposure to the uncertainties of the future and that a decision can and should be made now.
The overall project cost of 75 FA18 aircraft is $2,430m in August 1981 prices. This includes the costs of some configuration changes to meet the RAAF's operational requirements and the cost of simulators, spares, test equipment, training equipment and the like. It includes also the cost of new buildings and overhaul facilities at RAAF bases and the costs of establishing important support capabilities in Australian industry. A contingency sum has been included where risks remain.
There has been public speculation about the difference in the cost of the alternative F16 aircraft. The overall project cost for the F16 would have been some seven per cent less than for the FAI8. However, it is estimated that when we take

Tactical Fighter Force

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2205

account of running and support costs and of attrition throughout the life of the aircraft this difference will be substantially reduced. The FAI8 costs have grown during development. I cannot rule out further increases. I have said that there could be modifications flowing from structural testing. They are not expected to be costly.
The chief risk appears to be the possibility of reduction in the production rate in the early years.of the program, thus increasing production costs. To contain cost growth, the Government is stipulating that the manufacture of the first of our aircraft should not precede a specified number of aircraft in the United States program unless we decide otherwise. In this way, we will secure for Australia the benefit of the continuing testing and development of the aircraft and of early United States squadron experience. These and other arrangements too detailed to relate now give us advantages that we have not had in the management of earlier programs. There will, of course, be cost increases due to world-wide inflationary trends. As has been done in the past for other major equipment acquisitions, the Government will keep the House informed of movements in the cost of the project, both up and down, and both real and inflationary, against the base of the August 1981 price levels.
During 1980 I reached with the United States Department of Defense very satisfactory arrangements under which the project has been developed and under which the associated Australian industrial program will be put into effect and managed. The actual purchase instruments will contain more detailed conditions. My Department has necessarily yet to conclude discussions with the United States departments on a number of matters that relate to the specific aircraft chosen. I will not be agreeing to the acceptance of formal purchase documents until a satisfactory position has been reached. On present planning the RAAF can look forward to receiving its first FA 18 before the end of 1984. Pilots should begin operational training with them in the second half of 1985. Aircraft should be flowing from Australian production at a rate of three aircraft every two months by the middle of 1986, and deliveries should be completed in 1990.
I turn now to the role that Australian industry will have in the new tactical fighter program. Establishment of a capability for through-life support in Australia is a fundamental element of the program. The new fighter will be in service with the RAAF well into the next century. Australian industry must command key technologies needed to support it. Agreement has been reached with

the United States contractors on work to be carried out on our aircraft in Australia and on an associated export offset program. The added value of the offset work is to be 30 per cent of the imported cost of the program, and there will be compensation provisions if the United States contractors fail to honour their commitments.
The corner-stone of the Australian industry program will be the final assembly and test of the FAI8 at the Government Aircraft Factories at Avalon. This will equip Australian engineers and technicians with an intimate understanding of the sophisticated sub-systems of the aircraft. The engines will also be assembled and tested in Australia at the Commonwealth Aircraft Corporation at Melbourne. Major parts of the aircraft, of its engines and of its electronic and mechanical systems will be manufactured in Australia in order to introduce new technologies we need to support the FAI8. To carry out this work many new facilities and items of specialised production and test equipment will be introduced into our factories. This will have a dramatic effect on our skills in many areas-to name but a few: Radar, digital electronics, control systems and new supermaterials in engines and structures.
Unavoidably the cost of work done in Australia for our own relatively small numbers of aircraft will be greater than those in the United States. These additional costs are the price we have to pay for the self-reliance we want in the support of our own aircraft. They have been included in the total cost figure I gave earlier:. Included also in the costs are investments in tooling, test equipment and machinery, plant and facilities. Much of this investment would be required in any event to support the new tactical fighter in Australia and will have application to other defence programs in the future.
The Tactical Fighter Force project is a major national enterprise. It will involve not only the RAAF and my Department, but also a host of contractors and sub-contractors in Australia's defence-related industry. It will provide us with a central element of the forces needed to defend our nation's security. It will develop our technological and industrial capacity in an area critical to our defences but also important on a wider national basis. The presence, in this part of the world, of repair and overhaul facilities for the FAI8 will be a significant military advantage also for our ANZUS ally, the United States. The benefits of inter-operability and common support will be of value to both nations.
We have not entered upon this large and testing program without carefully weighing the risks. I

2206 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Tactical Fighter Force

am confident that we have in being a work force with the skills necessary to meet this demanding challenge. It would be difficult for anyone not close to it to conceive the scale of the task involved in the tactical fighter evaluation and its industry program. Those who have carried it
through have worked with extreme dedication. I express my appreciation of their efforts. I ask the House to give this undertaking its complete support.
I now inform the House of a further major decision concerning the RAAF and its long range maritime patrol force. Instead of the modernisation announced last year of the older P3B Orion aircraft, the Government has decided to sell the P3Bs and to acquire 10 more P3Cs subject to satisfactory contractual negotiations. The cost of proceedingthis way is estimated at $240m. It involves a premium of some $50m, which, in the Government's view, is worth paying. We shall avoid the major disruption of the maritime patrol force involved in a major modernisation of half of its aircraft. The P3Bs when modernised would have had a life expectancy of less than half of that of the existing P3Cs, which will not need replacement until beyond the end of the century. The new aircraft will be equipped, as are the 10 P3Cs we now operate, for the Harpoon anti-ship missile and the Australian-developed Barra anti-submarine sonobuoy system. I expect the first aircraft to be delivered in 1985 and the entire squadron to be reequipped by 1987.
The House will recall the Government's decision last year concerning the follow-on destroyer project. I announced the intention to build two FFG-type ships at Williamstown Naval Dockyard, subject to a satisfactory resolution of a number of problems affecting productivity there. I expect to be dealing shortly with recommendations about the future of t;lat dockyard. Meantime, the Government has decided to order longlead items from the United States for the construction in due course of the first vessel.
In making this decision now, we make it possible, should the problems that have beset Williamstown in recent years be resolved, to begin construction of the ship there next year. If there is no improvement-I emphasise this and I want this to be clear too, not in any provocative way-we shall have to look to construction elsewhere. An order of long-lead items for a follow-on destroyer involves no commitment to build at Williamstown. Our plans for the development of the Australian-designed minehunting catamaran are proceeding and tenders for construction of two prototypes are presently being evaluated. But to complement these .minehunters we need to be

able to sweep mines. confirm to honourable members that the British Hunt class is under consideration for this role. We have the opportunity
to acquire vessels ordered originally for the Royal Navy. We are investigating the acquisition of two
vessels with an option on a third. The decision will
be made later in this financial year.

There have been a number of recent decisions regarding new equipment for the Army. Equip-
ment operated by the Army is characterised by
quantity and diversity. Few items attract the pub-
lie'spotlight sometimes focused on the ships and aircraft of the other Services. The Budget makes
provision for a range of new equipment for the
Army. Several decisions, for example, those relat-
ing to the acquisition of some 2,235 new trucks of
two types, totalling over $150m have been the subject recently of separate announcements.

However, I want to mention three projects today. All three will be implemented in such a way as significantly to enhance Australian self reliance. The first is the establishment of production capability and the initial manufacture by
Australian industry of a new 105 mm artillery weapon of an overseas design. On the successful completion of this preproduction phase, the weapon and its ammunition will be ordered in substantial numbers. It will be considerably more capable than our existing 105 mm guns. I shall be releasing further details shortly.

The second, Project Raven, aims to provide the
Army with a new family of single-channel port-
able tactical radios. The Government has decided to allocate resources to the development of the
high frequency component of the radios by Aus-
tralian industry and to preparations for its subsequent production. This project will transfer de-
sign skills and technology to Australia that will

Tactical Fighter Force

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2207

place this country in the forefront of world developments in the field. Thirdly, I inform the House of the Government's decision to spend over a period of several years some $80m on expanding the Army's operational reserve of ammunition. Some $60m of this will be spent on manufacturing ammunition in Australia, including types of ammunition not previously produced here. The development, within industry, of capacities needed to supply and support the Defence Force, and thus to increase our self-reliance, continues to have a high place in the Government's considerations. Participation by Australian industry in the provision of defence equipment is one important means of implementing this policy. Another involves direct expenditure on facilities in industry.
The 1981-82 Budget makes provision for an approximate doubling of expenditure-to $39.5m --on new plant and facilities in the aircraft industry and munitions factories. Major facilities being established include a nitroglycerine plant at Mulwala, New South Wales, a composite material and bonding workshop at the Government Aircraft Factories, Melbourne and a heavy shell-forging facility at the Ordnance Factory at Maribyrnong in Victoria. We are also expanding the existing facilities for manufacturing Barra Sonobuoys in Sydney and for the production of micro-electronic components in the plants of several commercial defence contractors. Virtually all defence activities depend on infrastructure-bases, barracks, dockyards, workshops, stores, training establishments, communication stations and a variety of other facilities.
In 1981-82, $179m is to be spent on capital facilities for defence, much of it in the north and west of Australia. This month, a new ammunition depot was opened at HMAS Stirling, near Perth and a fuel storage capacity is being provided there. During 1982, the new patrol boat bases at Cairns and Darwin will be completed. Improvements will be made to the RAAF airfield at Darwin, in preparation for the basing there of a tactical fighter squadron, and to the airfield at Learmonth. Work is continuing on the design of theproposed airfield at Derby.
The second major stage in the modernisation of Garden Island Dockyard, Sydney, will be commenced this year. The RAAF's operational flexibility will be improved by additional fuel storage capacity at Tindal, Williamtown, Amberley and Richmond. Planning will continue for facilities at Williamtown to support the new tactical fighter. The Government proposes to improve the airfield at Woomera so that it can be used more intensively by all the types of aircraft operated by the Defence Force.

The increasing technical sophistication of modern defence equipment requires additional trained manpower. Accommodation to cope with extra trainees is to be provided at RAAF technical training schools at Wagga and Laverton. Plans are also being developed for new training facilities at the RAAF School of Navigation at East Sale. New works will be undertaken at the Army's Armoured Centre at Puckapunyal, and improved facilities for training army officers for higher command are proposed at the Army Staff College at Queenscliff. It is also proposed to construct a new building at the Materials Research Laboratory at Maribyrnong to support advanced research and development in laser technology.
An important element of the defence infrastructure is housing for servicemen and their families. Over 200 houses will be provided in Townsville, for members of the Operational Deployment Force and 95 houses at LiverpoolHolsworthy, for members of the Army's 1st Task Force. Some service houses which are below acceptable standards, such as at the Army establishment at Woodside, South Australia, will be replaced. The measures and decisions that I have so far been describing, all involving capital investments, are mostly directed towards longer term objectives. Some, especially those in the areas of industrial capacity and defence facilities development, are specifically tailored to allow us to shift more easily to a higher level of defence preparedness should that later be judged necessary.
Time will not permit me today to describe .the multiplicity of other activities being undertaken with a similar purpose. Many of them are not especially glamorous and do not attract much attention. They concern infrastructure and manpower, especially in areas where there are long lead times for the acquisition of skills. They are, however, very important for their effect on our expansion capabilities for the future. So, too, are the underlying planning priorities that require each new initiative to provide in Australia, managed by Australians, arrangements that will allow us to use and support our weapons, in an independent force: The training schools; the 'software' facilities; the computer-managed stock control systems; the command, control and communications systems; the joint staff training; and the hundreds of other activities that could stand us in good stead should we need greater military strength and have to fight alone.
As well as these longer term measures, we are lifting our levels of defence preparedness in the shorter term by a number of means. I touch briefly, today, on but a few. The activity of the Defence Force has been stepped up. This will

Over the period since 1976 estimated real growth in Defence outlays has already averaged slightly more than 3 per cent per annum, as compared with an annual average real growth in total Commonwealth Budget outlays, over the same period, of slightly more than 1 per cent. The Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) announced early in 1980 that the Govc;;rnment had decided upon greater allocation of resources to our defences. Allocation of resources is made in the context of the Budget each year. However, the Government understands the need for a stable planning base and in March 1980 I announced details of the planning basis for the five years 1980 to 1985. It involved defence spending of some $17,600m in August 1979 prices-over the five years; a~ average annual growth rate of about 7 per cent; spending to increase to around 3 per cent of gross domestic product by 1984-85; and an increase, over the five years, in the proportion of expenditure on capital investme"nt rising to over 25 per cent by 1984-85.
The 1980-81 Budget provided for real growth in defence outlay of about 7 per cent, including a provision towards the purchase of the new tactical fighters. In the event, a number of factors, of which the most substantial was deferral of the decision on the fighter, resulted in a somewhat lower real growth of about 5.6 per cent in' 1980-81. The deferral last year of the decision on the fighter, together with difficulties in progressing other capital equipment projects, will also affect the rate of increase in expenditure in the immediate future. Thus in 1981-82, the Budget provides $4, 112m for defence outlay, a real increase of 5.3 per cent over 1980-81.
However, the obligations already entered into, mainly for capital equipment and facilities, now total $2,580m. These obligations will be further increased by the very large commitments soon to be entered into for the new tactical fighter and for other major equipment projects. Expenditure in the next several years against these obligations will plainly require the allocation of resources to Defence to continue to increase. If I may give but one example, on present projections a little less than $100m will be required for the new fighters in 1981-82. The figure will increase to $400m a year in 1983-84. That is comparable with what we spent in 1978-79 on all capital equipment.
The Government is resolved to sustain the long haul begun in February last year to lift our defence preparedness onto a new plateau. What I have announced represents a significant burden to the Australian taxpayer. I am thoroughly conscious of that fact. Our circumstances and our responsibilities have changed greatly in the course

of the past ten years. We have a clear commitment to fend for ourselves and to contribute to stability in our immediate region. I find myself in complete agreement with that philosophic view which contends that men and women who have a preference for the estate of liberty must carry with grace the burden of protecting it.
