Motion (by Mr Sinclair)-by leave-agreed to:
That so much of the Standing Orders be suspended as would prevent the Leader of the Opposition speaking for a period not exceeding 34 minutes.
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition) (4.41)-After 13 years of hestitation and apprehension, after nearly a decade and a half of timid backtracking and confused decision making, the Government at last has made a decision on the new tactical fighter. After 13 years of florid rhetoric, during which we were assured that there was an imminent threat to the security of this country, we find a decision has been made. Woe befall the country if that is the basis of military decision-making at a time of genuine peril. I am stunned to find that, for all of the rhetoric, for all of the time which has been absorbed in making this statement to the Parliament, so much which is crucially important to a better understanding of our defence preparedness and a better arrangement of our defence capacity has been left unresolved. Effectively, this statement is an FI8 acquisition statement or, if the media are correct, a grand farewell gesture by the Minister for Defence (Mr Killen) before he departs from this country to more salubrious climes. But in the wake he is going to leave the enormous economic burden of meeting the costs of what he has outlined for the rest of the community to bear. It is all very well for him to say, as he did in his concluding peroration:
. . . that men and women who have a preference for the estate of liberty must carry with grace the burden of protectingit.
He could well have deliberated at some length to explain why there have been so many deferrals of crucial decision-making commitments on major capital equipment items for the defence forces so that we have now reached a point where there is a very large bunching up of financial commitments; and they, in turn, will represent a heavy domestic

economic strain and a very serious balance of payments strain in the years ahead.
I said that there are defects in this statement before the House. The Minister has ignored serious defects in the defence arrangements of the country. He has left unanswered major questions which arise merely from the statement he has made to the House, let alone other serious defects of which we are aware in relation to our defence preparedness. For instance, his statement ignores
the fact that the defence system in this country is bewildered, disorganised and has a low operational capacity because of servere restraints which have been imposed upon it by this Minister. We have air crew in the Royal Australian Air Force who spend too much time on the ground.
We have sailors in ships which are harbour-bound and we have an Army which is on short training rations. The consequence of that is extensive frustration, anger and quite noticeable demoralisation within the ranks of the armed Services. Those in the ranks are remarkable people. They forbear with enormous disadvantage unfair burdens imposed upon them by this Minister. But there is a limit. There is a limit to how much confidence, optimism and vitality they can inject into the discharge of their duties in the face of this chronic neglect on the part of the Government.
The defence Services of Australia have been casualties of the grand but empty gesture by the Minister for Defence in the past six years. I will illustrate that. The first five year defence program was outlined in 1976. Four years later it was $1,500m behind on spending target. On capital alone it was $900m behind on the target set. The 1980 program is already $102m behind what was committed less than two years ago. On present indications, and bearing in mind the implications of current economic trends, one anticipates that again promises solemnly made are about to be dishonoured.
Let us look at it another way. The 1976 five year program set a target of defence spending increases in real terms of 5 per cent a year. In fact, the average was much less than 4 per cent. The target to be achieved was 24 per cent of the total defence budget being spent on capital equipment. In fact, it was marginally over 15 per cent. We now have a new five-year defence program. They are recycled every time the previous one is exhausted from neglect, and it is quite obvious that the dishonouring of promises by the Minister for Defence is something that will no longer be forborne by either the community or his backbenchers. We have a new commitment, a 7 per cent increase in real terms in defence expenditure each year of that five-year program. Last year the

2210 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Tactical Fighter Force

increase was much less than 5 per cent and this year it will be about 5.3 per cent. We dispute the figure that the Minister has declared for last year. If it were to be accepted, it would mean very simply, on the Minister's own figures, that the increase in real terms of defence spending this year represents a fall on last year.
The problem with the Government is that it has a preoccupation with big boots that it cannot fill comfortably or adequately. Let me illustrate that with some of the commitments it makes. Let me zero in on the particular case we have before us of the,Fl8 and the unresolved questions associated with it. The Minister for Defence says that the total cost, including spares, training and equipment is $2,430m. That is verifiably a crass underestimation. The United States Secretary of Defense, Mr Carlucci, only a few days ago, said:
Recent pricing and availability data for 75 F / A-IS aircraft developed by the Navy and provided to the Government of Australia include an estimated total program unit cost far in excess of the cost cited by the Australian Press . . . The pricing data provided to the Government of Australia are strictly best estimates.
Guesses are what we are going on, and underestimates at that. I will mention some official sources. The official project unit cost of the F18 to the United States-called the SAR cost-was in December 1980 $24m a plane. That amounts to some $1,800m for 75 planes. One needs to add a minimum of 50 per cent for spares, training and so on. In December 1980 that gave a total cost of $2,700m, which is well above the figure quoted by the Minister, and that was some 12 months ago. The latest United States Navy estimates proposed a cost of $27.7m a plane in 1982, which is a more relevant comparison. Even an assumed inflation rate of only 10 per cent in the United States will represent about $30m a plane for these planes in 1984 when they are declared to commence arriving. If we add on the necessary 50 per cent which I mentioned, in order to get an all-up project cost, we have a total cost not of $2.4 billion but of over $3.3 billion. Now we know what the Minister means when he talks about the fly-away cost.
In addition, we have to take account of the following factors which could add to the $3.3 billion. The net production of a plane is vital to cost. The Marines have chosen some 300 AV8Bs in preference to the F18s. The Canadians are becoming restive about their proposed order. The United States Secretary of the Navy told the National Press Club that if the real increase in price of the F18 continued 'we may be forced to shop elsewhere'. Therefore, the volume of production will fall on that sort of basis. If this occurs, quite obviously it implies a substantial increase in each unit produced because the learning

curve economies are not exploited. Particular specifications that the Government rri~y add will further increase the cost. I repeat: All costs are predicated on the United States inflation rate which has been predicted by the Reagan Administration. The Government Accounting Office of the United States questioned whether the inflation rate, implicit in the pricing mechanisms of the F18, will be met.
If we put all those things together, it is quite obvious that there is only one conclusion: We are not being given all the fads of the real cost of these aircraft. The Minister, on behalf of the Government, has sought to throw dust in' our eyes. The real cost is nearly $1 ,000m greater than the figure he has cited. Later I will come back to the very real and disturbing economic management implications that that portends.
Let us look at the performance of the F18. If the plane did everything originally claimed it would' certainly be an outstanding aircraft, but there are serious uncertainties that it would. It failed to meet its original specifications. Only 12 to 15 aircraft are flying and they are still undergoing evaluation. The FI8 is not operational in any air force. It has not been tested with harpoons which presumably the Government prefers to attach to it. When used in a strike role its air defence capability is degraded because the targeting electronics for the strike role are held in pods which replace its sparrow air to air missiles. Put all these defects together and we almost have a replay of the disaster which was associated with the commitment to a very good aircraft, the Fill, but this is a much more outrageously expensive purchase because the Government did not apply the disciplines that should have been applied right from the start.
I mention in passing-this is relevant to the figures being cited-that there is already a twoyear delay on the stated delivery period given in March 1981. Mr Atwood, a deputy secretary in the Department of Defence, in company with Admiral Sir Anthony Synnot, told the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence that the planes would be delivered between 1985 and 1988. Today the Minister says they will be delivered between late 1984-which for all practical purposes is 1985-and 1990. Already we are running into a delay in delivery. Let us look at one of the alternatives, the F16. I merely mention this, not as any expression of preference, but rather as clear evidence of the lack of disciplined evidence presented to this House to justify this very expensive decision by the Government.

Tactical Fighter Force

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2211

We do not have before us comparative analyses which justify the ruling out of alternative aircraft. I remind honourable members that we are talking about very large slabs of cash which Australian taxpayers will have to meet in the future. We are talking about very large slabs of cash which will go out of this country and which will have to be paid by an extraordinarily resilient and healthy balance of payments in that period. Given the weakening international situation and the weakening of our own, balance of payments account at present which we cannot expect to remain extraordinarily strong in the face of international distress there is every reason for concern about the capacity of the economy to bear this cost and some others which have already been committed either in principle or in fact.
Unlike the F18, the cost of the F16 is known. 'The December 1980 SAR figure was $13.7m which amounts to $1,027m for the project unit cost. The total cost, adding the necessary 50 per cent for an all-up project cost, is some $1,54lm. That is almost half the cost of the F18, not 7 per cent less as the Minister suggests. If we take into account additional cost uncertainties about the Fl8 that do not apply to the F16, the cost could even be less than that half. We could buy something like 150 F16s for the cost of 75 FI8s. In those circumstances, the attrition rate, the loss of single engined F16s as against double engined F18s, becomes somewhat insignificant. The cost of the Multiple Stage Improvement Program for the F16 would give us all that we need. The F16 has some deficiencies in terms of the declared requirements of the defence Services. With the Multiple Stage Improvement Program, we would get all that we need. For instance, it would meet various roles, especially a more effective all weather strike role which has been declared as essential. Its performance is known. It is in service with seven air forces and, contrary to the propaganda, it is not just a simple dog-fighter but has formidable strike capability, attested to in its repeated victories in bombing and strike competitions.
Let us look at the Tornado. My office has received a telex from Panavia in Australia which states:
A package of 75 Tornado aircraft including spares, weapons and ground support equipment would cost in the order of 1508 million Australian dollars based on January 1981 values. A unit fly-away price would be about $15 million as delivered to Royal Airforce.
The operational cost could be increased by the fact that it requires two pilots, but there are strong arguments in favour of two pilots in a multi-role aircraft with its enormously complex

avionics. The fact is that, on the basis of that fiscal evidence, it is still a much cheaper proposition. It is at least technologically as advanced as either the FI8 or the F16. It is rated as probably better. There are two versions of the plane, the strike and the air superiority fighter. There is a 95 per cent commonality between the two versions. It has a short runway capacity and it uses a harpoon equivalent, the Sea Eagle. It has a stand-off defence capability equal to the F 18 and better than the F16 at present, although that will be improved.
On all of these criteria, there are alternatives which could have been seriously considered by the Government and would have been equally as effective. Over all that is the important consideration that we could have saved very large amounts of money as a result of an alternative aircraft being chosen. It may well be, in the light of all the analysis that the Government has carried out, that for all that the FI8 is a preferable buy, but on the basis of what the Minister said in this House today, he has not established that case. He has established an extraordinarily costly undertaking, far in excess of what would be involved in alternatives. There is a lack of fiscal disciplines in respect of that and in the absence of a proper defence of this decision-making of the Government.
I repeat that we have had too many bungles in the past. We seem to have our defence forces operating like motor cars without crankshafts or with three wheels instead of four. I will give honourable members some illustrations. We went through a similar costing farce with the Fill. It was a good aircraft, but I am talking about the fiscal approach of the Government, the conservative Government of that time. The FIll is still incapable of fulfilling its planned role more than 20 years after it was ordered. It still uses outmoded iron bombS. The new avionics Pave Tack system to be attached to it will apparently be used in conjunction with Pave Way bombs, which have a range of less than five miles. This negates any long range stand-off capability for maritime or landstrike. Again, the Pave Tack system is incompatible with the harpoon missile and to fit further avionics to the Fill to handle the harpoon may not be possible. Certainly one suspects, having spoken with people who fly these aircraft, that it will grossly overcrowd the equipment space on the aircraft.
Let me deal with the new frigates. In evidence to the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence on 18 March 1981 Admiral Synnot stated:

2212 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Tactical Fighter Force

From an FFG you can see about 20 miles. If you want to fire something like a harpoon missile over the horizon you have got to know where the target is.
He was talking in terms of the FFGs having helicopters. If the helicopters are not provided on the FFGs, they become extremely vulnerable to attack and much less capable of their offensive role. Turning to the patrol boats, the Fremantle class patrol vessels were' built on specifications which were far too high for the role of fisheries protection. At the moment they are armed with a World War II cannon, the only certainty about which is that it will jam when somebody needs to operate it. No missiles are provided with that vessel. That is an irresponsible approach. I restate what I have said already: There is a serious defective approach to capital equipment acquisition by the Government. It buys large, shiny, speedy, impressive new technology and large, shiny, speedy and highly vulnerable items of capital equipment but it does not buy everything that is needed. So there is a serious distortion, imbalance, defectiveness, in the defence capacity that the Government provides. The Oberon submarines are a classic example of this. We have four and a half crews for six submarines when it was the considered view of Admiral Synnot before the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence of this Parliament that there should be seven crews. I repeat that we are getting the F18, and the purchase has all the hallmarks of a replay of the fiasco of the Fill fiscal undertaking. We do not know the true cost and there are doubts about the real cost capabilities.
Let me move on to the general nature of the defence forces. This is something which should have been considered by the Minister in his address to this Parliament. What are the strategic concepts behind defence thinking? What are the doctrines underlying all of this? It is one thing to buy this expensive equipment, but unless those things are declared cleatiy, comprehensively and practically, the whole thing has very little meaning and can lead to serious distortions in what is provided. In November 1979 the sub-committee of the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence reported:
The Committee is not satisfied that ihe core force, as presently constituted,could expand to its required level within a realistic period of warning should the threat of major direct attack emerge in the future.
Nothing there has been disturbed. If anything, the defectiveness is even more serious. We have had 13 years of rhetoric on a new tactical fighter. If it takes that long for decision-making in relatively sanguine times, one shudders with fear and apprehension at the prospects implicit in conservative

government defence decision-making in times of pressure.
There is a desperate need for a clear declaration of strategic concepts, a clear exposition of the doctrines underlying those strategic concepts. I declare mine quite simply. I believe we start off by asserting that we look after No. I first and work on the worst possible scenario-that, for all the rhetoric of our strong and powerful allies always being there, if we make sure that we are always clinging to their shirt tails when there is a presumed need by them for us, we will be saved and they will turn up when we require them. I argue that that is a very dubious proposition, and I do not have to go as far as America in the instances I can relate to justify that view. I have related historical instances before.
I will use an illustration from East Timor, a country which, within its own region, is regarded as a somewhat large and powerful nation. Weall know of the heroic struggle of the East Timorese people in support of the two commando units which were isolated there in World War II. We know the high price and the high personal sacrifices which were involved. Yet it ha~ been decided that the best interests of those people, as they presumably saw it, was of secondary consideration to the views of this conservative Government. We have heard the outrageous reports of the neglect of the enforced famine of these people. Yet there was not a protest from the Government, in spite of the public outrage. It means, very simply, that in the final wash-up we have to calculate that we might have to rely on ourselves. So we look at the nature of the potential threat in our region and our capacity to handle it, the capacity for that threat to be launched, and the sort of equipment we require to respond to it.
In those circumstances it is sheer nonsense for us to be spending large amounts of money on the odd very large, highly vulnerable, capital unit of equipment such as an aircraft carrier. I will give honourable members an illustration of that. There is a proposal to buy one aircraft carrier. If that aircraft carrier is sunk, we have lost all. We have lost the equivalent of 50 Tornado aircraft, the equivalent of 20 Oberon submarines, the equivalent of four FFG frigates and 20 Orion maritime aircraft. We have 12,000 nautical miles of coastline to be concerned about. One aircraft carrier will go nowhere in looking after that. It took five days for the Melbourne to move from Sydney to Darwin at the time of Cyclone Tracy. What sort of deployment prospects do we have if we invest so heavily in that one highly vulnerable item of capital equipment?

Tactical Fighter Force

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2213

I was aghast to hear the Minister mention that the Invincible from Great Britain is being seriously considered as a contender to fulfil that requirement. The Government blindly ignores all the defects-the vibration problem; the compatability problem in electronics and propulsion; the very serious machinery problems; the lack of space, for instance, for the storage of spares and munitions; and the lack of workshop space for the Sea Harrier aircraft. There are very serious defects in the electronics system and an inadequate command control and communication system to maintain the operation of the Sea Harrier aircraft. It is not really an aircraft carrier anyway; it is a cross between an aircraft carrier and a cruiser. It has been built for North Atlantic service and not for tropical and sub-tropical operations. Effectively, it is an anti-submarine unit. That, in itself, explodes so much of what the Navy has been talking about. The Navy has been arguing that it needs this sort of platform to project aggressive air power to perhaps some land area occupied by an enemy. The Navy would have extremely limited capability in that respect were it to be equipped with Sea Harrier aircraft because of their limited operational range and performance. It would require major alterations to operate in this country. It would make more sense to be spending that money on Orions and submarines. Submarines, in cost effective terms, are overwhelmingly the greatest as~.!t in which we can invest, much more so than a highly vulnerable aircraft carrier.
As I have said, we have a desperate need for a clear declaration of strategic concepts and the underlying doctrines. We need to stress deterrents. We need to develop the theory of disproportionate response to ensure that equipment and manpower are organised on a basis which will ensure the maximum impact in defending the shores of this country. Our arrangements, in terms of the acquisition of capital equipment, need to ensure that that there is mobility and adaptability in those units for this vast and very difficult geographical terrain in which we have to operate. Industrial capacity in this country needs to be maintained at a high level in association with our defence forces so that, in the event that we are by ourselves, we can call on that sort of support with confidence. Therefore, it makes nonsense of the Government's decision to close down the Ordnance Factory at Bendigo, which manufactures general engineering equipment, reconditions guns and gun mountings, .produces consumables such as gun barrels, bomb bodies, ammunition clips and links for the armed services, and undertakes a

wide range of services in support of our defence capacity.
When I talk about a disproportionate response I am talking about something like the capacity that Sweden has established. Despite a fairly small defence arrangement necessitated by the limitations of its population and budget, it is still calculated that it would take something like 70 Soviet divisions to overrun that country. In those terms the cost is so extraordinarily high that it discourages contemplation of undertaking such a venture. Let me cite the case of submarines versus aircraft carriers. I wish to quote from wM.t has been said by no less an authority than Admiral Gorshkov of the Russian Navy. I quote him with some. confidence because in the last couple of years the Minister for Defence has been wont to quote him with great conviction and certainty. Admiral Gorshkov said this of the Second World War:
To everyone German submarine the~e was 25 ships of the Allies and 100 planes . . .
These ships and aircraft were tied up because of the existence of one submarine. In his work 'Sea Power and the State' he concludes:
Of all the armed forces in Germany only the submarine fleet, right up to the end of the war, represented a most serious threat and the 'anti-su.bmarine war' ended only after the territory of Germany had been occupied by Allied armies.
What I am saying boils down to the fact that we would be wiser to buy equipment such as submarines, PC3 Orions, surface vessels of the frigate standard, and strike aircraft, but not necessarily at the most expensive end of the ranges the Government is proposing, and improve the Fills rather than extravagantly splurge on a few major items becau~e we want to impress our strong and powerful allies. I have heard only one persistent argument pressed by the Navy, when its other arguments have been resisted, about the justification of an aircraft carrier; that is, that in operations and exercises at sea the Admiral requires a platform because it is very embarrassing with the United States Navy having something smallet than that. That is a very expensive status symbol. It is scarcely justification for the way in which defence acquisition decisions will be made.
We need a new tactical fighter with reliable known capabilities, not capabilities which are guessed at. We need to know also quite reliably the cost factors that are related to this. I will not go into the details, but the analysis that we have carried out shows simply that the total of projects approved, such as this new tactical fighter, the carriers, the destroyers, the tankers and other items which the Minister has announced in his statement-and others to which he has given no

2214 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

River Murray Waters Agreement

commitment on this occasion-will represent, up until 1990, in current day terms, an expenditure of $10,000m. That $IO,OOOm on these capital items to which the Government has been rather casually committing itself is the equivalent of 16 years' expenditure on equipment in 1981-82 Budget terms. That is a lot. In a matter of less than a decade the Government will be required to meet the equivalent of 16 years' capital expenditure on the external account.
I wish now to concentrate on two items-the F18 and the carrier. Payments for the F18 and the carrier-another $200m a year-will be competing with profit repatriation of more than $2,000m a year in the late 1980s and early 1990s. Repayment of private and public debt overseas will involve annual outflows during this period of $1,OOOm to $1,500m on account of resources investment. It remains to be seen whether the extra export income from these developments will be able to accommodate massive defence-related cash outflows on top of the expressed desire of Government spokesmen to stimulate imports further by a reduction in protection levels.
The evidence is clear. The Government just has not thought its way through the economic disciplines which are explicitly involved in this matter. Our aim should be towards obtaining ships and aircraft with a capacity to attack and to sink aggressors before they get here-to hold and destroy them. We need to maximise the mix and maximise the effectiveness of what we do. In many important respects there has to be a trade-off between quality and quantity. We need more of certain things at the expense of some quality. In other areas we will have to maximise the quality. But we can do much better than we are doing at present. If we persist with these sorts of policies we will be weakened by gaping gaps in the defence arrangements of this country that will worsen over time because of the neglect of the Government and the incomprehension of the Minister.

RIVER MURRAY WATERS AGREEMENT
Ministerial Statement
Mr MALCOLM FRASER (Wannon-Prime Minister)-by leave-I am pleased to inform the House that the Commonwealth, New South Wales, Victoria and South Australia have endorsed a draft of a new River Murray Waters Agreement. I table a copy of the draft. I understand that the Opposition has a copy. It is subject to final revision by the Crown Law officers. I table it on that basis and on that understanding. This Agreement was the outcome of a meeting which

the Minister for National Development and Energy (Senator Carrick) and I attended last Friday in Melbourne, together with the three respective Premiers and their water resources Ministers. The Premiers and I have agreed that the draft new Agreement now be referred, as I have indicated, to the Crown Law advisers of the four governments with a view to the early introduction of the necessary legislation. In the meantime, the River Murray Commission has been authorised to operate fully within the terms of the new Agreement, pending completion of the necessary legislative action.
The draft new Agreement is the result of the first major review of the Agreement signed in 1914. It is now proposed to expand the responsibilities of the River Murray Commission to include water quality in addition to the responsibilities it now has for water quantity in the main stem of the river and associated storages. At the same time, the Agreement is being updated to make it more relevant to present day requirements by giving the River Murray Commission responsibilities in regard to other water management objectives. Under the proposed new Agreement, the River Murray Commission will be authorised to formulate river water quality objectives or standards, as appropriate, for various locations on the river. State authorities will be required to inform the Commission of all proposals under their consideration which could significantly affect water quality and quantity in the river and provide the necessary information to permit an assessment of their likely impact. Obviously, the agreed water quality objectives will be of central relevance to such an assessment. The Commission will be empowered to make representations as necessary to the appropriate State authority on such proposals. Further, the water distribution provisions of the new Agreement will enable account to be taken of the quality of the inflows of tributaries and man-made outfalls in assessing the credits of the States in the Commission's water accounting procedures. Rules are to be drawn up for this purpose.
As in every river system, water quality in the River Murray is the end product of numerous activities which take place in the catchment-in irrigation, dryland agriculture, processing industries, urban centres, forestry and many other activities. These are all State responsibilities. There is no intention of the River Murray Commission having a regulatory function, the power of sanctions in regard to these matters or the right to interfere in the States' affairs. The new arrange-
ments aim at a joint assessment of all relevant water and land use and other factors significantly

River Murray Waters Agreement

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2215

affecting water quality. This is consistent with the development of river management strategies where a river and its catchment fall within the responsibility of a single government. While in this regard the River Murray Commission will have only the power of persuasion, its representations will be based on mutually agreed water quality objectives or standards, and on an ever increasing body of knowledge of the river system and its problems.
Subject to the new water quality provisions, the current water sharing principles and arrangements between the three States will continue. South Australia will be entitled to 1.85 million megalitres annually as measured at the South Australian border in accordance with specified monthly volumes. This includes the recent 23 per cent increase made possible by the completion of Dartmouth Dam. The two upper States will share the balance of the water resources available to the River Murray Commission, but with some flexibility to cope with year to year pressures in water demands.
While the River Murray Waters Agreement is, and will continue to remain, the main policy instrument for the Commonwealth's involvement with the three States in the management of the nation's most important water resource, considerable financial assistance has been provided to the three States for those salinity control and drainage works in the Murray Valley which fall outside the area of the Commission's responsibilities. These are all priority works recommended by consulting engineers, Maunsell and Partners, in the plan of action they proposed in their 1979 report on salinity and drainage problems in the Murray Valley. By the end of this financial year, we will have provided about $18m in grants to the cost of underground and surface saline water drainage works on a dollar for dollar basis with the three States-a total of $36m. Together, we are well advanced in the implementation of urgent works identified in the Maunsell plan of action.
It is the view of this Government that, before any decisions are contemplated regarding a follow-up to the implementation of the plan of action, time must be allowed for the accumulation and assessment of vital information arising from the three .important recent joint CommonwealthStates initiatives. We need to know the effectiveness of the new water quality responsibilities now proposed for the River Murray Commission, the full operational impact of Dartmouth Dam and the efficiency of salinity control projects already completed, or" under construction. Subject to this review, we will give careful consideration

to new salinity control projects in accordance with priorities identified by the States in conjunction with the River Murray Commission. This was done recognising that because of the measures already undertaken the Maunsell report is already to a degree out of date. Basic data for this purpose is already being gathered. The Commission has now established a water quality monitoring network along the river to record salinity lind other key parameters and consultants are being engaged shortly to develop a mathematical computer model to simulate the'operation of the river system, not only in terms of river flows and storage changes, but also in terms of river water quality. This model should be an invaluable instrument in the development and operation of long term strategies to reduce salinity in the river system to manageable proportions, and all efforts are being made to ensure its early completion.
There are already signs that recent initiatives by the four governments are gradually becoming effective. Water quality recordings by the River Murray Commission during last season's severe drought indicated that salinity levels were lower than in previous comparable dry periods. This comparative improvement has been attributed to the initial operations of Dartmouth Dam-now about two-thirds full-and the effectiveness of already completed salinity control works. High river flows resulting from heavy winter rains and melting snow are having a very beneficial flushing
effect on the river system, and adequate water supplies of good quality should be available next season.
It is recognised throughout the world where irrigation has induced salinity problems that techniques which reduce the application of water surplus to plant requirements are essential to the success of any long term strategy to bring the problem under control. Amendments in May and December 1980 to the Income Tax Assessment Act now provide strong incentives to irrigators to improve their watering practices. The new provisions of the Act allow full deductibility of expenditures for income tax assessment purposes on water conservation and conveyance, salinity control and associated drainage works in the same year as they are incurred. We understand that these incentives are now starting to become effective. However, a better definition of the magnitude of the problem is required, and the four governments agreed last Friday that a Commonwealth-States working group should review the extent to which irrigators are adopting improved irrigation practices and assess the possible need for additional incentives. It is clear that without complementary preventative measures at

2216 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

River Murray Waters Agreement

the farm level, initiatives by the four governments in the fight against salinity will be impaired.
The Government is mindful of South Australia's specific problems with River Murray water quality, particularly during prolonged periods of minimum river flow conditions. To some extent, these problems originate outside the State. In relation to drinking water, there is the additional problem of turbidity caused by the presence of suspended soil particles and organic material, which reflect run-off conditions in the vast catchment, including parts of Queensland. Commonwealth financial assistance, already reaching $40m, is enabling South Australia to build a water filtration scheme for metropolitan Adelaide. Two filtration installations are already operational. The scheme, when completed, will contribute significantly to an improvement in the colour and taste of drinking water from the Murray integrated into Adelaide's water supplies.
The River Murray Waters Agreement has worked to the advantage of all three States for over 60 years. Admittedly, managing the river for water quantity is far less demanding than taking account also of water quality, having regard particularly to the constitutional role of the States in practically all aspects of water and land use which affect river water quality. We do not underestimate the difficulties and complex issues which lie ahead. But with good will on the part of all concerned and a determination to make the proposed new River Murray Waters Agreement work, the long term environmental health of the river and the economic well-being of the numerous urban centres and vast irrigation settlements in the Murray Valley can be assured.
The River Murray Commission and the agreement between the States result from the way in which over the years many officials and Ministers have worked together to make sure that that great natural resource is preserved and improved for future years. There are not only many thousands of farmers-many thousands of irrigators-but tens of thousands of people in rural communities, as well as of course the much larger number throughout Australia, who are dependent on the River Murray and its systems. It really is one of Australia's greatest natural resources. In the future it will be used to the best advantage of everyone concerned only while the governments concerned are prepared to work in co-operation, recognising their responsibilities to each other. I pay recognition to the officials of the Commonwealth and the States, the State Ministers and Premiers who helped to bring about this agreement in a way which I believe will be beneficial to

a very large part of Australia. I present the followingpaper:
River Murray Waters Agreement-Copy of draft agreement, dated 16 October 1981, and Ministerial statement, 20 October 1981.
Motion (by Mr Sinclair) proposed:
That the House take note of the papers.
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition) (5.26)-Australia's Federal Constitution has always caused difficulties for the sound and responsible control of the waters of the Murray River. Water management, like the management of other natural resources, is not specifically listed among the areas of Commonwealth responsibility in the Constitution. Whilst that omission has made resource management generally a continuing problem, it has created special problems with the Murray. First, the river forms a boundary between two States; it enters the sea in a third State; and it draws over 20 per cent of its water from a fourth State. Secondly, conservative national governments have used the constitutional omission as a convenient excuse to avoid any substantial responsibility for Australia's primary water resource.
The Commonwealth's involvement began in the interstate royal commission appointed in 1902 to consider the rival claims of the States of Victoria, New South Wales and South Australia to the waters of the Murray. At that time, the primary interest in the river was the need to provide for navigation to facilitate interstate trade. As a result of the royal commission's report, the original River Murray Waters Agreement and the River Murray Commission, formed in 1915, were established to ensure that all steps necessary were taken to regulate the flow of water in the river to provide for navigation, irrigation 'and water supply purposes. But that Agreement no longer serves to protect the river, or indeed to protect the interests of its wide range of users.
Further, the new draft agreement, and the arrangements under which it will operate, while they offer some improvement, are still seriously deficient. In fact, the major innovation in this agreement, the expansion of responsibilities of the River Murray Commission to cover water quality as well as the quantity of water supply, is nothing more than a recognition of what has been happening since mid-1978. In 1973 the Commonwealth established a working party to suggest changes to the functions of the River Murray Commission. In 1975 that working party recommended the expansion of the Commission's role along these lines. Since July 1978 water quality monitoring has been undertaken by the Commission.

River Murray Waters Agreement

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2217

The Opposition welcomes this change, but I point out that in doing so we express our serious concern that monitoring is only a very small part of the task which is necessary to protect the river. Alone, it will not insure that, for example, residents of Adelaide get access to ample supplies of clean drinking water and it will not insure that primary industry in South Australia has available sufficient water to maintain that State's large agricultural output.
The central and most pressing problem with the Murray is salinity, the level of salt in'the water. The World Health Organisation has set a limit of 500 parts per million of total dissolved solids, or salt, in water for human consumption. River Murray water frequently exceeds this level. At Murray Bridge and Mannum, for example, salt levels are frequently above 800 parts per million and, despite assurances in the statement by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser), there is no clear evidence that this situation is improving. Yet, this is the origin of much of Adelaide's drinking water. Consequently, Adelaide's water supply is often saline to the extent of 400 parts per million, compared with Sydney's 90 parts per million and Melbourne's 50 parts per million.
Further, the State's irrigation areas produce over 70 per cent of its grapes, 50 per cent of its stone fruit and almost all its citrus fruit. Citrus trees and vines cannot tolerate more than 600 parts per million salt and stone fruit is even more sensitive. These industries support directly 52,000 people in the Murray Valley in South Australia, where the gross annual value of primary production is over $1 OOm.
As I have already said, under the existing arrangements there have been no significant trends towards improved Murray River water quality. The draft agreement announced today, lacking enforceable safeguards, seems unlikely to lead to desirable and necessary improvements. In the absence of any sanctions against unsound use of the river, in the absence of any regulatory function of the River Murray Commission, the Commission will only have 'the power of persuasion'. When conflicting interests exist between users at different parts of the Murray and in different States-they do now-I suggest that this is an entirely insufficient power.
In this connection also, I remind the House that the River Murray Commission has at present a staff of ten. Staff and members of the Commission already feel themselves under public pressure as a result of the river's inadequate management. Clearly, however, it is the terms of the Commission's operation and the poor level of its

staffing which must be held primarily responsible. Mr T. A. O'Brien, Commonwealth representative on the Commission, pointed out last year that the Commission, with its widened powers and responsibilities, would shortly find itself needing to make difficult decisions. There will be conflicting requirements of quantity vel sus quality of water. There will be conflicting needs for recreation, irrigation and flood mitigation uses and there will be decisions with significant effects across State borders. The firm authority needed to deal with such problems is not provided by the new Agreement.
Finally, I point out that if the Government were serious about a long term commitment not only to protecting the River Murray and its various users' interests, but also to restoring its quality for present and future use, it would support the proposal of the honourable member for Hawker (Mr Jacobi) for the establishment of an institute of fresh water studies. We would move to set up such an institute with great dispatch because of the urgency with which we regard what would be its functions and the need to contribute much more practically and infl uentially towards remedying the problems I have been discussing. There is at present no co-ordinated research of Australia's inland waters.
In passing, I make a point that the first national survey of water use in Australia was based on data collected for the year 1977. I make a further point that many of the mineral development projects now under way or proposed for this decade will depend on the availability of substantial quantities of water. Without adequate information, proper long term planning of our water resource use cannot be undertaken.
In connection with the River Murray the same comment applies. The River Murray Commission does not have a charter to undertake research, despite the extraordinary lack of knowledge of the river's ecology and the effect on that ecology of human demands. Research will not save the Murray, but it is a fundamental need and it is a legitimate interest of a responsible national government. Additionally, opinions exist to indicate that the so-called 'National implied powers' of the Commonwealth would be sufficient to extend the Commonwealth's role into research in that area. As a positive proposal, I commend the institute for the Government's support. It is a matter requiring early decision.
Let me point out the number of other specific problems that need to be attacked and resolved. First, the River Murray Commission has functions limited to the main trunk of the Murray and

2218 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

River Murray Waters Agreement

its tributaries above the Hume. Second, the Commission has no representative from Queensland, despite the fact that that State is a source of over 20 per cent of system's water. Third, the Agreement does not provide for any obligation on the Commonwealth or any of the States to prevent actions which would further damage the river.
In the longer term there is no doubt that the effective management of the River Murray requires Commonwealth constitutional power over its management. I suggest that the most positive thing the Commonwealth could do to protect the different users of the Murray would be to begin the process of seeking Commonwealth control over this national resource. In this respect I point out that the South Australian Government this year has urged the Commonwealth to take full control of the river, regardless of State boundaries. I repeat that the Opposition supports the need to expand the River Murray Commission's role in monitoring River Murray water quality. But I assert, in conclusion, that much more needs to be done by the Commonwealth in the interests of that large proportion of the Australian community and the Australian economy which is dependent upon continued, assured supplies of high quality water from Australia's major river basin.

Mr FRY (Fraser) (5.49)-When the debate on the consideration of the report of Estimates Committee A was adjourned last week I was speaking in support of the amendment moved by my colleague the honourable member for St George (Mr Morrison), particularly in relation to that part of the amendment which supports the resumption of the two aid programs to Vietnam. I was pointing out that the economic deprivation of Vietnam was largely the result of American intervention both in that country and in its interference in the affairs of Kampuchea. Of course, when the Americans completely destroyed the social infrastructure of those countries they then moved out and just left those countries to their own resources. Then, of course, those countries were criticised because they accepted aid from the Soviet Union.

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2221

Australia, because of its participation in the Vietnam war, should accept some responsibility also ror the economic deprivation of Vietnam. I think the greatest way that we could do that would be not only by resuming our aid programs but also by acknowledging the Heng Samrin regime in Kampuchea. The Heng Samrin regime has restored stability to Kampuchea. It has stopped the killing-the mass murders that were going on under Pol Pot. The country is now recovering. It is restructuring its agriculture and its industry. We could playa very significant role in the restructuring of Kampuchea. Instead, we hang off and do not acknowledge the reality of the situation and recognise the Heng Samrin regime.
It is interesting to note that the conflict in Kampuchea that was reported over the weekend did not involve Heng Samrin or the Vietnamese forces in any way. It involved the Pol Pot forces and other forces which are unrepresentative of the people of Kampuchea fighting each other in the north of the country. The threat to the stability of the country is not the Vietnamese presence. Of course, if the Vietnamese were to withdraw then the country would be exposed to further killing and depredation by the Pol Pot forces. So, the quickest way to get Vietnam out of Kampuchea is for those countries which are supporting Pol Pot to stop supporting him. His forces could not survive without support from Thailand and, indirectly, support from America and from the Chinese. Of course, as long as that support is forthcoming those forces pose a threat to the security of the people of Kampuchea and the Vietnamese will stay there.
I think that Australia had a wonderful opportunity to adopt an independent policy in relation to Kampuchea. Normally, this Government follows the policy of the Association of South East Asian Nations but on this particular issue ASEAN is deeply divided. We find that two of its member countries~Malaysia and Indonesiaacknowledge that Kampuchea is a legitimate sphere of influence for the Vietnamese forces. They privately seek accommodation with the Vietnamese forces in Kampuchea. On the other hand, China, Singapore and Thailand still support Pol Pot. Even though those countries have communist insurgents in their own countries being supported by the Chinese they still have this divided policy of supporting Pol Pot at the same
time. I find this quite contradictory but it seems that it is possible to explain it in terms of Asian ideology.
I think that we should be taking an independent viewpoint. There are two countries supporting Pol Pot. There are two countries supporting the

Vietnamese presence. The Philippines seems to be uindecided at this stage. We should be taking the lead in giving an acknowledgement of the Heng Samrin regime. We should discourage other countries from supporting Pol Pot so that the Vietnamese forces can get out and concentrate on reconstruction in their own country. There is no way they will get out while the threat of Pol Pot still remains. I think we should stop burying our
heads in the sand over this issue. We should face up to the reality of the situation and realise that we, too, should be trying to encourage other countries to stop supporting Pol Pot so that the Vietnamese forces can withdraw.
Before I wind up I refer to the fact that during the Estimates Committee hearing I asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) for a report on the situation in East Timor. He promised to supply that report. I have not sighted it as yet. Several weeks have passed. In the meantime we have had several reports from other sources about a very serious situation developing in East Timor, including an internal report from the Administration in East Timor to the Indonesian Government in Jakarta. We have had nothing from our own Government. We have had also reports from other sources. I suggest that the Minister has information about the situation in East Timor. I suggest that it is the sort of information that he ooes not want the Australian people or the Australian Parliament to know about. I call on him to take the Australian people into his confidence and to tell us what the situation is in East Timor. Everybody else knows what the situation is. We do not want a second hand report based on a propaganda statement from the Indonesian Government. If the people of East Timor are in desperate need the Australian peopl~ should be told. I am sure that they would respond most generously. I am sure that the vast majority of people
in Allstralia have a great deal of,sympathy for the East Timorese and do not support the shameful way that we have treated them in the past.

ever before for the democratic countries in the Western alliance to support this new regime in Egypt of President Mubarak.
Let us examine the Camp David Accords and the Camp David process. Two nations that were at war and are now at peace are trying to disengage. Israel is handing back territory to Egypt. I believe that no Arab nation will really protest about Israel handing back Arab territory to an Arab nation. The United States is organising an international force to supervise that peace and those Accords. We are having a debate about whether there will be a Sinai peacekeeping force because at the United Nations Security Council the Soviet Union clearly indicated that it will veto any United Nations force. That is something that this House must bear in mind and remember. We are debating Australia's participation in a Sinai peacekeeping force because the Soviets refused to allow the setting up of a United Nations force. The Soviets do not want peace and stability in the Middle East; we must remember that. The Soviet Union is behind a pact between Libya, South Yemen, Ethiopia which is, in fact, encircling and threatening Egypt. That is another reason for the West, at this time, to support the new regime and government of President Mubarak.
Are we in Australia and in the Western World to throw out the opportunity for peace in that strategic region of the world because the United Nations is unable to send in a force because of a Soviet veto? The answer is no. I believe that the West, and Australia in particular, has to keep an open mind on the subject because if we participated only in peacekeeping forces that were UN-backed the world would be in a worse position. The settlement in Zimbabwe came out of the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting and was a Commonwealth initiative. So, there will be other solutions to peace. We must not throw out a non-United Nations potential solution just because the Soviets veto it.
As events have unfolded it may be more urgent and imperative to support President Mubarak carrying on the legacy of President Sadat and the peace proce-ss. Not to participate at this time may have more repercussions than there were before the tragic assassination. We must, I believe, support the new leadership in Egypt. Not to make a contribution through the Sinai peacekeeping force might, in: fact, encourage radicalism, encourage the Soviets and encourage instability in this region of the world, all of which would please the Soviet Union ..

Mr SHIPTON-Before the suspension of the sitting for dinner I was speaking about the possible participation by Australia in a Sinai peacekeeping force in the Middle East. I said that in my view Australia, Australians and the Australian Government need to keep an open mind on participation in that force. It is my personal view that if the force is broadly based geographically and politically Australia should participate. But of course as honourable members know, the Government has as yet made no decision on that matter. I was saying that as events have unfolded in the Middle East and Egypt since the tragic death of President Anwar Sadat, it may now more than ever before be important and imperative to support a stable regime there. Today there are reports-as yet unconfirmed-from Washington that the French in some way are sympathetic to participating in the force or may participate. But if that is so, it would add a new dimension to the force because we would have European participation in it.
One wonders what the attitude of the Opposition in this place might be to having a socialist government participate in it because the Opposition has clearly come out against Australian participation in a Sinai force. In fact when I look at the Opposition's attitude on international events-it was negative on the Commonwealth Heads of Government and negative on the Sinai peacekeeping force-I can see an isolationist trend there. The Opposition wants to opt Australia out of the real world. If the French were to participate,as I have said, it would take the situation much nearer to meeting the acceptable criteria from the Australian Government's point of view, as stated by the Minister.
I return to the situation in Egypt and the stability of the regime there following the tragic death of President Sadat. President Mubarak has said that he supports and will follow the legacy of Sadat and the Camp David Accords because the Camp David Accords are establishing peace in the area. It is important for the West that we do have peace and that the Sinai area is handed back by Israel to Egypt. It is important that that process of peace continue and that the stability of the Egyptian regime be supported. But at the same time as this is happening-and it was happening before Sadat's assassination-there is an enormous Soviet buildup in the Gulf and Middle East region. I think that ought to be brought to the attention of this House. The recent alliance treaty between Ethiopia, Libya and South Yemen is creating further instability in the region. The

Appropriation Bill (No. /)

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2223

countries have Cuban troops, East German administrators and Russian tanks. In fact an encirclement of Egypt is taking place in a physical and general sense by a Soviet buildup that was occurring before the tragic assassination. So for another reason it is more important for lhe West to make sure there are stable regimes in the Middle East. Let us face it; it is the Soviet Union that, as I have said before, is in favour of instability. It does not want peace; it does not want a resolution of the Palestinian question.
Let us look at what we are talking about in relation to stability in Egypt. We are talking about a stable government that administrates and controls the Suez Canal, that will ensure that there is free passage for the world through that canal and through the Red Sea. Those are the things that are at risk. We must have peace and stability; we must work towards it. That is why I support Australian participation in a Sinai peacekeeping force and why I appeal to Australians to keep an open mind on the issue.

Mr HURFORD (Adelaide) (8.7)-We are discussing a report from Estimates Committee A. I took part in deliberations on the estimates for two departments-the Department of the Parliament and the Department of the Prime Minister and Cabinet. I do not know whether I will have time to talk about the latter, but I will refer to the estimates of the Department of the Parliament and what happened at the Estimates Committee hearing which relate to that Department. I moved a motion, which I will read to the Parliament, as follows:
That this Committee resolves that in appropriation procedures for 1982-83--
The next financial year-
the Funds for Parliament should be contained in a separate single line Appropriation Bill and notes the support For this procedure contained in the recommendations of the report of the Senate Select Committee on the Parliament's Appropriation and Staffing.
I also moved a second motion which was passed by the Committee with support from both sides. It reads:
That this Committee registers its grave disquiet at many of the proposed cuts in real terms in the Budget For the
P~rliament.
I repeat, these motions were supported by members from both sides of the House, indeed by every member of the Committee present except the Leader of the House, the Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair). I assert that there is substantial support on both sides beyond the members of that Committee for the sentiments of those resolutions. I am glad that in reporting from that Committee to this House we do note those resolutions. I trust that this House will note them and that the Executive will go further and act on them. The Speaker, who took part in the deliberations and discussions on the estimates for the Parliament, expressed serious concern that the procedures set down for determining the 1981-82 appropriations had not been followed. Following the theme of the Speaker and of others, I believe that Parliament must not be treated as a prize for winning government. It must not be treated as a creature of the E-xecutive. It must have its independence, and the Speaker of the House of Representatives and the President of the Senate must be included in the deliberations when the appropriations of the Parliament are determined. That did not take place in the deliberations leading up to the Budget for the financial year 1981-82. That caused the grave concern which is

2224 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

expressed in the first of the two resolutions which I have read to this chamber.
Savage cuts have also been made to the appropriations for the Parliamentary Library in particular. These cuts have absolutely nothing to do with the fact that provision is being made for a new Parliament House. The cuts are to the dayto-day running expenses-the photocopying and so on-of the Library. Cuts to other areas are less severe but will compound an already serious situation in those particular branches of the Parliament. I want the House to note the most recent report of the Joint Parliamentary Committee of Public Accounts which points to the understaffing of the Joint House Department and to the fact that this is seriously affecting the quality of that Department's work.
I will talk in more detail on the points I have made. The work of all members of parliament is dependent to a large extent on the standard of assistance given, in particular by Library staff. I illustrate this by pointing to the fact that opposition in this Parliament to the Library, or parts of the Library, being moved out of the building is very strong. One member of the Estimates Committee A, the honourable member for North Sydney (Mr Spender), a Liberal member from the other side of the chamber, pointed out that he was strongly against the cuts to the Library appropriation. During the hearings of Estimates Committee A, Mr Speaker said:
I am interested to learn that Mr Spender--
That is, the honourable member for North Sydney-
is prepared to put up with the room he has because that is certainly not the indication I had.
The response of the honourable member for North Sydney to that, and he shares a room, was:
Il is a lousy room, but if it is a choice between the smaller facilities in the Parliamentary Library--
I interpose that the smaller facilities result from the lesser appropriation for the Library-
and putting up with a lousy room I will put up with a lousy room.
I have quoted that as an illustration of a bipartisan approach which is being taken in opposition to the Executive's cuts in the appropriation for Library facilities in particular. Although we can do little to improve the building, much could be done to improve facilities available to members of parliament. Members of the Parliament do not have a good reputation in the community. We have to face that fact whether we like it or not and whether it is deserved on not, but there might be general support for lifting the standards of members of parliament. Therefore, if people are

consistent there would be general support for lifting the facilities made available to members of parliament to do what is a fairly difficult task in this vast country considering the distances we have to travel and the hours we have to keep.
I am not just referring to additional staff for shadow Ministers. The way the alternative government's shadow Ministry has been kept short of staff is a scandal. There is not a ministerial officer for each of the 22 members of the shadow Ministry. I am referring. to simple things such as vocadex machines, photocopying machines, modern furniture and adequate telephones. The improvement of these services would be a great step forward in providing improved conditions, thereby fesulting in improved services given by members of parliament to their constituents and to the nation. Why should Library staff be rehoused elsewhere than in this building where they are available to members while other departments, which are of lesser importance to members in helping them to carry out their duties, are housed here, and the intention is to keep them here? There is not a good answer to that question, and I bring that to the attention of this chamber right now when it is reporting on what happened in Estimates Committee A. Indeed that was the theme of the discussions in that committee of which you, Mr Deputy Speaker, were Chairman and know that what I say is correct.
It cannot be seriously suggested that the provision of a separate appropriation Bill for Parliament is the important factor. The important fact to which we must draw attention at this time when discussing money made available to the Parliament is that the officers of the Parliament, the Speaker of the House of Representatives and the President of the Senate and their advisers should be included in the deliberations on the sum to be made available. Control of staffing need not be dissimilar to that provided under the Public Service Act. Already staff on the Opposition side have slightly different obligations under that Act. The pay scale for Opposition staff bears no relation to equivalent pay scales in the Commonwealth Public Service in terms of duties done and responsibilities taken.
I will list some of the worst exampIes of the cuts that have been made. The overall cut to the Library in real terms is the equivalent of 22 per cent. This includes specific money cuts for books of 46 per cent, for photocopying and stationery of 43 per cent and for microfiling services of 63 per cent. Because of new staff ceilings the 2 per cent reduction in the Library's permanent staff ceiling has applied and the cuts to temporary positions are to be met by 30 June 1982. This compares

with the target date for razor gang cuts of June 1983. The Library and the whole of the Parliament is suffering.
