Dr THEOPHANOUS (Burke) (8.17)-1 want to make some remarks about the fact that the estimates for the Department of Foreign Affairs seem to bear little or no relation to the priorities of foreign policy. Of course, it is true that foreign policy needs to be flexible and to change with the times. But the Department of Foreign Affairs seems to be extremely slow to change its arrangements in response to shifts in policy. We have heard a great deal from the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) about the NorthSouth dialogue. We have been told about his concern for the underprivileged nations of the world. Yet this is not reflected in the Budget Estimates.
Firstly, for a nation pretending to be doing something to assist poor nations; our foreign aid budget is ridiculously low. We announced an increase of $lOOm this year. But we are far away from the United Nations target of 0.07 per cent of gross national product. This alone exposes the hypocritical attitude of the Government in pretending concern for poor nations. It is easy to talk in abstract terms about assisting those poor nations but it is another matter to develop concrete policies. The first and major concrete need is for direct aid. We must ensure that that aid goes to assist the people of those nations to help themselves. Anstralia can do a great deal in this matter; it is not doing enough. Secondly, the very organisation of our embassies and high commissions reflects what little concern we really have for Third World nations. The organisation is an anachronism based on structures which have been built up over decades and which have not been revised in the light of policy.
Thus, on the one hand, according to the estimates 557 people are employed at the Australian High Commission in Britain. On the other hand, in our six missions in black Africa-an area with which the Prime Minister is supposedly very concerned-there are only 88 employees. All the overseas posts in that underprivileged region are in Commonwealth countries. The other nations in that area do not have a single Australian embassy. How can we claim that we are trying to assist these poor nations if we spend most of the money put aside for overseas posts on missions such as those in Britain and in the United States of America, where there are 345 employees in five missions. I ask: How can the Government carry

out the kind of foreign policy required for a genuine North-South dialogue, which it has been advertising to the world, if it bloats its staff in these countries? One wonders what many of them do there. What do the 557 people in the British Embassy do? On the other hand, in many poor countries of the world we have no representation at all.
Furthermore, the contradictions do not stop
there. If one looks at Latin America, one sees an interesting situation. Of the six nations where we have missions, the two which have the most staff are Argentina and Chile. These two nations are well known internationally for their use of horrible torture and the tactics of intimidation to impose a fascist military rule over their bwn people. Earlier today we had the shocking disgrace of this country's Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) attempting to justify the outrageous regime in Chile of Pinochet, whose hands are stained with the blood of hundreds of thousands of people, including the last democratically elected President of that nation, the great Salvadote Allende. While the name of Allende will burn like a torch forgenerations to inspire the people of Latin America towards democracy, our Deputy Prime Minister stands condemned for his love of fascist regimes.
To return to the point, what is the justification for the maldistribution of embassies in Latin America, where the most reactionary have the most staff and countries such as Cuba, Nicaragua and EI Salvador have no embassies at all? It might
be said that we are trying to put pressure on these reactionary regimes to moderate their policies, to release political prisoners, and to distribute wealth more equally amongst the people. But we are not doing that at all. On the contrary, after making inquiries of the Departments of Foreign Affairs and Defence regarding Latin American political prisoners, I was informed that our embassies did not have figures available. They did not even know how many political prisoners there were in the various countries, never mind making any attempt to assist those people, including attempts, with which I am concerned, to bring some of them out of the barbarous situation in which they live and give them a chance to live in another country such as Australia. If that is so, one asks: What are the tasks of these embassies? As far as I am concerned, unless the Government is pressing to achieve reforms in Chile and other such nations, we should close down our embassy in that country as a sign of protest against the continuing denial of human rights and open up missions in more enlightened nations in Latin America.
There is a further element in the estimates re-
lating to this matter which I believe pinpoints the

2226 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

muddle-headed nature of the current administration of the Department. Let us consider for a moment the staffing of our embassies in the superpower nations. We have 38 employees in China, 557 in Britain and 28 in the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics. It appears that on this Government's priorities we employ more people in the Netherlands than we do in Russia, and more than twice as many people in Malaysia than in China. This Government claims some considerable concern about the state of tension between the super-powers, the avoidance of war, and the attempt to bring about some of kind of dialogue between the super-powers on a whole host of pressing international matters. How is that to be achieved, given the situation of employment and the Estimates as we see them in this Budget? In my view, we have a situation where we do not even have enough staff in China and the Soviet Union to give a substantial analysis of what is actually happening and to give us an idea of how we might act in an independent way to improve our relationships with those nations.
I believe that the facts I have put before the House indicate a pressing need for a further look at the organisation of the Department of Foreign Affairs in relation to its overseas posts and at a better distribution of those posts with respect to what we are trying to achieve in foreign policy. Unles's we actually organise the Department along those lines we will not be in a position to achieve the kinds of foreign policy goals which even the Government claims it is trying to achieve. I emphasise again the importance of more embassies, more staff and greater knowledge in the Third World countries so that we can assist them in those places. Let us completely overcome the situat.ion we have at the moment where certain diplomatic posts and diplomatic missions are no more than c.ushy places where politicians can be sent. We need a situation where the organisation of the Department is brought more into line with the needs of our foreign poli~y.
Mr CAMPBELL (Kalgoorlie) (8.27)-In speaking to the report of Estimates Committee A, I would like to observe that the 1981-82 Budget has created more discord and unhappiness around the community than any other Budget) can remember. In this controversy very little attention ha:s been paid to one aspect of the Budget that will define to a considerable extent if, and how, we can secure ourselves in a world that is becoming increasingly more insecure. I refer to defence and the way it has been abused by the erratic behaviour of this Government.

In 1976 the Government came out with what at the time sounded like the very last word in fiV,ec year defence plans. In that plan the Government committed itself to an equipment purchasing program that necessitated real annual increases in expenditure of 5 per cent. In the period between then and 1979-80, the increases averaged less than 4 per cent each year. As it happened, that year-1980-8l-in a neurotic reaction to the Soviet invasion of Afghanistan, the air was let out. The first five-year defence plan was deleted and another five-year program was pumped up. Defence spending was supposed to rise by 7 per cent in real terms. In the first Budget of the new plan--- the 1980-81 Budget-defence expenditure rose by 4.8 per cent. In the next Budget, this Budget, in fact it is expected to rise by 5.3 per cent. Already the procrastination that ruined the first five-year defence plan has begun to ruin the second. The procrastination that disfigures out defence procurement programs will put an enormous, perhaps even impossible, strain on our economic resources.
To meet defence equipment commitments that
are firm or reasonably firm, the Government will be facing a bill of more than $5 billion by the end of this financial year. When proposed purchases are added we will face a bill of $10 billion by the end of this decade. At the present rate of defence spending, it will take us 16 years to pay off this debt. We will be trying to pay 16 years bills in nine years expenditure. It is appreciated that defence these days does not come cheap, especially in a country such as Australia with its logistic, topographical, geographical and other complications. But we are entitled to expect that the vast amounts of money we provide through our taxes-remember that this Budget allocates more than $4 billion-are spent carefully and wisely. That is demonstrably not the case. Two years ago the all-party Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence trenchantly criticised our defence procurement practices in a report to this Parliament.
The recent fiasco over the new Defence Integrated Secure Communications NetworkDISCON-illustrates how our purchasing system can lead to expensive mistakes that are not only stupid but dangerous for the defence of Australia. New tenders have had to be called, only 18 months after the initial contract was let. The cost of this large project, originally estimated at $150m to $200m, may now be much higher. The purchasing authorities must have been blind, deaf and idle not to have known that the proposed Univac system was still full of problems. There will be a dangerous gap in Australia's defence at a time

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2227

when new systems, such as a new tactical fighter, will be coming on stream and will require a much more sophisticated and reliable communications network.
I will give the House an example of another fiasco that has not been discussed to any great extent. It concerns the FIll, the aircraft which was chosen in such emotional circumstances in the late 1960s. The FIll has never been free of criticism. Its costs steadily inflated. The production model sold to Australia was never equal to the promises made for its speed and range. Much more serious, however, is that it is now clear that the FIll in service with the Royal Australian Air Force cannot perform the role designed for it. The wings of an FIll with a full load of conventional or iron bombs cannot be swept, thus reducing the plane's performance. Performance is further reduced by the drag effect of the conventional payload. In this condition, therefore, the FIll, with a conventional payload, is no more than just another subsonic bomb truck.
The avionics systems fitted to the FIll may be adequate for several other roles but they are incompatible with most modern air to ground missiles. The only version of these so-called smart weapons possessed by the RAAF is the Pave Way system. The problem is that its guidance relies on the target being illuminated by a laser beam directed by ground forces or from another aircraft, restricting once more the situations in which the weapon can be used. This problem it is hoped will be. overcome by integrating the Pave Tack system with the FllI. Pave Tack is, however, pointless with the Pave Way bomb, which has only the most limited range. Cost apart, the selection of Pave Tack as the way to modernise the Fill also has serious shortcomings. Its United States of America Air Force role envisages it as a short range, high speed, low level penetration weapon, which contradicts, one would have thought, the stand-off role planned for the plane in the Australian scheme of things.
Another potential problem is that, for various technical reasons, Pave Tack is incompatible with the Harpoon air to surface missile, the most likely candidate for the RAAF's smart weapon. In other words, for the foreseeable future, the FIll's present ability to fulfil its long range stand-off role is debatable.
Why do we have this confusion in our defence programs? Why should taxpayers, who are providing more than $4 billion this year on defence, not demand that defence policy and administration be administered with the same cold-eyed objectivity with which the government closes down

colleges of advanced education and child care programs?
The defence farce is not the responsibility of the dedicated men and women of our Services. The blame must be laid squarely on the shoulders of the Government. The duty of a government is to provide clear and achievable guidelines for the defence of Australia. This Government has not done so.
Driven by the mania of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) to play a world role, the Government swings wildly between outmoded concepts of forward defence and the compelling logic of the defence of Australia. We must devise strategies and purchase equipment which can deter and, if necessary, defeat any enemy seeking to attack Australian territory. A solitary vulnerable aircraft carrier largely limited to an antisubmarine role at extraordinary expense cannot do this. Half-baked weapons and avionics for the FIll cannot do this. A creaking and disasterprone purchasing system cannot do this. We need many submarines, many maritime strike aircraft, frigates and patrol boats with effective stand-off capacity. We need new doctrines for our land forces, including both the Army and the Air Force.
It is not the task of government to provide a status symbol for admirals, or to make a laughably ineffective attempt to play with the big boys. As the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) has stated repeatedly, the Australian Labor Party will play its role in a stable -system of nuclear deterrence by accepting United States facilities in Australia as long as Australian sovereignty is not impaired. But short of a nuclear challenge, our first, second, and third priorities must be the defence of Australia.
Mr YOUNG (Port Adelaide) (8.35)-1 will not keep the House very long. I wish to refer to a couple of matters which concern the relationship that Australia has with some other countries and the importance of looking at our standards. I was quite amazed today at the outburst of the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony). He said that, as a result of the protest that people had held in Chile some time ago, he felt that perhaps the Chilean economy had picked up and people werenow better off in that country. The point that was missed by the Deputy Prime Minister-which I hope was not missed by the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street)-is that at the time he was in Chile at least people were able and had the right to demonstrate against the Government, which" is not a right that they have today. The Deputy Prime Minister should be aware also that some of the

2228 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Appropriation Bill (No, 1)

standards and procedures which have been
abolished in Chile are taken for granted as a democratic right in this country. If we believe it is important for us to resist any dwindling of our
rights in Australia we should apply the same types of standards to those other countries.
Of course, Chile has a very brutal military regime. Had I been given the opportunity after Question Time this afternoon I would have reminded the Deputy Prime Minister that Amnesty International is investigating the disappearance and suspected murder of several of the leading Chilean parliamentarians since the Pinochet coup some years ago. Other international organisations are very concerned about the lives of many people in that country. It is true also that over 2,000 Chileans have made their way to Australia as a result of the political persecution that took place in that country following the overthrow of the Allende Government in that country.
We should not give the impression-especially leading people in public life in this country-that we are in any way impressed by the way in which countries such as Chile may overcome their economic problems. Very serious doubts surround Chile's economy as well, which is not an argument which I want to get into. But I think it is important. I sincerely hope that the Minister for Foreign Affairs is never attracted to the crass ideology put forward by the National Country Party on questions such as this. If the standards spoken of so glowingly by the Deputy Prime Minister appeal to him perhaps he would like to see them applied in Australia and to do away with the rights of people to demonstrate against governments or to protest about government actions. Perhaps people, on reading what the Deputy Prime Minister has had to say, would get the wrong idea. I hope that people outside who are listening to the Parliament or the people who may read about what the' Deputy Prime Minister had to say about Chile in this Parliament today will not for one instant think that what he said represents the views of anybody else in this Parliament. I hope that when the Minister replies to the debate on the Estimates he can say something about the Government's official attitude to what is going on in Chile.
The other matter to which I wish to refer is the recent visit to' Japan of the spokesman for the Palestine Liberation Organisation, Yasser Arafat. For a number of years this country has had the policy that until the PLO recognises the State of Israel we will not talk to the PLO. That is what the Minister for Foreign Affairs said in an answer in this House last week. There should be greater debate on that issue in the Parliament of Australia. Reading the briefing of the meetings

that took place between Arafat and the Japanese Prime Minister, Mr Suzuki, and Mr Sonoda leaves no doubt that the views they presented to him are much the same as those that would be presented by anybody in this House, be he a Government or Opposition member. First and foremost we believe that there has to be recognition of the State of Israel. There can be no bargaining or compromising on the attitude that Australia has adopted on that issue. That is a view that has been put to the PLO representative by the Japanese Government. But the Japanese Government thought it important enough to speak to Arafat to put those views and to put also the views in which it was interested to assist in bringing about peace in the Middle East. Australia may be better off taking the line taken by the Japanese and many other countries in talking to PLO representatives, to say, firstly, that Australia recognises the State of Israel but, secondly, that we would like to assist in the establishment of peace in the Middle East. It seems to me that at least we would have a greater influence than we have at the moment by t,urning our back on those representatives and saying that we will not speak to them at all. That is the view that has not been expounded by many countries today. Most countries ate talking to the PLO. Most countries are trying to have some influence on its view about the State of Israel and in relation to the forming of a homeland for the Palestinian people on the West Bank. The difficulty about formulating another view is that these matters are kept within the confines of the Executive of Government. They are discussed at the Cabinet level but they are never brought into this place for open discussion so that honourable members can put their views about what is occurring in the world. I suspect that that is another matter that ought to be brought into the Parliament.
The third and final matter that I wish to raise is the question of EI Salvador. I raise it because it
seems to me to be a classic example of where Australia may get itself into trouble by having the policy of following the United States Administration irrespective of who the American people elect to be President of that country. For some time it may have been a policy that suited Australia because there was some stability in the presidencies of the United States but at the moment it seems as though the American people will change Presidents at every election. It is true to say that an accurate description of President Reagan is that he is an extremely conservative right wing President of the United States. He seems to be isolating the United States more and more in his policy issues. Except in the case of

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2229

South Africa, the Government seems to be quite happy to go along with whatever the President of the United States does. I suspect that in doing so this Government is t~king us into the isolation in which the Americans now find themselves, whether it be on the question of South Africa, the question of the Middle East or the question of El Salvador.
On the issue of El Salvador, the United States Administration is way out on its own. It seemed to me on a visit to the United States just a few weeks ago that that is not the view of many of the Congressmen or many of the people in the United States, who are very upset about the attitude adopted by the United States. It seems to me that Australia would be much better off supporting the initiatives of France and Mexico in calling for a political settlement in El Salvador and a recognition of human rights in that country. We would be doing ourselves far more good by taking those initiatives and joining with those people than by saying blindly the we are part of the American alliance and that, whoever the American people elect as President, we will go along with United States policy. Obviously, the Minister will deny that that is what we do, but that is obviously how it is interpreted by the people who observe the statements and actions of the Australian Government.
I think those matters are enormously important. The issue of El Salvador is one upon which Australia should have an independent view. In my opinion, that independent view should be more closely allied with the initiatives of France and Mexico. Secondly, there ought to be a debate in Parliament, if we are to have some influence in the Middle East, about how we are to use new initiatives instead of old ideas. Thirdly, I hope that the Minister for Foreign Affairs will dissociate this Parliament from the grubby remarks made by the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) about how good things are in Chile because that is not the sort of thing that we on this side of the House wish to be associated with.
Mr STREET (Corangamite-Minister for Foreign Affairs) (8.44)-1 wish to confine my remarks to the result of the examination by Estimates Committee A of Appropriation Bill (No. I) 1981-82 in which three resolutions or expressions of opinion were agreed to by the Committee on the proposed expenditure relating to the Department of Foreign Affairs. As a result the honourable member JOr St George (Mr Morrison) has proposed the following amendment:
Omit the words 'the expressions of opinion agreed to by Committee A in relation to the Department of Foreign Affairs

be noted', substitute the following words: 'this House fully endorses the expressions of opinion agreed to by Committee A in relation to the Department of Foreign Affairs and requests appropriate action by the Government.
On behalf of the Government I oppose the amendment. The reasons for the Government's opposition are as follows. The Committee expressed its concern over the reductions in allocations to the Australian Institute of International Affairs and the United Nations Association of Australia and requested that the amounts provided by institutions, including the AustraliaBritain Society, be reviewed. During my appearance before the Committee I opposed this motion on the grounds that the Government's decision in this regard was made in the Budget context to achieve its overall objective. The Government believes that the estimates as presented to the Committee achieve this objective. It should not be inferred that this decision in any way detracts from the Government's appreciation of the very valuable contributions made by both the Australian Institute of International Affairs and the United Nations Association of Australia. in the area of foreign policy concerns. To suggest otherwise would represent a deliberate attempt to mislead this House.
The Government's decision to make an annual grant to the Australia-Britain Society was announced by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) in the House on 26 August 1980 and the first grant was subsequently made in the financial year 1980-8l. The grant is closely linked to the Government's decision, announced simultaneously by the Prime Minister, to provide $160,000 per annum to cover salary and travel expenses associated with the Centre for Australian Studies which is to be set up within the institute of Commonwealth Studies of the University of London. To function with full efficiency the Centre needs an effective secretariat in Australia and the grant to the Australia-Britain Society is to enable it to establish and maintain such a secretariat. The objective of both contributions is to strengthen the relationship between Australia and Britain at a time when British attention has focused increasingly on Europe.
The second part of the amendment relates to the view expressed by the Committee that the Government should accept and work towards meeting the United Nations target of 0.7 per cent of gross national product as official development assistance. The Government already endorses the

2230 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

0.7 per cent of GNP target and ~or its part will en-
deavour to maintain its official development as-
sistance at the highest level consistent with the needs of developing countries and Australia's
economic and budgetary circumstances. As a consequence there would seem to be little purpose
in pursuing this matter further in the manner
proposed by the honourable member for St George.

The Committee further expressed the opinion
that the Government should consider the resump-
tion of two suspended aid projects to Vietnam as a contribution to the stability of the region.
Australia decided to suspend its bilateral aid to Vietnam in January 1979 following the Vietnamese invasion of Kampuchea. The Govern-
ment's action was taken to express its deep con-
cern at Vietnam's policies which were contrary to internationally accepted principles and to the det-
riment of the interests of neighbouring countries.
The question of Australian aid to Vietnam has been reviewed but it has been concluded that it
would not be appropriate for Australia to resume it at this time. The Government believes that the
reasons for the suspension of aid continue to be
valid. We are not prepared to consider a resump-
tion of aid while Vietnam's policies on Kampuchea remain unchanged.

That the words proposed to be omitted (Mr Morrison's amendment) stand part of the question.

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Armitage)- The question before the House is:
That the following proposed expenditures be agreed to:

De partment of Health Proposed expenditure, $471,875,000

Proposed expenditure, $130,973,000
Mr KENT (Hotham) (9.2)-1 do not wish to take up the time of the House unnecessarily, especially as I was a member of the committee dealing with the estimates for the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs. I had the opportunity to voice my criticism, in particular of the

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2231

129.1 per cent increase in the estimate for deportation. This amount was appropriated to deport some over-stayed visitors or a Greek or Polish sailor who jumps ship. At the same time we accept boatloads of people who have jumped the queue. I do not wish to be misunderstood. I have nothing against genuine refugees, people who come from South East Asia and seek refuge here becau~e they feel threatened in their own countries. I think it is our humanitarian duty to accept a number of those people. At the same time, we will be using some of the estimate of $1.69Im to deport Chilean seamen who jump ship because they feel threatened in Chile, and for good reason. There is a facist regime in Chile which is murdering and imprisoning innocent people without trial, including members of parliament. I think that the Government,by such actions, is showing its hypocrisy towards refugees.
However, there is another matter which I bring to the attention of the House today. It is a matter which does not show up directly in the Estimates, such as the appropriation of $1.69Im for deportations. I refer to the general racist attitude governing our immigration policies and practices. At a very young age I came face to face with 'the worst manifestation of racism in human history. I lived through the occupation of my native country by Hitler's Germany and I have seen innocent men, women and children put to death because of their race or'religion. Is it any wonder that I abhor racism in whatever form it is manifested, even in a milder form such as migrant selection in which racial bias plays a part?
In all fairness I have to state that both major parties have been guilty of supporting racist immigration policies and practices. We in the Australian Labor Party have a long history of it, too. The stated aim of the ALP's immigration policy of 1904 was 'to maintain racial purity'. That was later changed to the 'white Australia policy'. It was altered to its present form, which finally eliminates racial bias from our policy. I do not pride myself on being an expert on the Liberal Party's
immigration policy, but I can state without equivocation that the policy the Government administers at present under the Numerical Multifactor Assessment System has inbuilt racial bias. If one adds to this the racist zeal of many immigration officials and the deliberate understaffing of our posts in some countries, one will understand the disadvantage suffered by people from non-English speaking backgrounds or non Anglo Saxon origins.
NUMAS, which served as a thin veneer or a camouflage for our racist migrant selection policy, has been under review because its deficiencies

could not be hidden any longer. It was used as a justification for the rejection of many people who could have been highly successful settlers. It is based on the assumption that educational, economic or occupatiorial characteristics are the most important qualifications for successful settlement. Although these factors are important, it is doubtful that simply having a higher occupational background, more money or a better education automatically means that one would be able to settle more successfully in the Australian community. Kinship, the age at which an immigrant arrives in Australia, and the number of relatives already living in this country are far more important factors
in successful settlement. The present system of selection does not consider family ties as an important factor in migrant selection. Integration into a country is a complex problem. It varies with each group and individual. I believe'that it is impossible to encompass all factors contributing' to
successful settlement in a numerical system, as NUMAS attempted to do.
In devising a successful immigration policy we have two alternatives. On the one hand, because of racist reasons, we can continue to prevent certain people from certain countries coming to Australia or, on the other hand, we can introduce a much more flexible policy which would not, a priori, state that certain. people will make good settlers while others will not. Under this Government's policy we are accepting only 18 per cent of the total intake under the family reunion scheme. From the previous statements of the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs (Mr Macphee) it is obvious to me that this will not be altered significantly under the new policy to be announced later this year. Instead of family reunion, the main emphasis in the new policy will be placed by the Minister on acquiring migrants on the basis of
their trades or skills. In other words, he still looks on migrants as fodder for industry.
There is no valid reason why we should not train our own people, our own youth, in skills which Australia will need in future and base our immigration policy mainly on family reunion. To base a migrant intake on the needs of one section of our community-the needs of industry only-is a short-sighted policy. The Minister is proposing to bring in migrants with certain skills, while tens of thousands of our youth are unemployed and tens of thousands more will leave school in a month or two and face the same fate. The Government's immigration policy will not only prevent many of our young people getting jobs and training in our industries,' it will also cause
long term chronic unemployment in our community. I know that Government members will

2232 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

argue that if we bring out migrants under the family reunion scheme, more people will be competing on the job market. Let me tell them that there will certainly be added competition for jobs in times of heavy unemployment, whether migrants are allowed in on the basis of family reunion or on the basis of skill. The only remedy to the problem is to reduce the total intake to a figure our industry can absorb to the number Australia can absorb without throwing our youth on the scrap heap of unemployment.
There is another side to the Government's short-sighted policy of bringing out migrants with skills required by industry. Those welders, electricians or computer experts we invite as migrants today will also wish to bring out their families tomorrow. As a member of parliament I have witnessed the frustration and heartbreak of many migrant couples whose families and relatives are kept out of Australia by this Government. I call on the Minister to adopt a more humane approach to this grave problem. I believe that we need an immigration policy which. places absolute precedence on family reunion. Within the family reunion category precedence could perhaps be given to younger persons possessing desirable skills. Employers recruiting skilled immigrants from overseas should be able to do so, provided that they pay all expenses, including transport to Australia, housing, provision of schools, et cetera. Applications for migration to Australia which were not considered during one year should be automatically reconsidered the next year. The family reunion category should be extended to include, as well as dependent relatives, unmarried children, brothers, sisters, or other close relatives. Our immigration policies should be based on our needs and our humanitarian obligations and they should be administered' with compassion and understanding.
Mr COWAN (Lyne) (9.11 )-1 briefly refer to payments by the Department of Veterans' Affairs. For some time now I have been making representations to the Minister for Veterans' Affairs (Senator Messner) concerning the payment of pensions on a monthly or a fortnightly basis into a recipient's banking account. At present such payments are made at the request of the recipient, but there is a delay of three months. Many of the recipients of Veterans' Affairs pensions would like payments to be made into their banking accounts, but the Minister refuses to do that. It might cost more but it would give a service to people. Many of the recipients want to leave their home for a month or more. At present they are dependant on their neighbour or a member of

the family attending their mail box to collect the cheque.
Surely, we can give this servic,e to people. These are men and women who have served their country over the years. I am informed that the Department of Social Security will meet the requests of their recipients in this respect but, the Department of Veterans' Affairs will not. I strongly urge the Minister and the Department to consider what can be done to meet the demands of these people. This is the computer age, as we know. Surely, arrangements can be made to meet the requests of the people concerned.
Mr YOUNG (Port Adelaide) (9.12)-It is not my intention to take too much of the time of the House. I know that there are many people on our side who wish to speak. I take up from the point where the honourable member for Hotham (Mr Kent) left off. I have raised this question with the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs (Mr Macphee). Very shortly, he will make an announcement to the House about changes in the Numerical Multifactor Assessment System. He is aware, as is everybody else, that there are alle-
gations that NUMAS discriminated against some people who wanted to come to settle in Australia. I hope that the Government will make arrangements so that, when the Minister makes that statement to the House, time is made available for more than one member a side to speak on this matter. People who look at the statement and judge it against what has been in existence in Australia over the years can then make some con-
tribution to the Parliament. I very strongly believe that the only way policies on immigration and refugee programs can work successfully is, firstly, through the general agreement of the Parliament and, secondly, with the confidence of the Australian people. To that extent I think that over the years, to a very large degree, members of parliament have been ignored.
I put to the Minister tonight for his consideration what I believe to be a very important step that the Government ought to take on behalf of the Parliament. When I was overseas recently visiting Washington, Madrid and Rome I talked to people from the immigration programs of those countries. I spoke particularly to people from the United States of America. I had lunch with sev-
eral members of the United States Congress, all of whom were part of the congressional committees which had travelled to the problem areas of refugees-to central America and to Indo-China. They had been in our region as members of their own congressional committees looking at the problems of refugees, trying to define what policy the American Administration ought to follow. I

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2233

must say, as I said this afternoon, that many of those people went home convinced that the policy being adopted by the American Administration was not the policy that they themselves favoured.
I do not want to belittle or denigrate the role of public servants in this field. Immigration seems to me to be a far more important issue than one which involves sending public servants on an errand to try to sort out what ought to be the policy adopted by this Parliament. We have had a very vigorous migration program operating in
Australia for a long time. Many different decisions have to be made from here on. The last five years have demonstrated the sort of massive changes in Australian attitudes that will have to be adopted. It seems to me that, in those circumstances, there is great pressure for us to have a look at the mechanisms that are used to adopt these policies.
I think it would be of great advantage to the Australian Parliament to have a committee on immigration and refugee programs so that parliamentarians could visit and talk to the people involved, particularly the professional people from the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees. We are not just talking about our region; we are talking about 10 million refugees spread all over the world; we are talking about a million people who make preliminary investigations to come to Australia to settle. I do not believe we are dealing with this matter as professionally as we should be. There is too little debate in the Parliament about these great issues. There is too little scrutiny by parliamentarians. There is too little involvement by parliamentarians in reaching the end result about what the future programs of Australia ought to be. If we are all to be involved, if we all have to defend what we are doing in Australia at the moment in our electorates, our regions, our constituencies, then we should hav'e sonte part to play in the finalisation of this program. I ask the Minister to look at the matter seriously and discuss with the Government the way in which both sides of the Parliament could be used to look at this question.
The honourable member for Hotham, of course, spent a great deal of time on the old sys-
tem, as we call it, NUMAS. I think the Minister is aware of all the criticisms that have been made at the venues that we have attended throughout the year. There is no doubt that the ethnic communities in Australia quite legitimately believe that family reunion has been submerged as a result of the introduction of NUMAS. This flies in the face of the evidence that has been given to the Department by virtue of the research done on the migrants in Australia, which shows what a strong

feature proper settlement of migrants in Australia is as a result of family reunion programs. There can be no doubt that any new migration program announced by the Minister in the next few days must have a very strong link with family reunion. All of us have to understand also that Australia has changed a great deal. We will be faced with problems in the 1980s and 1990s that we certainly did not face in the 1950s and I960s. It is no good bringing a lot of people here if they have no future. We will have to make some .pretty tough decisions in determinin'g which people we want to settle in this country.
Running parallel to that we must also recognise
that we have an international responsibility in the refugee problem. Of course, we have always acknowledged-I do not expect that we will see any change in this-the relationships we have with New Zealand. I know that it causes some heartburning in the ethnic communities when they see New Zealanders coming to live in Australia and being counted as part of the migration program. There may be some case for isolating the New Zealanders from the total migration and refugee program. Nonetheless, I cannot see any move or support for a move to change
the very good relationships we have with that country or to change New Zealanders' access to Australia or Australians' access to New Zealand. There are some problems there but I think that by and large they can be overcome if we have a genuine, greater involvement of parliamentarians.
The Parliament is changing very radically. In this Parliament we have numbers of people, particularly on our side in the House of Representatives and the Senate, from the ethnic communities. They are people who have experienced coming here to settle and have now found their way to represent in the national Parliament not only their communities but also the Australian community in general. They are more aware of these problems than perhaps parliaments of the past were. I respect their voice on these questions. ' I think that they want to be more active in the formulation of the policies concerning the ethnic communities and the people who may seek to come here.
There has been a lot of speculation about the Ministry being reshuffled when the House goes into recess. I say quite categorically on behalf of the ethnic communities of Australia that we do not want the Minister for Industrial Relations (Mr Viner) shoved off to immigration and ethnic affairs. I think he should be shoved off to the back benches because he has made a mess of Aboriginal affairs, industrial relations and employment. He is not trusted by anybody in this community. We

2234 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

certainly do not want the honourable member for Stirling, the Minister for Industrial Relations, shoved off to the ethnic communities of this country because he is not wanted by anybody else. We consider that the policies and problems ofmigrants and refugees and the post-settlement services of the ethnic communities in Australia are far too important to be given over to a person such as the honourable member for Stirling.

Dr Yeatman went on to comment on the way the Government has responded to the report. He stated:

Mr McLEAN (Perth) (9.37)-The outstanding feature of the estimates for the Department of Social Security is their size and their growth in recent years. It amazes me that a country such as Australia, with 14 million to 15 million people, with a high living standard, which is rich in resources, with well developed education and health systems and which will spend about $11 ,357m this year on social security and welfare, still has areas of poverty and real needs which are not being adequately catered for. It is not the size of the welfare cake, rather the way in which it is distributed, which is the real problem. I think a classic example of this occurred in the recent Budget regarding family allowances.
The Government decided to increase by 50 per cent the family allowance for third and subsequent children in all families in Australia. The -cost of this initiative in a full year is $142m. That is big money but in my opinion it is not well directed. This money is distributed universally; that is, on the basis of a certain specified contingency-the number of children in a family-and not on the basis of whether a family is in a situation of financial need. The trouble with this approach is that millionaires' families and high income families, such as those of members of this Parliament, if they have three or more children, will get help while low income families with two children or less will get nothing extra. I cannot see the equity in that. It means that those families in real need are not being specifically targeted for assistance. Low income families with three or more children will certainly benefit from this initiative, and that is highly commendable, but they will benefit accidentally rather than as a result of deliberate Government policy directed towards the needy.
The Minister for Social Security (Senator Chaney) has recently made statements to the effect that unless we place greater emphasis in our welfare policies on the neediest sections of the community,' a number of things will happenparticularly given the fact that we have an aging population with a declining section involved in a productive capacity. One effect will be that a crippling tax burden will have to be placed on the productive capacity of the economy, thereby reducing our ability to provide adequate welfare resources in the longer term future. Another effect will be that the Government's capacity to assist the poorest sections of the community will
be diminished. I think the Minister is quite correct in expressing that concern. However, this last Budget presented an opportunity for implementing a needs-based approach to welfare through the family allowances program. The Government

did not accept that opportunity, in a program which will involve expenditure of $1,040m in 1981-82.
The Minister has also referred, in recent times, to the future trends in pension appropriations with the aging population and has related this to the way in which many relatively well-off people are circumventing the income test and hence gaining access to pensions to which they would not normally be entitled. In relating the pension to needs-based welfare, the Minister and the Government face a real dilemma in confronting the two principles of, firstly, the need to direct greater assistance to those in greatest need and, secondly, the desire to maintain in the pensions area a system of official pension benefits which does not discourage people from providing for their own retirement.
We have a pension system whereby those who rely on the pension as their sole source of income and have to pay rent can fairly ask why their share of the welfare cake cannot be increased rather than having millionaires and well-off individuals receiving a means test free pension after reaching the age of 70 years. We also have wealthy people or relatively well-off people over the age of 70 stating that they have paid taxes and deserve a pension irrespective of their needs. We have an income test which allows married couples to receive some pension when they have a private income of up to $256.70 a week. The free private income limit under which a full entitlement to a pension is allowed has remained unchanged since 1972 and this has effectively tightened the income test. Those with a small indexed private superannuation income feel that the income test penalises them because as relatively low income earners they made sacrifices during their working lives to make provision for their retirement, but inflation and an uncha'nged free income zone has made that provision inadequate. We also have a situation in which both major political parties in this country are increasingly seeing the need for a greater concentration of welfare resources on the needy.
Given these important and conflicting but very arguable views of the income support system, given the present and future budgetary and social importance of this issue, given that it embraces the whole question of national superannuation schemes and the question of universal versus needs-based welfare, given that it involves a politically sensitive area of policy making, given that it involves a consideration of the relationship between and integration of the income support and taxation programs and given that it involves hundreds of thousands of Australians, I believe the

2238 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

whole matter should be referred to either a standing committee of this House such as the Expenditure Committee or , more preferably a joint select committee of the Parliament. I make the suggestion because a subject of such importance as this should receive the kind of bipartisan examination that only a committee of this Parliament could provide and it should involve the widest possible consultation with the Australian community and particularly the pensioner groups throughout Australia.
I also want to make the point that in the welfare area one does not have to spend hundreds or thousands of millions of dollars to provide necessary assistance to needy groups. In fact many of the areas of great need in our society are catered for by the financially less significant programs of this Department. I will cite two examples in which I have been involved. I have long advocated in this Parliament the need to increase the subsidies payable under the Homeless Persons Assistance Act, an Act directed towards helpingthe homeless, the destitute and the derelict and an Act which in 1981-82 will involve an expenditure of only $6.3m. Between 1974 and 1980 the subsidies for meals and accommodation paid under this Act remained unchanged. In a number of speeches to this House I sought to have the subsidies increased, and in the 1980 Budget increased funds were appropriated. These increases came to about $1 m but they meant so much to those approved institutions under the Act which provide meal ancl accommodation services to a very needy section of the community.
Another example is the Delivered Meals Subsidy Act, which is designed to assist the establishment, the expansion, the improvement and the maintenance of approved meals on wheels services. This service, which is estimated to cost some $4.13m in 1981-82-a small appropriationmakes possible the provision of a most essential service to so many sick and aged persons who still seek to retain their independence by living in their own homes in familiar surroundings but who still need this kind of help to enable them to do so. Again, in 1980, this Government, at very little cost to the Budget but to the very great benefit of those organisations providing this essential service, increased the subsidies payable under the Act. I simply cite these two examples because I have been involved in seeking more assistance for these programs and because they constitute two of the very many areas in the welfare field in w~ich small adjustments and small appropriatIOns can mean so much to the beneficiaries of these services and the beneficiaries who have very

genuine needs. If we are to concentrate more resources in the future to these most essential programs the Government will have to ensure
that its larger welfare' programs are not directed
to the relatively well-off.

Another example I could cite relates to the present inquiry of the House of Representatives Standing Committee on Expenditure which is looking into the accommodation needs of the aged. Although I do not want to ,pre-empt the findings of that Committee, it is. becoming Qbvious that over the years successive governments have been pouring hundreds of millions of dollars into providing institutional accommodation for the aged without actually determining what it is that they desire. It is becoming obviou~ tp me, and I think to the Committee, that many aged people would in fact prefer to remain at home as long as possible provided they can receive adequate domiciliary support.

Of course, institutional accommodation will have to be expanded but a change in the emphasis
of funding might be necessary. l.mention this not to comment on what this Committee might report
but simply to make the point that the aged, and
those representing them, have nor actually been asked what accommodation preference they may
have. Again, we may be able to provide at much
less cost than institutional care a service which is
both cheaper and more in tune with the needs of
the aged. I refer to services such ,as meals on
wheels, chiropody, home help and-hOme maintenance, all of which are highly efficient and cheap
and directed to those in real need.
