20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2239

worth to the community in both services and cost. If the Whitlam Government could be accused of extravagance-this seems to be the one consistent point that members on the Government benches hammer day in and day out-this Government emerges as the exponent of false economy. Preventative health care is cheaper than hospitalisation or institutionalisation, and those centres which employ salaried doctors have proved this over and over again. If this Government were genuine about trying to contain health costs it would encourage the development of salaried medical services rather than support the openended fee for service system.
A reduction in health centre services, which is imminent with the Commonwealth's opting out of the responsibility to f1,lnd health centres, will drive more people back to the public hospital casualty centres and out-patient departments, which of course are dearer to run than community health centres. We will also see the same situation we had pre-Medibank in which people will neglect primary health car,e and warning signs and will seek treatment when their case becomes urgent. The financial cost increases along with the unnecessary suffering. The community health care program focuses on two main objectives: The provision of improved community health services to those living in areas where there are significant unmet needs and the promotion of aspects of health care prevention, health maintenance and rehabilitation that have been neglected in the past.
It is irresponsible for the Federal Government, having given birth to community health centres, to now opt out of the direct funding. The program sponsored projects in such fields as community health and mental health centres, alcohol and drug treatment centres, health education, geriatric and rehabilitation services, domiciliary and
home nursing services, staff training programs, women's health services and youth and adolescent services are vital to our community. The com-
munity health service program has financed some projects to meet the special needs of rural and remote communities, immigrants, Aborigines, family planning and occupational health. The main virtue of the program is that it has focused on the real needs and, despite some problems of management, on cost effectiveness in meeting them. With the advent of block grants to the States, we will have differences within the States.
Victoria, for instance, is now asking the community health care centres to ask for payment for service. The administrative costs of collecting those payments will far outweigh the amount received. This will give the commuriities who use

those health centres a totally different psychological feeling towards the centres. At the moment people in the community see them as their health centres. They are staffed very strongly by people who relate to the communities in those areas. The number of voluntary helpers outstrips the number of paid staff by about two to one. Once we start asking people who cannot afford to pay anyway to pay for those services they will develop a different attitude to the programs being run.
The Government's own policies have increased the demand for community health services. Not only are more people sinking below the poverty line, but also the rising cost of health insurance has forced more and more people to seek less expensive care. In addition, and I think the Department of the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar) published this in its report last year, they have also reduced the number of services needed from psychiatric or mental hospitals and from public hospitals. Preventative health care is cheaper to the community in the long run. It gets away from some of the suffering people undergo about whether they should or should not go and find out what that pain in their back or in their shoulder is. When they cannot get free service from a community health centre, they will put up with the pain a lot longer than they would normally. The end result will be, as it was pre-Medibank, that people will put up with their gall bladders or their bunions until it becomes too much for them. They will land in hospital and the Government will have to face the extra hospital costs.
I think the Government has been extraordinarily short-sighted. This was one program that should have been kept in Commonwealth hands and should have been funded by the Commonwealth. It should be freely available to all the people of Australia and not peddled around, as it will be, depending on who is the government in which State. New South Wales will fare much better than Victoria. I do not think that is fair to the people of Victoria. I suggest to the Minister that it is not too late to think again.
Dr KLUGMAN (Prospect) (9.53)-1 will not detain the House tonight for any length because I have a sore throat, and I will ask for leave to incorporate most of what I would like to refer to. I discussed this matter with the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affair (Mr Macphee) before he left the table. I wish to refer to the question of refugee status and not to the people referred to in the statement made today by the Minister. There does not seem to be any doubt that they were not proper refugees. The general question of whether people who leave Vietnam or other countries at present are real refugees or whether they are what

2240 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

are called economic refugees-in other words, people who just want to better their position in life-is an important question. The Council of Vietnamese Organisations in New South Wales, which is based in my electorate, has produced a paper which has probably been circulated to every member of parliament. I think it is important that it receive maximum exposure in the media and that as many people as possible see the point of view of those who have been genuine refugees from Vietnam. It is a lengthy paper and I will quote only a little of it, but I will ask leave of the House for it to be incorporated in Hansard. It is nine pages and I hope it can be incorporated instead of my making a lengthy speech. I ask leave of the House to incorporate this paper in Hansard.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Drummond}-I should point out to the honourable member for Prospect that, subject to clearance by Mr Speaker for technical purposes, it may not be possible for Hansard to accommodate the honourable member in this exercise.
Dr KLUGMAN-I realise that.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-Under those conditions, leave is granted.
'The document read as follows-
There has been debate in Australia as well as in many other countries concerning the status of the refugees coming out of Indochina, particularly Vietnam. There have not been enough participation from the Indochinese Communities themselves in this public debate. Since it is such a crucial and vital debate concerning ourselves that the Vietnamese Organisations in Australia decided that it is in our interests to put our case before the Government, the Opposition and other interested parties, hoping that it will help all concerned to be better informed of our reasons for leaving Vietnam.
We understand the apprehension and share the concern of the Australian people, regarding the issue. It demonstrates the fact that members of the society do care for the future and the well-being of the country. It teaches us that the responsibilities of being residents and citizens are t~ protect the society and to strive for the betterment of Austr ia, the country that has opened arms to give us a chance tq; egiri a new life. It is also in our and our children's interests for the country to maintain a good moral as well as materials standards and to further advance in all fields, including humanitarian and we have to take part to help achieve it.
What we attempt to do is to present our case from the point of view of the person who has lived in Vietnam, understands the workings of the society over there and who has the first hand experiences about the effects imposed on them by the political decisions, particularly the people who have some connection in some way with the previous regime or the Americans.
We will not debate the appropriateness of the Hanoi's Government policy, though some times it is difficult not to give views on it. In short, we will concern ourselves with people, not politics. We will do it by examining various arguments that have been put forward to question the refugee status of the Vietnamese, aiming at reducing or terminating the Indochinese program.

Statement: Most of the people connected with the Saigon regime have already left.
This is simply not true. Since the first groups of people who left during the last few days of the Saigon regime up until now, there have been about 630,000 refugees coming out of Vietnam. Yet we must remember there were 1,200,000 soldiers in the Republic's Army, over 200,000 police and between 600,000 and 800,000 public servants before April 1975. That is we have not counted the few hundred thousand people who worked for the Americans and the allies, in various capacities. Now, if we take into consideration their families, including wives, children and others, we are in for some 10 million people. Comparing this figure with the number of people who already left, we see the fallacy of the argument.
Question: If there are so many people, how can any country help all?
From our first hand experiences, we know the desire to run away from tyranny is always there, but that is not going to be the decisive factor. The overiding factor is whether the people have the ability to do what they wish to do. As of now, that important factor is diminished every day. The good boats are gone. It is increasingly difficult to find boats in the country. It is getting more and more difficult to find the fuel (State owned and even the State is short of it). The equipment for sea going such as compasses and sea charts are no longer available. In addition the government's patrols are getting tougher and tougher and more and more efficient. People may say you can buy off the officials. That is partly true but you can buy only one locality, not all of them along the way. Therefore the chance of being arrested is always big. It is also known that the longer people stay in the country, the less able they can afford the price to buy off the officials. Once you get out of the Vietnamese water, you have to face the storms, the wind, mechan. ical problems, and food and water shortages and most disastrous are the pirates. Even if you overcome all these obstacles, you still have the hostility of the first asylum countries who are getting tougher and tougher. Just like anything else in life, the events we are looking at have their beginning, so they must have their end. We can see it coming.
It is therefore reasonable for us to ask Australia to extend their tolerance and patience, so that things can have time to work themselves out in a natural way.
Question: There needs to be persecution for a person to claim the refugee status. How are the Vietnamese persecuted?
Persecution refers to the circumstances in which life, liberty or security of a person is threatened. In the case of Vietnam, a person's life, liberty or security or all of them is threatened if that person belongs to a certain group before April, 1975 and that group has been determined by the present government to be "guilty".
Unfortunately, it has been a policy of the present government to make as many people as possible to feel gUilty. This is demonstrated by the fact that in all political indoctrination sessions, people including soldiers, workers, farmers, professionals are told that they are guilty. A nurse is guilty because he or she has helped the sick and the wounded to regain strength to fight the "revolution". A farmer is guilty because his crop is used to feed the Saigon Army. A worker is guilty because he has produced not for the "revolution" but for the enemy. In short, everyone is guilty, except those who have directly participated in their side. This line of argument is repeated in all political indoctrination sessions in the neighbourhoods, the factories, the offices and schools.
In that atmosphere where everyone is made to be conscious of his past in a negative way, people begin to withdraw to themselves, fearing associated with anyone because he may be accused of forming a conspiracy. The tragedy is that the more guilty you feel, the harder you try to stay away from those

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2241

who are in the same boat due to the fear of "conspiracy" accusation. Yet everyone has reasons to feel guilty. The whole society becomes a place full of suspicions and feelings of powerlessness. In effect, the regime, by its imposing on the people the feelings of guilt, has succeeded in separating people from each other.
We should further emphasise that once you feel guilty or made to feel guilty, you feel very vulnerable. If you are arrested, then by that time the guilty feelings already penetrate you deep enough that you tend to give up much more easily because of the odds against you. You know you have no chance of defending yourself. You are at the mercy of the regime. All these "crimes" are "political crimes", therefore they carry the heavy burden of the charge for crimes "against socialism, against the people".
Obsessed with the idea of class struggle, the regime treats people differently depending on whether they belong to "reactionary" or "proletarian" and "revolutionary" class. The concept of "who is friend, who is enemy" is very clear. Unfortunately, if you happen to be a soldier, a businessman, a person who has worked for a foreign organisation, property owner, public servants, intellectuals not trained under socialism, farmer or fisherman against collectivization, you are a reactionary. A person is treated according to the group he associated with in the past, not the individual he is. And yet if we remember that the power of the State is much more than that of a government in the West, then we can see the vulnerable position a person considered to be enemy of the regime is in.
So, the persecution in Vietnam is carried out against a class of people, not necessarily against an individual person although it becomes the case of individual when it is put in practice at the local level. Take for example the case of going to the New EcollOmic Zone. No one wants to go there, including those policy makers who pretend to be idealistic or great ideologists. The question comes down to deciding who has to go. At this point, political factors are decisive. Those who have to go or will be "encouraged" to go include former soldiers, army officers, traders, businessmen, public servants, the newly-released from prisons, writers or artist who do not "repent"; in short all "undesirables" politically. The economic advantage or disadvantage is solely decided by political consideration. In any factory or office, whenever comes the reassessment for wage increase. The first group of people to be considered are party members and the last ones are the people who have connections to the past. In fact the last group should feel lucky, simply because they are still there. But a person with any past connection will never be eligible for party memberships, the group that have the highest priority and privilege in everything.
Take another example: enrolment for tertiary or vocational educations. The children of those who have "unclean" past are excluded.
It may be appropriate to remind everyone that the intention of any communist leadership is to create a new "ruling class", coming from the people they believe were "persecuted" in the past and demonstrated by the "revoluationary" zeal. If someone proves to them that the lowly-paid public servants and soldiers of the old regime may be the ones most disadvantaged in the past, though they had no influence in the history they were forced to take part, the communists would have nothing to do with this reasoning, believing instead that these people are dangerous agents of capitalism.
Up to this point, we have only proved that the people are disadvantaged, discriminated but have not attempted to prove they are persecuted.
We say that are persecuted as a class of people. Take the soldiers of the old regime. In many provinces and in the outer districts of Saigon, whenever there is irrigation or other public

work to be done, these soldiers are rounded first and told to work, of course unpaid (forced labour). If there are antigovernment leaflets, they will be the ones rounded up first for a session of "education" lasting from one, two days to a week or ten days. If the authorities perceive threat during occasions such as the National Day, Ho Chi Minh's birthday and "liberation" day, these people will simply be detained as a preventive measure, for a few days or longer.
The above treatment is given not only to soldiers, but to any one or group of people who they believe are against the regime, and that invariably are the people with past connection. We can also tell you that for many people, they still have not given back what is called "the rights of citizenship". The law is written by the regime, interpreted and carried out, then enforced by it, you can see the awful position any individual with past connection is in.
We can further say that the people with past connection or against the collectivisation are persecuted because they are the ones who always get the attention of the local policeman whose role in one's life can be unbearable. It must be known that in Vietnam now for every 20, 25 families in a neighbourhood, there is a policeman who lives right in the middle of the neighbourhood. Part of his job is to know everyone, particularly those with a past, or who is believed to be against the regime. These people are good target for blackmail or harrassment which HAVE become a way of life over there. The people feel so powerless as there is simply nowhere they can confide at or ask for help.
It must be realised that in Vietnam the only employer and food and other essentials distributor is the government. It would be most unfortunate if anyone is disliked by that important body called government. If that government decides to link your past or its suspicion with the security of your job and the continuity of your food ration card, then you will be in for big trouble.
A word about the food ration card. This is a big weapon the government uses to carry out their policy. For example, if you belong to the group that have to go to the New Economic Zone and you do not go, the regime will not have to put a gun into your head and tell you to go. They simply have to take away the validity of ration card, then you go. If you do not go to neighbourhood political meeting or do not go to vote on election days, they can always hint on the validity of the ration card, then you go!!!
The conclusion is that if you have a certain connection with the past regime, or in some way are perceived as having some link or tendency against the regime or unhappy with it, then you will receive the full weight of the regime. It would be very unfortunate if you are discriminated by a government in the West, but it is a lot more frightening if you are not liked by the Vietnamese communist regime because the communist regime has the controls on a lot more aspects ofyourlife.
We would like to emphasize that the communists work on association, so that if you are not "progressive", then they will have the same treatment to your children, parents as they do to you.
Question: It seems that the people are running away from economic hardship rather than political persecution?
It is true only if we are confused between the effect and-its cause. As we say earlier, economic advantage or disadvantage is decided by political factors. If a teacher is denied the opportunity to teach, just because of his past and told to go to New Economic Zone, a professional cannot practice because of his past, then it is not economic. It is political. Furthermore, as reasoned by the Wall Street Journal, since when did it happen that the economic order does not follow the political order? We can also tell you that Vietnam has had economic trouble in the past, including a famine in 1945 that killed more than 2 million people, but the question of leaving the country was not

2242 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

the thing to do. Just like any other peoples who are rooted deep into the land on which they live, the Vietnamese would be the last ones who will be willing to leave the land that holds memories, ancestors and every meaningful thing in their lives.
Therefore when people leave, they leave because of political discrimination, political persecution that threatens their liberty, their security or their lives. That persecution is having effects not only on their liberty, their security, but also economic well-being which is mostly only what outsiders see.
Argument: But we see a lot'of young men in the camps. They are running away from the draft, aren't they?
Anyone who studies Marxism and Leninism would have known that the regime considers the army as the hammer of the CLASS (so-called proletarian). Their role is to use "revolutionary violence" to smash the reactionaries and the enemies of the "proletarian". For that reason, the regime selects soldiers very carefully. Their consideration is based on class background, for three generations back. The background has to be one that the regime can instill the class hatred to motivate the soldiers to fight. That explains the reasons why Ha Noi would not draft the Catholic youth in North Vietnam during the Vietnam War until they ran out of politically acceptable men in the middle and toward the end of the war. These Catholics can never be more than soldiers ..
Although being in the Army does not necessarily give the soldiers any direct material rewards, but they do give political priorities when it comes to the question of party membership. When you are a party member, you are .in the elite. People in the West think of communist party 'membership as a result of great ideological commitment. It should be but it is not. It is looked upon as a mean to get promotion, to have priority in job, in having annual wage increases and other advantages.
Knowing the role of the Army, you will realize that the children of the people with past connection will not be "qualified ideologically" to be in the Army. Instead they are taken into the "Volunteer Youth League" to clear the jungle, the minefields, to dig irrigation canals. They are taken out of the school, given no work, not permitted to be in the army and especially the police (need to be even more ideologically qualified). All doors and windows are closed except volunteer youth league. The local police and communist youth league (a different organisation) come to their houses all the time to "encourage" the young man or woman to "volunteer". Your mental resistance can only offer so much resistance and you give in after a while, knowing that the past of your family can be questioned if you persist in declining to go. Then you go.
It is a disguised discrimination and persecution. Remember that these young "volunteers" do not have wages or salary, only food eaten in collective dining hall, but very often not enough because of their heavy work. We should once again say that the death or the cost on the young "volunteers" are never in the regime's worries when we are reminded that their motto is to build socialism on the blood and sweat of the "reactionaries", among others.
These young are destined, in terms of ideology, to be in such organisations as "volunteer youth league". If there is any change, it is due to the need of the regime at a certain period. Looking at it under this light, we will realize when the regime began to take these people into the Army in 1979 because of the military need in Kampuchea (to free the "true" soldiers to go North facing China), they did it just as when they took the Catholic youth in the North earlier. They did it because of their needs and discrimination and the suspicion will go to these people in the Army too. They are to carry ammunition, doing hard labour work, only soldiers under North Vietnamese officers' command.
Therefore when these young men leave the country, they are running away from persecution, not running away from the draft in an ordinary way.

Question: What about the fishermen and farmers, why would they be the victims of discrimination and persecution?

If they are in any way having connection with the regime in

the past such as being in the Army, rural pacification program

or rural officials, public servants, anti-communist religions

like caodaism, hoahaoism or Catholics or their parents or chil-

dren having that sort of connection, then they are marked per-

sons. Furthermore, if they are against the collectivisation of

means of productions such as farms, fishing boats, then they

are marked persons too. More than anyone else, as a group of

people, the farmers and fishermen cherish private ownership.

The farms and fishing boats ARE their lives. They are born

onto it and they will die with it. The collectivisation makes

them feel lost and dispossessed. The general assumption is

that they prefer city and industrial life. There is nothing more

wrong than that. Th~y are not mobile as people in indus-

trialized countries. As a result, they are against collectivisa-

tion, violently. The precedents have been established every-

where that the farmers and fishermen would not agree to

collectivisation. When it is forced upon them, they either re-

belled, slaughtered the cattle or in the case of Vietnam, "leav-

ing" the country because they see no chance in uprising and

yet if they stay, they will be subject to harsh treatment. In

short, the farmers and fishermen ARE simple people, but they

are still victims, targets of persecution if they have connection

with the past or have attitudes that do not conform to that of

the government.

'.

Question: How can the situation be so bad? If it were, the regime would not have lasted that long.

In terms of human sufferings, the situation is very bad. We must remember that it was a civil war lasting for many decades, therefore it involved everyone on both sides. If the victors are not serious' in national reconciliation as promised, everyone will be in for a bad time. In the South, if you remember that there were 2 million Catholics, 1.2 million soldiers,
200,000 police, 600 to 800 thousand public servants, 2 million Chinese, 3-4 million Caodaist and Hoahao (two religious sects vigorously against communism), I million northern refugees
of 1954 and if the regime does not restrain its obsession with "enemies from within", then the regime will be bound to act
on their obsession which will result in wanton discrimination and harsh persecution. That is exactly what is happening. Therefore to the people who have connection to the past in
some way, the regime is currently pursuing a policy of active
discrimination and disguised persecution.

Concerning the second part of the question, why the regime
lasts, we can point to the role played by police at all levels and furthermore, the "guilty complex" described earlier has made people afraid of each other and can never talk to each other
trustingly. In addition, the method of preventive detention also helps foil all possible conspiracy against the regime.

Question: OK, the people are persecuted and there are many of them, how could Australia possibly help all of them. We are limited in our means.

We fully share the concerns. In fact, that is one of the factors that make us hesitant or timid in participating into the de-
bate. We realise Australia is doing its share, and we do not want to appear to push or take advantage of the good will of
Australians.

However, with your understanding and permission, we take the liberty to put the following suggesting forward. We
suggest that Australia maintain the Indochinese refugee programs. Our suggestion is based on the following:

I. The refugee flow will end by itself. We said earlier that the difficulty in finding boats, fuels, seagoing equipments,
government patrols, storms, wind at sea, the pirates and the hostility at the first asylum countries will act as obstacles to
the wish to run away from tyranny.

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2243

2. The UNHCR still considers the Vietnamese as prima facie refugees.
3. A reduction in Indochinese refugee intake will act as an encou'fagement for the hostility of new arrivals into the first asylum countries. It will put the Vietnamese at even more danger with the priates. It will also have the effect of undermining international efforts in accepting the refugees, particularly with such a country as Canada whose Minister of Immigration has declared in Hong Kong that his country will maintain the present program last month.
4. By maintaining the program, Australia shares the problem with Asia.
5. We also believe that Australia should not be one of the first countries to announce a reduction or a tough refugee policy. She does not need to. Even if Australia has a bigger refugee program, but if the camps in Southeast Asia are empty, she would not have people to fill the program. In another word, if Thailand (already tough), Malaysia and Indonesia decide not to be the countries of first asylum any more, then the camps will be likely empty. In that case, Australia will be released, yet still be praised for her high humanitarian concerns which are not numerous in' this world.
In order to obtain public support, we suggest Australia broaden the concept of persecution. This concept, as we understand it, is never defined clearly any way. In the case of the Vietnamese, we maintain that a person is discriminated, persecuted because he or she has some connection to the previous regime and that, as a result, threatens his or her liberty, life or security. One needs to understand the narrow concept of class struggle and the overwhelming role played by the State in Vietnamese communism to explain the fact that sections of the Vietnamese population in the South are discriminated and persecuted because of their past.
Finally, we would like to present some intriguing thoughts toyou:
Try to imagine a society in which if you want to go to another district, you have to get written permit from your village authority. When you arrive at the destination you have to report to the local authority over there.
Try to imagine if you want to go to another province, you must get written permission from the district level and if you want to go to the border provinces or the beach you must get written permission from the provincial level. You simply cannot spend overnight at your parents, your friends or relatives without asking permission and many times it is not given, particularly with those who have past connection because they want to limit your mobility.
Try to imagine that your ration card is often used to whip you into doing what the government wants. You must offer free labour for so many days a year to "build socialism". You should attend weekly or forthnightly political study sessions in your neighbourhood, you should go to vote on election days; otherwise.your ration card will be a topic to be discussed in the Committee. Try to imagine the government wants you to go to the New Economic Zone and if you resist, one of the methods used is to take away your ration card. A whole society cannot think of anything other than to avoid troubles with the authority and "my food ration card".
And remember this practice is not temporary. It is a permanent feature because it is ideological. We speak from personal experiences, coupled with the understanding of the ideology behind it. We have lived under the regime that we are talking to you about today. It is honest, true and not intentional communist-bashing. There were so many idealists, so many social reformers, so many nationalists before April 1975 and are presently in Vietnam. They are the people most unhappy and have a great deal of regrets. Before, they could not believe the new regime can be that bad for the people. Now they

know, but it is too late. They are condemned to live there for the rest of their lives, our poor compatriots.
People talk about the family reunion scheme under the "orderly departure program ". It is very good and we hope it will get started soon. However, it ha~ to be carried out along with other refugee program from the camps. The family reunion only helps those who already have close relatives overseas, but what about the people who do not? They m~y need even more help.
One final thought. Vietnam is being ruled by Marxist revolutionaries. As experienced by many people, we believe it is sometimes bad to be ruled by politicians, but we can assure you it is most frightening, most fearsome to be ruled.by revolutionaries, especially Marxist revolutionaries.
It sounds like we are asking too much from the Australian Community. Ifit does, we can only hope the noble tradition of Australia of giving refuge to the poor, the homeless, the disadvantaged, the displaced and the refugees will win over. We will enlieavour to demonstrate with time that we are not burden to the society. On the contrary, we have every intention to contribute, to love and to defend this country as much as we love our unfortunate Fatherland, Vietnam.
Mr KLUGMAN-This material, or argument, has been prepared by a group of Vietnamese. Their English grammar is not perfect, but I am sure it is much better than the grammar of most of us who claim to be able to speak foreign languages. It attempts to answer a number of questions, although I do not necessarily agree with all of the answers. One of the arguments is on the matter I mentioned earlier, and on this point the document reads:
Question: It seems that the people are running away from economic hardship rather than political persecution?
I think it is important to look at it from the point of view of those Vietnamese who have left and to read what they are saying about it. Certain groups of people, they argue, are being discriminated against-I do not think there is much doubt about that-people who were associated with the previous regime in Saigon before the communists won in 1975. They are persecuted, they say, as a class of people. They talk about not only soldiers but also other people who the present regime believes are against it and invariably these are people with what they call past connections. The document states:
We can also tell you that for many people, they still have not given back what is called 'the rights of citizenship': The law is written by the regime, interpreted and carried out, then enforced by it, you can see the awful position any individual with past connections is in.
They are talking about what people with those past connections, such as soldiers of the previous regime, are forced to do. They continue:
In many provinces and in the outer districts of Saigon, whenever there is irrigation or other public work to be done, these soldiers are rounded first and told to work, of course unpaid (forced labour). If there are anti-government leaflets, they will be the ones rounded up first for a session of 'education' lasting from one, two days to a week or ten days. If the authorities perceive threat during occasions such as the

2244 REPRESENTATIVES 20 October 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

National Day, Ho Chi Minh's birthday and 'liberation' day, these people will simple be detained as a preventive measure, for a few days or longer.
We can further say that the people with past connection or against the collectivisation are persecuted because they are the ones who always get the attention of the local policeman whose role in one's life can be unbearable. It must be known that in Vietnam now for every 20, 25 families in a neighbourhood, there is a policeman who lives right in the middle of the neighbourhood. Part of his jOb is to know everyone, particularly those with a past, or who is believed to be against the regime. These people are good target for blackmail or harrassmeilt which have become a way of life over there. The people feel so powerless as there is simply nowhere they can confide at or ask for help.
It must. be realized that in Vietnam the only employer and food and other essentials distributor is the government. It would be most unfortunate if anyone is disliked by that important body called government. If that government decides to link your past or its suspicion with the security of your job and the continuity of your food ration card, then you will be in for big trouble.
I suggest that people who speak rather blandly about some of the refugees being economic refugees should read the statement from the Council of Vietnamese Organisations. I hope they will come to a different point of view.
I will devote one minute to a suggestion relating to select committees, which the House ought to take seriously. If we consider the House of Representatives and the Senate, there are 64 senators and 125 members of the House. There are, of course, a larger number of Ministers in this House than there are in the Senate. Nonetheless, there are many more people left in this House than there are in the Senate to deal with the committees. If we look at the Senate Notice Paper for today, or for any other day, we will see some nine pages of committees and the matters that have been referred to them. I first became aware of this when Senator Haines moved originally for the referral of the question of private hospitals and nursing homes to the Senate Standing Committee on Social Welfare. That Committee already had a significant number of other references to deal with. Therefore it was decided to set up a Senate select committee. I notice that the only person on the select committee is Senator Haines. I think that is unsatisfactory. The Government-the people who run the business of this House, such as the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar), who is at the table-ought to look at it. There are some people with specialist knowledge in this House. There ought to be a possibility of setting up reasonable committees in this House which will deal with these matters.

Dr KLUGMAN (Prospect) (10.1)-1 conclude by reiterating that there ought to be a possibility for propositions in this House to be put before either select committees or the more general types of committees such as those the Senate has-committees comprising people who have some interest in, for example, questions of social welfare, health, immigration or finance. I feel that with the help and advice of experts outside this Parliament it may well be possible to improve the workings of government and make reasonable recommendations. In a large number of cases a bipartisan policy will, in fact, arise. I appeal again to the Minister and to the Government as a whole to have a look at the question of committees, so that when propositions are moved in the House of Representatives it might be possible to refer them to such committees.
Dr CASS (Maribyrnong) (10.2)-1 wish to comment firstly on the contribution made by the honourable member for Fadden (Mr Donald Cameron) when he was dancing around in the aisle lecturing us on the illogicality of the Australian Labor Party's approach to immigration. He asserted that on the one hand we wanted to bring in hundreds of thousands of people and that, on the other hand, we cried about the enormous rate of unemployment. He questioned why we wanted to increase immigration in massive numbers and encourage family reunion and then complain about increasing unemployment when, according to him, most of the people likely to come into Australia under the family reunion category would be unskilled people.
Our position on this matter has been quite clear and consistent. We acknowledge that; in economic terms, there is a problem at the moment in the Australian community, as there is throughout the rest of the Western world, leading to an increasing problem of unemployment. For this reason the policy of the Australian ''Labor Party has been quite consistent. We have been opposed to bringing in large numbers of people. In fact, during the years that we were in government the number of people coming into the country was lower than the number of people being brought in under the present Government's Numerical

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2245

Multi-factor Assessment System. We have said also that we believe NUMAS should be abolished. As our spokesman, the honourable member for Port Adelaide (Mr Young), pointed out, we be-
lieve, and all of the evidence seems to suggest, that NUMAS in its actual workings had been biased against non-English speaking people. It certainly has been very biased against family reunion.
In our view, in the present climate we should not be seeking to plunder the rest of the world for skilled workers. That does not mean that we should cut off our noses to spite our faces and not have any skilled workers coming to Australia. If skilled workers are to be allowed to come here it should be only because there are specific jobs for them to come to. There should be none of this nonsense that we 'will have all these great
Eidorado schemes, or development projects for which we will want umpteen thousands of workers whom we will bring in from overseas. In the first place many of these development projects have fallen flat; they will not eventuate. All the estimates of the number of skilled workers that we would need have been grossly inflated. Of course, these projects will not need skilled workers in the immediate future anyway. If we had the will we would have the time to train those people who are already here-old and recent inhabitants of this country, people born here and people who come here as immigrants, people whose skills are no longer required-or the children of those immigrants who are coming out of schools with practically no options at all. We could train them to do those skilled jobs.
We have been against the trend pursued by the Liberal Government of seeking to invite more and more skilled workers to this country, bringing with them dependants-wives and children. Often they will bring in wives who are not skilled, but who feel they will need to work like many other wives of working men in this country. When we bring in skilled workers with no family attachments already in Australia we increase the pool of people in the country who may want to bring their relations to this country at some time. They are people. They are human. They are not just skilled workers. Often they are a part of a significant family group. When they come here, as the Minister would have done if he had migrated from somewhere, and as my parents did when they migrated here, they come in the hope that if they find the country to their liking and can settle they may bring the rest of their families here. That is a natural human instinct.
Our answer to that difficult situation is that we should train as many of the skilled workers as we possibly can. However, we should recognise our

obligation to the immigrants who have come here, who have helped to build this country in the last 20 years or 30 years through programs that both parties have supported. Those people have rights. Whatever number of immigrants it is considered that we can absorb-as a rich country, despite the difficulties, we can absorb some-we should concentrate on that number of immigrants being mainly family reunion cases. I am leaving aside our responsibilities in the refugee area, which is a difficult question. We agree with the Government that that should be governed by negotiations and agreement with the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees. We pick no quarrel with the Government on that. We are talking about the non-refugee immigration. We are suggesting that, at this time, for the reasons that I have given, we should concentrate on family reunions.
Mr MacKellar-How do you define the family?
Dr CASS-We owe it to those people who are already here. Our definition, which would be rather wider than that of the Government, would go beyond the immediate family members of husbands or wives, fiancees and dependent children as defined by the Government. We stated quite clearly in bur policy that unmarried children would still be considered dependent irrespective of tijeir age. We considered also that other relations should be given the opportunity within the quota system that is obviously envisaged by this Government if it is to allow 70,000 immigrants into this country. There would be nowhere near that many immediate close relations wanting to come in to fill that whole quota.
Mr MacKellar-It depends on how you define 'relations'.
Dr CASS-I am talking, for instance, in terms of unmarried children of any age. There would not be--
Mr MacKellar-But you mentioned others?
Dr CASS-Precisely. There would not be the numbers to fill the quotas. Whatever number is left could be filled by other members of the extended family such as sisters, brothers, uncles, aunts, nieces and nephews who, at the moment, have no chance unless they qualify under NUMAS as having particular skills. We suggest that most people in the' country do not see the family as just the nuclear family. They see it as the extended family. I certainly am aware that my family, and most people I know, consider it in that way. Yet our immigration policy limits the family to the nuclear family, cutting it off dead once a child reaches the age of 18. We think that is wrong.

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

Mr MacKellar-And if the extended family too~ up the whole of the quota there would be no skilled immigrants?
Dr CASS-I ask the Minister to hold on. Obviously it would have to be done on a quota system. We would have to have some priorities. But at least we should give some hope to the other family members to get here over the course of some years. At the moment there is no hope. I do not wish to continue the debate in this way as I want to turn to something else. I think I have stated quite clearly how our policy differs from that of
the Government.
I conclude with a few brief comments about the short-sightedness of the Minister for Health in emasculating the community health program by just putting the funding for that program in a block grant to the States and leaving it to the States to administer. I say that it is short-sighted because we are told that the purpose of the exercise is to cut down the cost of health care in this country. I wish to give the House some interesting figures. If we compare the amount spent on health as a proportion of gross domestic product in the United States of America, Canada and Australia we find that in 1975-76 the United States spent 8.4 per cent, Canada spent 6.8 per cent and we spent 7.7 per cent-an enormous amount of money. When we look at how that money is carved up we find that the doctors, as they often claim, are not responsible because their fees are not the main cost. That is true. In 1975-76 medical fees totalled 19 per cent of health care costs in the United States, 16 per cent in Canada and 17.9 per cent in Australia. But the cost of hospital care as a proportion of the total health bill was 39 per cent in the United States, 50 per cent in Canada and 54.4 per cent in this country. Most of the cost re-
lates to institutional care-hospital care.
The idiocy of the Government's approach is that it does not recognise that the community health program will help keep people out of hospitals. Because this program is to be based on salaried medical service it will reduce the incentive for d9ctors to supply the sorts of services which require admission to hospitals. After all, when the Government set up the Jamison Commission of Inquiry into the Efficiency and
Administration of Hospitals, it failed to note that the hospitals do not have patients but doctors have patients; that patients do not admit themselves to hospital to have their appendix out, their hernia done or anything else. It is doctors who make those sorts of decisions. It is doctors who determine the level of utilisation of those services. It is doctors who determine ultimately the total cost of health care in institutions; it is not patients

and, I suggest, it is not even governments, other than when they are idiotic, such as this one is being at the moment by cutting back the one program which could reduce the demand for hospital services by increasing the capacity of the community health centres to provide the sort of preventive health care which will be available only in community health centres.

Mr HOLDING (Melbourne Ports) (10.12)-1 wish to deal with the estimates for the Department of Aboriginal Affairs. As a member of the Estimates Committee, I am bound to say that a great deal of information was provided to that Committee, much of which was very useful. Unfortunately, with all the information that was provided, it was not possible for the Committee to go to the great questiQn of Aboriginal affairs in Australia in 1980 and, I suspect, in 1981 and 1982. I refer to the question of Aboriginal land rights which, of course, provides the basis for Aboriginal identity. Upon that issue depends the future preservation of Aboriginal culture and Aboriginality in Australia.
Aboriginal demands for the return of property and for compensation as they are being put to this community at the moment represent essentially a plea for the restoration of a just relationship with other Australians. It should not be forgotten that when white settlement of Australia first' took place every Aboriginal clan was in possession of its land, the boundaries of which were largely defined and understood. White settlement involved a massive exercise in dislocation, dispossession and genocide. In many parts of Australian society those issues have come to be understood only lately. The 1967 referendum which gave the Commonwealth constitutional power to deal with Aboriginal affairs was, I think, a recognition by a dominant white Australian community that the responsibility rested fairly upon the Commonwealth Government and upon a government with the sorts of resources that are available to the Commonwealth to do something to redress the balance.
I think it is true to say that the extent to which advances have been made-c-advances have been made-has been largely due to the funds that have flowed from the Commonwealth to the States. The major advance, of course, came during the Whitlam years. Whilst it has been fashionable Jor this Government and for the present Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) to criticise many aspects of the policies of the Whitlam

Mr MILDREN (Ballarat) (10.22)-1 wish to make a statement on a matter relating to one of the departments considered by Estimates Committee B, namely, the Department of Social Security. It is a matter which has concerned me for a great deal of time and I am sure that when I draw the House's attention to it in this the International Year of Disabled Persons it may well strike other honourable members of the House in much the same way. I see nothing particularly remarkable in the estimates for that Department in the sense that I see no particular change from pre-existing policies that perhaps reflect our attitudes towards disabled people in our society, probably not only in our society but also in many other societies. I really do not see much point in actually directing attention to specific sums of money outlined in the estimates papers. I merely say that they reflect an attitude towards the disabled which does not really consider the disabled people to be citizens of this country along with the rest of us, having the normal rights for development of whatever talents they have.
I would say that all political parties, my party not excepted, reflect the kinds of attitudes which are inherent in our society. We have an attitude towards the disabled which states, I think, that it is reasonable for them to be objects of charity. In fact, very many very fine, well-intentioned people in our society join charitable organisations and conduct all sorts of campaigns to raise money to provide a start on some kind of treatment for disabled people. That is a very archaic way of looking at the matter. It is an attitude that probably was left over not just from the nineteenth century but from previous centuries, an attitude whereby we see disabled peoples as being not whole, not complete human beings; as being people who are simply there for us to dispense our charity on; as being perhaps to some degree people who are to be tolerated. In our society we even go so far as to say that it is reasonable to distinguish between

them and others before they are born and, if possible, to make it convenient for other people by dispensing with their lives. I find that totally abhorrent and I find it abhorrent that people, after
they are born, are treated as less than human beings.

I believe that the policies reflected in the state-

ments by the Government in the estimates for the

Department of Social Security indicate that we

have a long way to go. We are approaching the

eleventh hour of the International Year of Dis-

abled Persons and I do not believe that this society

has indicated very much enlightenment on this

issue. I do not believe that our society has

indicated that we have gone very far at all along

the road towards recognising the rights of those

people to be considered fully as citizens of our so-

ciety. I would like to see all parties accept the

proposition that from here on in disabled people

should not be given rights that are just equal to

those of other people-that would not be

sufficient in terms of funding-but that we should

take affirmative action and use positive discrimi-

nation in their favour. The able-bodied people of

this country must bear the brunt of the financial

sacrifice necessary to ensure that disabled people

are given the maximum opportunity to develop

their talents. No matter how meagre those talents

might be, those people must be given the right to

fulfil their lives.

.

I know that many people are stunted in their
development through charity. Charity is not bad, but it does not get to the root of problems; it
leaves the problems unsolved. Many people-
people with cerebral palsy, for instance-are treated as children all of their days by well-
intentioned people in our society. I look forward
to the time when all parties of this nation demon-
strate a maturity of understanding of what it is to be disabled and demonstrate a will to overcome
the problems that confront the disabled. I hoped
that in this Year of Disabled Persons a start might
have been made in that direction. I think that is a
charge to the conscience of members of this House, to the conscience of the parliamentary
leaders of our society. It is not good enough that
we just reflect the attitudes that are still held in our society. Parliamentary leaders should take the
lead and demonstrate a will of their own to pro-
vide a decent world, a world of true equality for
the disabled persons of our society.

briefly to a couple of the points raised by honour-
able members during this debate on the Estimates. I point out that, in relation to the Community
Health Program, the Commonwealth really does

Appropriation Bill (No. 1)

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2249

have more faith in the State governments than obviously the Opposition in this place has.
Dr Klugman-Ours is variable.
Mr MacKELLAR-I agree with the honourable member; his is very variable. We believe that voters in the States have the opportunity to make known their points of view and impress them upon the various State governments, regardless of the political complexion of those governments. If the States agree with the points of view in relation to the worth and benefit of community health centres, as put forward this evening by various speakers, particularly from the Opposition side, there is nothing to stop those governments, regardless of their political complexion, from organising their community health programs in that way.
We are seeking to bring about a situation in which the allocation of scarce resources is such that the taxpayer is put upon to the least possible extent. If there is a divided responsibility on these matters there is a very great tendency, the Government believes, for an untoward amount being required from the individual taxpayer. If we move in the direction in which the Government has moved we believe that there is more likelihood that efficient administration and efficient use of total community resources will be the final effect. This has been demonstrated very conclusively already in New South Wales. Shortly after the Commonwealth put aside the hospital cost sharing arrangement with the State governments, the New South Wales Government found that doctors were taking advantage of the situation by undertaking weekend work in a particular public hospital in New South Wales to the extent that the taxpayers were being required to pay an extraordinary amount of money to fund the work of those doctors in what can be described only as moonlighting activities during the weekends. What we seek to achieve is high quality health care services, available at the least possible cost, so that individuals in the community will not have to face an ever-increasing burden, in tax or in any other payment, for high quality health care.

Mr CUNNINGHAM (McMillan) (10.37)-1 will address my remarks to the estimates for the Department of Home Affairs and Environment. I suggest that more of the funding for that Department should be spent on some of the great heritage of this country. The Department spends $139m, but very little is put aside to guarantee to future Australians the preservation of some of our greatest assets and treasures..For the last I ~O years Australia has been the biggest producer m the world of gold nuggets, but the number of nuggets still in this country is absolutely nil. The last nugget found of any importance was the Hand of Faith nugget. However, at present the fam~us Hand of Faith nugget is the only large AustralIan nugget in the world which has survived the smelting furnace. The Hand of Faith nugget, whic.h was found at Kingower in Victoria, is today on display at the Gold Nugget Casino in Las Vegas in the United States. This nugget was sold to overseas interests for $USlm. Today it is insured for $US3m.
Checking with the Bureau of Mineral Resources, I found that the largest nugget in its collection of gold nuggets is a one-ounce New Guinea nugget. The largest Australian nugget is a five-pennyweight nugget. In Victoria $Im has been spent on building a gold museum at Ballarat, and the largest nugget there weighs 22 ounces. I present in the Parliament this evening a nugget that was discovered very recently in Victoria, and I will put it on the desk so that all members in the House can see that it resembles the map of Australia. It is a very important find. The nugget weighs 256t oz, which makes it the second largest nugget discovered in this country since 1908. It would be one of the largest nuggets in the world. It should be in a museum if this country can see its way Clear to make sure that this nugget stays in this country. Its approximate value is $200,000. I urge this Government, on behalf of the people of Australia, to see that this nugget stays here.
It was discovered on the side of the road just six inches down. For many years the local grader drivers had been driving past it, missing it by less

Many nuggets have been discovered but none of them survive in this country. The reason that I brought this nugget into this Parliament tonight is to emphasise that these things do not come ve~y easily. They are very rare. I urge those W?o are m a position to make decisions to see that thiS nu~~t does not leave Australia. It would be a shame If It had to go to Las Vegas like the last decent nugget that was found in this country in order for people to see it. We want to keep the nugget here in Australia. Three gentlemen were involved in discovering this nugget. One was keen to see that the money was collected. We would not blame anybody who was rather poor to w.ant to.h~ve a sha~e of the funds as quickly as possible. Slttmg on thiS desk at the moment is a nugget worth three nice homes. The other two gentlemen are fairly keen to see that the nugget does not get melted down. They have made arrangements to take the other gentleman's share over to. see that t~e nugget .stays in Australia. The nugget IS here. It IS AustralIan. I believe that it should stay Australian. I urge everyone to support that approach.

Mr IAN CAMERON (Maranoa) (10.45)-1 address my comments to the estimates oUhe Department .of Communications. Before doing so I must say-that'tdonot have any gold nuggets to present. (~snhatmaybe the nugget displayed by the honoutablem6lTiber for McMillan (Mr Cunningham) go to the Breakfast Creek mob in Queensland because I think the honourable member for Griffith (Mr Humphreys) and his crew need the money more than the National Party does in Queensland. If honourable members are really looking for something to do with that nugget they could melt' it down and spend it on telephones in Maranoa. That is one of the subjects on which I wish to addres~ the Parliament tonight.
The Government' is allocating a tremendous amount of money towards satellite communications. An-atlloU'nt of $52,000 is being allocated to the satellite receiving antenna experimental program. Of the outlay of $4.28m, quite a deal is going to my electorate. Some is being speiU on Taroom, Wandoan and Injune. Texas and other places are listed for connection to the remote area television service. I commend the Government for the efforts that it. has made in this regard. Of course, we are alI'hoping that the satellite, will be launched on time. An enormous amount is to be spent on that project. It is hoped that those people who do not get radio and television services will be able to do so. It. is hoped also that with the work the Department orCommunications will be doing we Will get the costs of an aerial down to something that we can all afford to pay; that is,

between $500 and $1,000. Of course, it is hoped also that when the new satellite goes up it will be of much greater power-in the range of 50 amps compared to the present five-amp power. This will give us much greater coverage and much better reception.
I move to the allocation to radio. I made it quite clear to the Estimates Committee that the Department had overlooked some of the areas needing a radio station, particularly Charleville. I continue to make a plea to those concerned in the Department and the Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair) to look at allocating some funds to Charleville. It is still a fact of life that today in some areas of Australia one still cannot hear the radio. I think that is an indictment of the way in which some of the funds are being spent. We are pushing ahead with new types of video transmission, et cetera, and still there are people who cannot get radio reception. I think this is
something that we have to look at very closely.
Some of my constituents have complained bitterly that they co\lld not receive test cricket. It is important to remember that the Government is spending $2m to purchase the rights to televise test cricket in the coming 12 months. The other item of interest that I note is that we are spending $21m on symphony orchestras in Australia in the coming financial year. We are to spend $100m on television, $50m on radio and $20m on orchestras. I think it is important, when we sit down to lis~en to good music, to remember that it is costing us a great deal of money.
I turn my attention to the telephone system operated by Telecom Australia. I must congratulate the Minister. He has managed to get the acting general manager, Mr Pollock, to come to the House tomorrow night to meet with those of us who are interested in some of the problems involved with Telecom. The main criticism I have concerns the cost of telephone connections. Most people who live in Australia do not realise what it is costing some of those who live outside the 16kilometre circle to have a subscriber trunk dialling phone conn'ected. This is not a new service; it iS,an old service that is being converted. It is a fact-I have figures here that I received from the Department today-that 5,793,000 people are subscribers to Telecom. Of those, 61,000 people still operate from manual exchanges. There are only 8,000 of the five million outside the circle. So it means that five million people in Australia have had an STD phone connected at no cost and that 8,000 people who live in the more isolated areas of Australia are being asked to contribute to the capital cost of a connection. I argue that that is grossly unjust.

Mr MILDREN (Ballarat) (10.54)-1 want to comment on a matter which arises under the estimates for the Department of Home Affairs and Environment. The matter was raised by my colleague, the honourable member for McMillan (Mr Cunningham) a short time ago. In fact, the matter is difficult to forget. It may seem slightly humourous, of course, because we do not have these things happen every day in this House. But the nugget really does conjure up in our minds many colourful and romatic images of our history and the glory of the golden triangle in which it was found. Of course, I refer to central Victoria which has one of the most romantic golden histories of this country. I feel that it would be a great shame if that nugget were lost, simply melted down or sold on the international market or into private
hands.
This is something which I guess we will not see very often from now on because there must be a diminishing number of such beautiful pieces of nature left. It would be a great tragedy if this nugget were not deposited in an appropriate place. I hope that the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment (Mr Wilson) is listening to this speech and sees fit to give this matter very serious consideration because this matter concerns our national heritage in the same way that the beautiful South West Tasmania does. I think a most appropriate place for this nugget to be kept would be in the very fine and unique gold museum at Ballarat. If honourable members have never seen this museum I exhort them to come to Ballarat and have a look at it. It is situated over the road from Sovereign Hill in Ballarat which is a very fine tourist resort. I suggest that this museum has all of the appropriate security features. It has a very fine collection of gold.
Mr Barry Jones-Magnificently presented,
too.
Mr MILDREN-It is magnificently presented, as the honourable member for Lalor said. It is a world-class museum. I do not think anybody who has been there would suggest that I am exaggerating whatsoever. The largest nugget in that mu-
seum is one of 22 ounces. I guess that is nothing to sneeze at but the nugget about which I am speaking represents one of the more imaginative parts of our golden history. Over 1,100 nuggets larger than this one have been found in Victoria but this will be the largest nugget extant in Victoria and, in fact, in Australia. Surely it would be a great tragedy and a great indictment of our sense of national pride if the Parliament of this nation

(13) Is he able to state whether it is general practice for the US Congress and private bodies such as the US Air Force As-
sociation to be informed of operational details and developments with respect to the DSCS system before Members of the Australian Parliament and the Australian public.

Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) (a) and (b) The only DSCS terminals to have been in-
stalled at North West Cape are two AN/TSC-54 terminals. The first of these was installed in 1967 and was removed from

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 2255

the Station in 1974. The second terminal was installed in 1977 and commenced operating in 1978. This terminal is planned to be replaced by an ANjGSC-39V( I) terminal (formerly designated ANjMSC-61).
The only other satellite terminal installed at the Station is an ANjSSR-I receive-only terminal. This receives Heet broadcast information from the FLTSATCOM system.
(2) The installation of the ANjGSC 39V (I) terminal is scheduled to be completed in August 1982.
(2) Yes.
(4) The terminal itself has been installed, and testing of it is due to begin in June 1981.
(5) Yes, but this did not involve the installation of a third antennajradome at Nurrungar.
(6) Yes.
(7) (a) (b) and (c) The contract for construction associated with the installation of certain equipment at the Joint Defence Space Communications Station at Nurrungar, was awarded to Jennings Industries Ltd, an Australian company, in May 1979. Tenders were advertised by the Department of Housing and Construction. The equipment was not a defence satellite ground terminal.
(8) There are two DSCS terminals at the Joint Defence Space Research Facility. An SCT-35 terminal was installed in 1973 and an SCT-8 terminal in 1980.
(9) Yes.
(10) (a) (b) and (c) The DSCS I satellite referred to is designated satellite 93-26. The satellite occupies a subsynchronous, near equatorial orbit at an altitude of approximately 33,800 kilometres. Australia made use of the satellite for experimental, but not for operational, purposes between 1976 and 1978. The ground station at Salisbury, South Australia operated by the Advance Engineering Laboratory of the Defence Science and Technology Organisation was used to communicate with the satellite.
(II) No, although since April 1980 Australia has conducted experiments using a DSCS II satellite for research and development purposes. For the sake of completeness, I would add that the satellite terminal referred to in part (4) above wi II access the DSCS [[ system.
(12) Plans for Australian defence use of the DSCS II system provide for such use to be complementary to planned Australian use of the domestic communications satellite.
(13) As the DSCS system is a US system it is natural that operational details and developments with respect to the system should be made publicly available when the US so desires. I refer the honourable member to the Annual Reports of the Weapons Research Establishment for the years 1973-77 and the Advanced Engineering Laboratory for 1978-79 which contain information on the experiments conducted by the Advanced Engineering Laboratory's Satellite Communications Experimental Facility.

Overseas Travel: Department ofImmigration and Ethnic Affairs
(Question No. 1457)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(I) How many officers and employees of his Department travelled overseas on official business during 1979-80.
(2) What was the total cost of the travel, including fares, accommodation, allowances, etc.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) 18 officers travelled overseas on official business during 1979-80.
(2) The cost of this travel was $67,830.
It should be noted that these figures relate only to shortterm missions from Australia in accordance with the approved overseas visits program plus travel to Papua New Guinea (one officer) and New Zealand (three officers). The cost for travel of 2 officers in the latter category was mainly met by a grant from the Australia New Zealand Foundation. The numbers and cost do not include movements associated with overseas postings or movements between overseas posts by officers based overseas.
Alleged Fraud in Telecom, Brisbane Branch
(Question No. 2451)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 4 June 1981:
Has his attention been drawn to an article in the Brisbane Sunday Mail Septimus Column of 31 May 198 J concerning an alleged fraud in the Brisbane branch of Telecom and investigations which are expected to lead to criminal charges; if so, is there any substance in the allegations and are senior executives of Telecom involved.
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Following information received in February 1981, investi: gations were undertaken by Telecom into alleged discrepancies in the handling of case advances in a unit of Telecom in Brisbane.
As a result of those investigations, 17 officers were charged with offences under (he Telecommunications Act 1975 and By-taws under that Act. Twelve of the officers charged have resigned and five have lodged appeals.
The officers concerned occupied positions ranging from Clerical Assistant, Grade I, to Executive Officer (Class 7) within the Telcommunications Commission.

(6) (a) The accountability requirements in relation to Commonwealth Government recurrent grants ensure that the funds are used in the provision of recurrent resources. States Grants (Schools Assistance) Acts do not require nongovernment schools and systems to provide details of the subcategories of recurrent expenditure to which recurrent grants are applied. The information requested by the honourable

member in respect of Commonwealth payments for recurrent purposes is therefore not available.
(6) (b) Commonwealth grants for building and equipment projects in non-government schools are determined in each case on the basis of the need of the school concerned for financial assistance with a project assessment as having educational priority. Accordingly, a project is generally financed from a variety of sources such as Commonwealth contribution, school contribution, and school borrowings. Individual projects are commonly undertaken to provide a range of school facilities such as general classrooms, specialist learning areas, student and staff amenities and administration facilities and it is not possible to specify, from the final project cost, the expenditure on each element. However, certain data concerning the provision of facilities for new enrolments and existing enrolments, and the occurrences of particular types of facilities in projects supported by the Commonwealth, is given in the Report for the Triennium 19.82-84 (Chapter 12) by the Commonwealth Schools Commission, which I tabled in this House on 26 March 1981 and I direct the honourable member's attention to that Report.

Defence Network
(Question No. 2685)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Defence, upon notice, on 18 August 1981:
(I) Has his allention been drawn to a report in the Australian of 9 June 1981, that a 'smart' defence network is planned to provide automatic launching of early-warning missiles and counter-missile interception within 10 minutes of a Soviet missile launch against the United States of America.
(2) If so, does the system provide For distinguishing decoy missiles from real threats, and possible use of a global positioning satellite system.
(3) Can he say whether this development will increase the temptation to the United Sta,tes to use a preemptive strike if gravely threatened and increase the priority for counterallack by the Soviet Union on communications facilities in Australia.
Mr Killen-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes. The system to which the article appears to refer is the Survivable Optical Forward Acquisition System (SOFAS), which is at present being studied by the US Department of Defense. It is not a planned program at this stage.
(2) The ability qf SOFAS to distinguish decoys from real missiles is the subject of studies and experiments being conducted by the US Ballistic Missile Defense organization. The US does not anticipate that SOFAS will use the NAVSTAR Global Positioning System.
(3) The system would, if deployed, be a defensive early warning system and would support US strategic retaliatory rather than first strike capabilities.

internal review in 1977. More recently, these needs were the subject of an inter-departmental committee review co-
ordinated by the Department of Foreign Affairs in 1980 to which this Department contributed. A survey is currently
underway of the language capabilities of departmental officers.

More generally, the Department ensures that vacancies
advertised in the Commonwealth Gazette and internally indicate that a second language is desirable whenever it is appropriate to do so.

Proficiency in a foreign language is a factor taken into account in the selection of officers for overseas service. Officers who lack appropriate language skills are given
language training prior to their departure in accordance with language priorities and the available funds.

An opportunity to continue language training is available on arrival at many overseas posts.

or language skills are available under normal Public Service ar-

Electoral Division of La Trobe: Post Offices
(Question No. 2745)
Mr Milton asked the Ministe,r for,Communications, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:
(I) How many post offices are provided for the use of the residents of the Electoral Division of La Trobe,
(2) How many of these are unofficial post offices,
(3) Will he give an undertaking that postal services in this Electoral Division will not be further reduced by the reduction of any existing post offices to unofficial status,
Mr SincIair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) 27
(2) 22
(3) Australia Post is obliged under the Postal Services Act to continually review its network of post offices (official and non-official) to ensure that the facilities and services provided best meet the reasonable needs of the community, having regard to economy and efficiency, Under this program, areas may be identified where new post offices are necessary while other post offices may be identi fied for closure, where the level of business transacted is not sufficient to justify retention, It is Australia Post's objective, in the latter circumstances, to ensure that reasonable alternative arrangements are made for communities affected by the withdrawal of a post office.

Social Welfare
(Question No. 2769)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:
(I) What is the position of social security beneficiaries and pensioners who make a gift of income producing savings or other sources of income to their sons, daughters and other persons, thereby depriving themselves of extra income for the purpose of obtaining fringe benefits.
(2) What is the position of a social security pensioner or beneficiary who (a) receives a gift of $100 in a raffle or (b) wins money at the races, or in some other game of chance, and receives winnings, on a relatively regular basis,
(3) What is the minimum sum of extra income which must be reported to the Department of Social Security,
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

Answers to Questions

20 October 1981 REPRESENTATlVES 2259

(I) Under the existing provisions of the Social Services Act, where a pensioner directly or indirectly deprives himself of income in order to qualify For a pension or a higher rate of pension, the Director-General may deem the amount forgone to be income for pension purposes. As announced in the Budget Speech, the Government intends to strengthen these provisions and to expand their scope.
Under the proposed provisions, iF a pensioner were to reduce his income in order to qualiFy for a pension, a higher rate ,of pension, fringe benefits or assistance under the new health care arrangements through classification as a person with low income, an appropriate amount of income may be deemed to be received and taken into account for income test purposes.
(2) In general, money received in the circumstances described would not be treated as income for pension purposes; however, where the person was a professional punter or gambier, For examplea bookmaker, his winnings would be taken into account.
(3) In the case of unemployment and sickness benefit a beneficiary is required to notify the Department immediately upon receipt of income at a weekly rate higher than the weekly rate last notified.
A person in receipt of supplementary assistance is required to notify the Department within 14 days if, in any period of two consecutive weeks, his average weeky rate of income is higher than the weekly rate last notified by him.
For pension and supporting parent's benefit purposes, a person is required to notify the Department within 14 days if, in any 8 consecutive weeks, his average weekly rate of income (including that of his spouse), exceeds $20 a week ($34.50 a week if married) and is higher than the rate last specified by him.
